THE RED PAGE.
“Campaign Pictures.”
~|\"T"OW A. G-. HALES (they call him
Smiler ”) is at least a man. Sometimes
his wits may fail; sometimes his words
may whoop and chortle in the carmine
fields of “skite”; but all his failings little mar
his worth. Like some resplendent parrot of
the bush he spreads his soul in flight; yet eke
withal he has a human heart to feel a tale of
wrath or wrong, and in his lively eye clear pictures
come. Small wonder he amazed - the simple Eng-
lish folk. And as his correspondence-trucks come
rushing to the tip, much mullock is poured out,
and here and there good ore. And so the
“ Smiler ” mine will pay to work.
Coming down from Hales’s iambic high-ropes,
it is pleasant on our lower plane to praise
A. Gr. Hales’s collected African war-correspond-
ence from Lond. Daily News —“ Campaign Pic-
tures ” (Cassell) —as, with all its faults, the best
picture of the war from an Australian pen or other,
so far as Hales saw it. Hales gives good
matter in what is often a good style; he
does not waste himself on trifles, but paints with
a broad brush, and te 11s the things one wants to
know; and, above all, he has the pluck to
tell the truth—he does not go in hobbles
lest he wound Jingo prejudices, as prac-
tically all the other correspondents have
gone. The pity is that the Daily News editor
did not have the sense to delete the “ hifalutin ”
nonsense which Australian pressmen are used
to recognise as the Hales trademark. That
gone, his work would be really very good.
It has not the weight of Forbes or the careful
literary quality of Steevens ; but it is very human
very graphic, doing honour to Hales’s heart as well
as to his head. And, though even Hales has not
been able to say all he thought or knew, he has
said much that no other was game to say—much
that badly wanted saying.
Hales was wounded and taken prisoner by the
Boers, and woke up, after several hours uncon-
sciousness, to find himself well taken care of. He
was carried along to a hospital laager.
tor a day and a-half I lay at that laager whilst our
wounded men were brought in, and here I should like to
say a word to the people of England. Our men when
wounded, are treated by the Boers with manlv gentleness
and kind consideration, \yhen we left the laager in an
open trolly, we, some half-dozen Australians, and about
as many Boers, all wounded, were driven for some hours
to a small hospital, the name of which Ido not know It
was simply a farm-house turned into a place for the
wounded. On the road thither we called at many farms
and at every one men, women, and children came out to
see us. Notone taunting word was uttered in our hear-
ing, not one braggart sentence passed their lips. Men
brought us cooling drinks, or moved us into more com-
fortable positions on the trolly. Women, with "-enile
fingers, shifted bandages, or washed wounds, or gave us
little dainties that come so pleasant in such a time • whilst
the little children crowded round us with tears running
down their cheeks as they looked upon the blood-stained
kharki clothing of the wounded British. Let no man or
woman in all the British Empire whose son or husband
lies wounded in the hands of the Boers fear for his wel-
fare, for it is a foul slander to say that the Boers do not
treat their wounded well. England does not treat her
own men better than the Boers treat the wounded
Bhtish, and I am writing of that which I have seen and
know beyond the shadow of a doubt.
That testimony would dispose of Jingo lies
about the Boers’ lack of civilisation, their in-
humanity, if these had not been disposed of by a
thousand similar testimonies As to the
kind of- life the Boers lead, Hales is no less em-
phatic.
I have been in a few back-country homes in America, in
Australia, and in other parts of the world—and I want to
place it on record that in my opinion the Boer farmer is
as clean m his home life, as loving in his domestic arrange-
ments, us pure in his morals, as any class of people I have
met. Filth-may abound, -but I have seen nothing of
it. Immorality may be the common everyday occurrence
I have seen it depicted in some British journals,
but I have failed to find trace of it. Ignorance
as black as the inside of a dog* may be the
prevailing state of affairs ; if so, I have been
one of the lucky few who have found just the re-
verse in whichsoever direction I have turned After six
months’ or nearly six months’, close and careful observa-
tron of their habits, I have arrived at the conclusion that
the Boer farmer, and his son and daughter, will compare
very favorably with the farming folk of Australia,
America and Great Britain. What he may be in the.
Transvaal I know not, because I have not yet been there •
but in Cape Colony and in the Free State he is much as i
have depicted him, no better, no worse, than Americans
Australians, and as good a fighting man as either
which is tantamount to saying that he is as good as any-
thing on God’s green earth, if he only had military
training. J
As a prisoner Hales had the best opportunity of
getting to understand the real character of the
Boers, the objects they have in view and the way
m which they resolved to reach them; and he
bears out all previous witnesses to the innate
manliness of the Boers, their high standard of
morality, their intense love of freedom.
I often asked them how they, representing a couple of
small States, came to get hold of the idea that they could
whip a colossal power like Great Britain in a life or death
struggle; and almost invariably they informed me that
they had expected that one of the great European Powers
would take an active part in the struggle on their behalf
and furthermore, they had been taught to think that
Britain s Empire was rotten to the core, so much so that
as soon as war commenced in earnest all her colonies
would fall away from her and hoist the flag- of independ-
ence, and that India would leap once again into open and
bloody mutiny. They expressed themselves as being
dumbfounded when they heard that Australian troops
were rallying under the Union Jack, and seemed to feel
most bitterly that the men from the land of the Southern
Cross were in arms against them. “We fell out with
England, and we thought we had to fight England In
stead-we find we. have to fight people from all parts of the
world, Colonials like ourselves. Surely Australia and
Canada might have kept out of this fight, and allowed us
to battle it out with the country we had a quarrel
with.”. ... H
“ Look here,” said one old fighting man to me, as he
-leant with negligent grace on his rifle, “I was one of
those who helped to corner Jameson and his men and I
can tell you that we Boers knew very well that we would
have been acting within our rights if we had shot Jame-
son and every ofian he had with him, because his was not
an act of war it was an act of piracy ; and had we done
so, and England had, attempted to avenge the deed- half
the civilised world would have ranged themselves on out-
side ; but we did not seek those men’s blood • we gave
them quarter as soon as they asked for it, and after that
though we knew very well they had done all that men
could do to involve us m a war of extermination with a
gre.it natibn, we sent their leader home to his own
country to be tried by his own countrymen, and the rank
and file we forgave freely. We may be a nation of white
savages, but our past does not prove it, and if Britain
wins in-the war now going on she will have to be verv
generous indeed before we will need to blush for our
conduct.”
“ Why should not the white population of South Africa
be ready to live under the protection of Britain? The
yoke cannot be so very heavy when men of all creeds,
colours, and nationalities who haye lived under that rule
for years are now ready to volunteer to U>'ht for-her
even against you, who have admittedly done them no
ci.r -ct wrong ?”
“ MO’ should we live under any flag but our own?”

all. So his pictures grip the mind, even if the

Nor is Hales without humour, though, like his

Another intimate picture—thistimeof the camp,
liar who has been the source of so many Jingo
libels of the Boers, often reprinted in Australia
and accepted by gullible people as truth.
What is a camp liar? It is not a man, neither is it a
maid, neither is it dog nor devil. It is a nameless
shadow, which flits through the minds of men, fashioned
by the Father of Evil to be a curse and a scourge to war
correspondents. A mining liar is an awful liar, but he
takes tangible form, and one can grapple with him when
he appears upon a prospectus. A political liar is a pitiful
liar, and vengeance finds him out upon the hustings! and
e !lgs and the- produce of the kitchen garden are his re-
ward. A legal liar is a loquacious liar, but he is bounded
by his brief and the extent of his fees. But the camp liar
has no bounds, and is equally at home in all languages, at
moment dealing with an army in full marching order
h^ii th VK eXt - batte L nnB 'ui e i upon one man in a mud!
hole. There is no height to which the camp liar dare not
ascend, there is nothing too trivial for it to touch. It has
neither sex nor shape ; but, like a fallen angel froS
Heaven, and not wanted in Hades, it flits through camp a
jwldfers miCrobe ’ s P awnin ff falsehoods in the souls of

These samples may exhibit Hales’s' “ Campaign
Austerile?
ON January Ist we, the dwellers in Aus
tralia, felt for the first time (as the pres
had it) “ the pulse of nationhood.” Le
, us blow off tiie political froth and comet
the bitter or sweet of our draught.
Are we imagining a vain nation out o
mere Eucalyptus and Sentiment? Is an Aus
tralian a Manglo-Saxon born or is he an
Australian ? Has our country the hear
to feed the pulse that every' man of u
should feel with the instinct of the child tha
blindly and strongly craves for the fulness of the
mother s breast ? Are we sojourners in a barren
land, thin-flanked, small-breasted, immature o
sex-listless with the greyness of age? Have w
pinned the rose of our desire upon an undevelopec
virgin or a sapless old maid ?
It is mainly a question of physiography : man
must stand or fall by Nature’s grim intentions o
mistakes. Australia has no geographical heart
Where mountain ranges should mark our centre
there is a grim blankness of desert. No great
nrers-th e arteries of the soil—spring from that
endless desolation to vitalise our land. Fringec
round our coast are rivers and mountains, cities
and people ; but the big heart, the fountain-heac
ot vigour—where is it ?
The race we pickpocketed the land from ? They
are almost the lowest of humans! When man is
born of a soil he does not stand still, but develops
or degenerates according to that soil. If our
aborigines fell behind other races it was the fault
of the soil they sprang from. Where Nature
plainly showed that alone and undisturbed—with-
out the terror of hunger-toothed animals-she
could not breed a race, can we, with the assistance
oi an artificial civilisation, succeed ?
Instead of strong, hot-blooded animals—lions,
tigers, leopards—our land breeds some of the
oldest and lowest orders of mammals. Weird
kangaroos, cold-blooded monotremes, and large
unwieldy birds ! Why have they not fallen, into
line with natural development elsewhere? Was
Australia incapable of developing along the lines
of evolution ?
We have had some hundred years for the glim-
merings of a national literature—the reflex of our
life. We have Gordon and Marcus Clarke, Boake
and Lawson. What is their “ note ” ? Pessimism
pessimism, and again pessimism. The tragedy of
desolation, the half-understood, the vividly-ex-
pressed horror of a vast, vague, and bloodless land
A young country, a young nation, should have a
lde. Not necessarily sentimental or child-
ish, but strong, clearly conscious of present diffi-
culties, with a ring of hope for the future. Where
is the Australian writer whose work is blooded
with hope ?
The most universal, the loudest-voiced emotion
we have yet experienced has been second-hand
Imperialism. Call it drunken Jingoism, if you
will, this almost unanimous voicing of British
sentiment. But oust country could not grip us We
were a people virgin of nationality, and we echoed
the war-cry of our fathers. The loneliness of our
scanty civilisation, embroidering vast, haggard
emptiness drove us back to the vitality of the
little island of our ancestors. If Australia had
breathing individuality would we have joined in
the savage hounding-down of the Boer ?
What are the pictures that paint themselves
most readily on the national canvas?
A grim immensity of desert. A man—a gold
adventurer—blindly staggering towards dead
desolation. A gloomy bird hovering over him
and a pitiless? sun scorching his brain and clotting
Ins blood. ... 6
A selection outside the fringe of small rivers
and dertain rain. ... A man brown and gaunt
and tired beyond human weariness. ... A few
barefooted, wistful children, thin with a diet of
treacle and damper. . . . A hopeless, acid-voiced
woman. ... A pencil-Ihin stream of water
trickling along a brown depression, in the land-
scape call it a creek. Despair is written on
broken fences and drooping cattle, and stamped
deep on the thirsty, gaping ground. .
Drought. ...
A bush-hut and and a hollow-cheeked woman
waiting for the drover husband. Bagged children,
ill-fed, ill-clad for the man sends only the
dribbles of money that escape the shanty-keeper.
Days and nights of dull, maddening waiting—
waiting—until the foolish husband returns to
drink or dies on the edge of a sunburnt plain.
A waste, desolate land—outposted by sombre
gum trees. . . . The ruins of a mine’s poppet-
head. . . . Mullock heaps like the graves of
mighty giants: . . . Ragged gaps in the ground
as if great teeth had been wrenched out of it by a
whirlpool. ... A sluggish creek creeping by a
few brown huts. . . . Chinese, like yellow ants
pecking among the mullock heaps. ...
You speak of your fine cities; but a doctor does
not say a man’s heart is sound because his eyes
are bright.
Again : A decreasing birth-rate, increasing ille-
gitimacy, degenerating physical stamina, the
breathless desire to crowd into cities, heavy
national debts, and tile' pitiful need for old-age
pensions.
Do all these point to a vigorous nation ?
Australia ! Canst thou be a full-breasted mother
to thy children ?

In an excellent article in Jan. Review of Rcvinvs
the Bev. Joseph Berry discusses the tendencies of
the white race in Maorilancl :
The faetors which will- tell upon the coming- New
Zealander are such as these : A healthy climate, with the
lowest death-rate in the world. A population mainly
agricultural. Two-thirds of the people now I’ive in the
country, or in towns of less than 5000 inhabitants. The
whole population lives and will continue to- live within
sight or sound of the sea. There is not an inhabitant
the colony more than a day’s journey (seventy miles) from
the sea, and nearly all are within an hour-or two. Th
land is so rich and productive and food is so plentiful an
cheap, that poverty will be at a minimum.
Again, the land is so subdivided that there is not muc
chance for the millionaire. New Zealand has no million
aires and not more than ten or a dozen .of its'citizens ar
worth more than £lO,OOO per year. Timber is s
abundant and cheap that a house does not cost more tha
hah as much as a similar house in Australia, for most o

Various Verses.
The Brown Bird.
[F OR THE BULLETIN.]
And this was the end of the honey-tree
That they came with clatter and din,
But had you heard when it shivered and stirred,
The flutter and grief of a little brown bird,
You’d have grieved somewhat for the greenwoor
sin,
Grown sad at the greenwood sin.
O, the little brown bird had cause to be
Sad at heart when they felled his tree,
For his house was built high up in its boughs
High up where the creepers climb,
And there he brought his little brown wife
Each year at the mating time.
And how will she act in the spring, do you think,
When he calls with a burning breast,
And she answers him in the woodland dim
With a shy small voice from a leaf-hid limb ?
Do you think she’ll live with her homeless love,
Or seek a new love’s nest ?
Now, little 1 know of the world above
The bird-world and the brown bird’s love ;
But this I’ll say —if there came a fairy
And made you the brown bird’s wife,
And made me into the little brown bird,
I’d follow you all my life.
I’d say to you, “Love, O Love, O Love ! ”
(And you’d plume your wings as you heard)
“ There are peace and rest on your brown mate s
breast.
And where is the need for a shelter, a nest,
l Is love not all these things and more ? ■
The love of your little brown bird.”
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.
Vae Victis!
So you thought you had as a prisoner, fast,
A man who has lived and loved, and thereafter
Has laughed at Life with Irony’s laughter,
Because of the stinging Worm that at last
Feeds red at the heart of all fair things ;
The heart of Emperors, purple, Roman,
The heart of lions, the heart of kings,
The heart of a rose, and the heart of a Woman.
White as the Flower of the Holy Ghost;
And pretty, doubtless, after a fashion,
You needs must taste of a strong man’s passion ;
For a whim perchance —perchance for a boast.
But that wine is strong for a maiden to sip,
And I doubt not, Dainty, that you will remember
The sting of that night on your scarlet lip
Till your little red heart is a perishing ember.
But the sport, if perilous, sure, is grand ;
For, the ages down and the wide world over,
To turn the strong man into the Lover
Is the game most dear to a girl’s white hand.
It is finished now, and another one
May come to you in the darkness, wooing;
But (acting over, and fiction done)
Your victory, dearest, is my undoing.
SYDNEY, FEBRUARY, 1901.
ACE.
A Few Tired Lines.
1 am dead, dead tired of living,
I am tired of everything.
Tired of taking, tired of giving,
Tired of winter, tired of spring.
I am unrelenting tired ;
I am tired, tired, tired.
But the thing I tire of hardest
Is the tire of being tired.
I am tired, oh ! very tired ;
Tired to death of all of me.
Tired of grinning, tired of spinning,
Tired of roving far and free,
And of long or laughing faces,
I am tired, tired, tired.
All the frowns and all the graces
Make me infinitely tired.
I am wearied out of hearing,
That you love me—you, most dear—
Tired of talking, tired of wearing
Love-looks in my eyes, my dear.
I am pitilessly tired—
* I am tired, tired, tired ;
But the thing I tire of longest
Is the joy of being tired.
Yet, though tired of being tired
In this paralysing way—
Tired of living, tired of giving,
Tired of work and tired of play—
I can see I’d be more tired
If I never felt like this,
Never loathed my bread and honey,
Never bickered with my bliss.
You, the monsters, you, the tireless,
Will you tell me truly this :
Is there not in all your fireless
Lives a minute when you miss
Something from your everlasting,
Long, unending, twin-shaped days,
And the thing you miss—l knew it—
Is it not one tired phase ?
We are all dead tired of living,
If we’d only own the thing.
Tired of taking, tired of giving,
Tired of autumn, summer, spring.
But there’s one excuse to proffer
For this fearful state of bore,
Everything the gods can offer
Once made someone tired before.
N.S.W.
LOUISE MACK.

publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
“ Correspondence" column all contributions submitted,
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in length,
or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters, or Newspaper clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that we
are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

SATURDAY, FEBRUARY 16, 1901.
“The Bulletin’s” Fiscal Recantation.
This paper doesn’t change its opinions very
often, and when it starts to recant on a
large scale the event is more or less memor-
able. For many years—as many as it can
remember, in fact—it has been consistently
Protectionist; but even now it is open to
conviction. It has heard very much about
the good example set by Britain, about the
progress of Britain, about Britain’s growing
wealth and commerce and manufactures, and
about the urgent necessity for this country
to adopt the system under which such
allegedly splendid results have been
achieved ; and the constant dropping of re-
marks wears away a stone. The title at the
head of this article was not put there
for nothing. After scouting for years the
idea of following the example of Britain
in fiscal matters The Bulletin has decided
on a change of front. It unreservedly with-
draws from its long-held belief that
Australia should not follow Britain’s
example in fiscal matters, and absolutely
reverses its policy in that respect. No man
is too old to learn, nor is any paper. Some
of them are too stupid to learn, but that is
another story ; and some are too inflated to
acknowledge that they have been misled,
but that is also a different narrative. The
Bulletin frankly admits, on further study
of the matter, that Britain’s example in
matters financial is better than it thought.
In all seriousness it says: Let us follow
Britain’s example.
• * * *
Britain built up its industries by some 200
years of Protection, before it exposed them
to the competition of the world at large, and
The Bulletin proposes that after 200 years
of Protection Australia should do likewise.
The present writer will be dead then, and
he hopes that his descendants of that period
will have accumulated enough money to pay
their passages to some other land. Britain
didn’t expose its industries to the competi-
tion of all the world until, after 200 years of
Protection, it had gained such a start over
all possible rivals that it was the greatest
manufacturer on earth—so great that the
output of manufactured goods from its fly-
speck group of islands constituted about
one-tliird of the whole output of the world.
When Australia gets into Britain’s circum-
stances The Bulletin proposes, out of pure
devilment, that it should do as Britain did.
Instead of turning out one-third of the
world’s manufactures as it did in its Protec-
tionist days, Britain now turns out less than
a sixth, and it is a very bad second
as a manufacturing State instead of
a good first, but, all the same, this
paper will take the chance and vote for
following British precedent. When Britain,
by centuries of Protection, had built itself up
till it was the greatest banking nation ex-
tant, holding nearly half the banking capital
and deposits of the world (it has now
dropped to second place) it tried Freetrade ;
and when Australia gets there also this paper
doesn’t mind if Australia does as Britain
did. When Britain h*l grown under its
Protective system to have the biggest mer-
chant navy in the world, the greatest com-
merce in the world (in the matter of total
commerce, internal and external, it is now a
bad second to the United States, not only in
the bulk, but in proportion to population
also) ; when it had become the third greatest
agricultural nation in the world (it is now
about the sixth), when it owned almost half
the steam-power of the world (it now owns
less than a fifth), and it had lent money to
nearly all the globe, and had, under its Pro-
tective system, paid off much of its public
debt, then it decided that it could face the
world’s competition, and it tried the experi-
ment. When Australia arrives at the same
circumstances this paper votes that it does
the same thing. The Bulletin doesn’t say
as the Reid and M'Millan and B. Smith
crowd does, “Let us follow Britain’s fiscal
example with reservations.” It cheerfully
goes the entire beast in the matter.
* * * *
This paper detests half-heartedness and
weak compromise. When it recants a fiscal
error, or any other error, it does it with all
its soul. Having decided, in this matter, to
go into the camp of George Reid (or, rather,
into the empty camp where George Reid
and B. Smith and M‘Millan and co. say
they are, but where they aren’t), and to vote
for following the good example of Britain in
fiscal matters, it wants to do the thing—not
to less than half do it. The Reid party holds
that because Britain took to Freetrade when
it had grown (under Protection) to be the
greatest manufacturing, commercial, bank-
ing, money-lending, and ship-owning nation
on earth, and the third greatest agricultural
nation, Australia should take to Freetrade
when it is deeply burdened with foreign
debt and is away far back in the ruck of
nations in almost every respect. That isn’t
following Britain’s fiscal example, and The
Bulletin, after being told so often by the
Reid party that we should follow it, wants
to do the thing—not wobble about it and
face both ways.
* * ♦ *
Yet in its anxiety to recant completely
when it is about the work, The Bulletin
will go further. It is prepared, under cer-
tain circumstances, to drop all mention of
the fact that Australia, if it adopts Freetrade
now, is not following Britain’s precedent.
It is prepared to take a lenient view of the
huge discrepancy. Britain, having followed
Protection till it became the boss country of
the earth, took to Freetrade, believing that
it had got so incalculably ahead that it could
hold its own against all comers. And for
Australia, because of this, to take to Free-
trade when it isn’t yet ahead in anything,
and when it know; perfectly well it can’t hold
its own against ah ;omers, is just the same as
for the man at Bourke to have a huge fire
in his drawing-room and thick underclothing
on his body at Christmas, because the man
in London has them. But this paper is
willing to give the Reid party the benefit of
the doubt here—large as tliQ doubt certainly
is. If George Reid and his party will
pledge themselves by some oath that will be
binding on their souls, if there is any such
oath, that when they get into power they
will follow Britain’s fiscal example as it is
now, The Bulletin, though it may not
actively support them, will make no serious
effort to keep them out of power. If they
get into power it will be glad to see them
there, and will feel that the most interesting
experiment in Australia’s history is just
about to commence. And if these people,
who have thrown Britain’s example at us so
persistently as a thing to follow and admire,
don’t accept this offer they are a very poor
and squalid set of shirks, and not worth any
serious person’s consideration.
* * * 9
Taking New South Wales as a case in
point, when that State begins to adopt
Britain’s fiscal system as G. Reid says it
should, in order to attain to what G. Reid
says is Britain’s present pinnacle of pros-
perity, it must cease to float foreign loans.
Britain, except in instances too rare to be
worth mentioning, doesn’t float foreign loans.
It must give up using about £BOO,OOO a year
of borrowed money for unproductive works
properly chargeable to revenue, for Britain
never does that ; and it must pay for these
works out of extra taxation. That is what
Britain does, and it is as much a part of
Britain's financial policy as Freetrade
itself. And in addition to this re-
striction- on the creation of new
debt, N.S.W. must pay off part of its old
debt every year out of revenue, and if the
revenue isn’t large enough for the purpose it
must be made large enough. To liquidate
its public debt at the same rate as Britain
does, allowing for the difference in popula-
tion, N.S.W. should reduce its debt, by special
taxation, at least £200,000 a year—this in
addition to finding another £BOO,OOO a year
that borrowing for unproductive works may
cease. All these things are essential features
of Britain’s financial policy —quite as essen-
tial as Freetrade. If Britain is great, they
have quite as much to do with its greatness
as Freetrade, and without them Freetrade
would have done no good—assuming that it
has done any good. Turkey also follows the
Freetrade system, but for want of the other
notable points of Britain’s financial system,
it isn’t great. If Australia is to follow
Britain’s fiscal example it wants to follow
the whole of it and be great like Britain—
not take the Freetrade item only and slump
like Turkey.
* * • *
And when Britain went in for Freetrade
(or rather pure Revenue-Tariffism) it did the
whole thing. If we are to adopt Britain’s
allegedly beneficial fiscal policy let us also
do the whole thing. There is no such
thing ( in Britain as free newspaper
postage to protect the local Freetrade paper.
There is no Protection to brewers, wine-
makers, sugar and tobacco growers, sugar-
refiners, tobacco-manufacturers, confection-
ers, and other industries, as in N.S.W. The
British Freetrade Government doesn’t sacri-
fice much-needed revenue by keeping the
excise on the local product, as the Reid
Government did, far below the customs duty
on the imported article, so that the local
manufacturer may be protected to the extent
of the difference. Even the import duty on
playing-cards in Britain is counterbalanced
by a stamp duty on cards of local manufac-
ture. When the British Government wants
moneyitdoesn’t do like theREiD party and go
whining over the surface of the country to the
effect that it believes in Freetrade and
direct taxation, but that it isn’t going
to impose direct taxation, and therefore
it must, for the sake of revenue, put on
many duties which will be incidentally pro-
tective. Every British customs duty on im-
ported goods is accompanied by a similar tax
on locally made goods, and if the Reid
crowd really believes in following Britain’s
noble and invigorating precedent, and really
believes that Britain’s example is good, it
will do likewise. It will get more revenue
that way than by putting on casual protec-
tive duties and saying that it is driven to do
so by want of money; and in addition to
getting more revenue it will preserve its
alleged beautiful principles intact.
* * * *
Britain, when it wants more money,
seldom resorts to the customs at all.
It says, as the Reid party does, that it
believes in direct taxation, but it really
believes, which the Reid party doesn’t. So
the British income-tax has been as high, in
times of emergency, as 2s. in the £ on all in-
comes over £l5O a year, with a smaller rate
on all incomes between £5O and £l5O. Even
within the present writer’s time it has been
ll|d. in the £ on all incomes between £lOO
and £l5O, and Is. 4d. in the £ on all in-
comes above £l5O. There are also very heavy
death duties, and a myriad of small direct
taxes unknown in Australia—even the hired
male rouseabout who cleans the boots has a
tax levied on him. Further, very many
items which are here paid out of State
revenue are in England charged to local
revenue, with the result that local taxation
is also very heavy—fully five times as heavy
as in N.S.W. By these means Britain has
proved that it is possible for a country with
heavy responsibilities to raise all the revenue
it requires by a short and simple tariff, and
Reid, M‘Millan, and B. Smith could do it
also if they had the faith and the courage,
and hadn’t degenerated so abjectly. Reid
in particular says that Britain is the greatest
country on earth ; that it is its fiscal system
which made it great ; and that he wants to
follow its fiscal system, only the needs of the
Commonwealth won’t permit. The latter
statement is grossly and shamefully in-
accurate. If the British fiscal system (which
includes 2s. in the £ jncome-tax in times
of stress, and local rates extending up to 6s.
Bd. in the £, with isolated cases of 9s. in
the £, of rental, in order to cover many items
that are here Government responsibilities)
were applied in Australia by a Reid Go-
vernment, it would supply enough revenue
to keep the most needy of the States afloat
as long as Reid lived. How long Reid
would live, unless he concealed himself in a
bomb-proof cellar, is a matter on which this
paper doesn’t feel called upon to offer an
opinion. There are no revenue necessities
in the road if George Reid, and M'Millan,
and Pumpkin Smith, and Bruce Smith, and
the other large and small Smiths, and all
the gassy crowd which says that it is Britain’s
present fiscal system which has made Britain
great, really want to advocate the adoption
of that system by the Commonwealth. The
only conceivable difficulty appears to be the
crowd’s own cowardice, or its disbelief in
its own theories.
* * * *
The Bulletin, with all the fervour of a
new convert in its soul, wants to see the
Australian Freetrade party, which alleges
that the adoption of Britain’s fiscal policy
will make this country fat and wildly
affluent, really advocating the adoption of
that policy. The Freetrade party’s conduct
in not supporting and not practising the
British methods which are to make Australia
glorious Taises a suspicion that it is hired by
some foreign power, probably Greenland, to
undermine this country’s interests. When
Britain postponed trying Freetrade till it was
the richest and greatest country of the
world, the Reids and Smiths say we should
try it while Australia is still in its infancy,
loaded with debt, and with no commanding
lead in any industry, and when this dis-
crepancy is pointed out they say that the
difference of circumstances doesn’t matter—
the truths of Freetrade apply equally, what-
ever the circumstances. Where Britain
practises absolute Freetrade, floats no
foreign loans, pays for all unproductive
State works out of revenue, reduces its debt
every year, and levies about £2 6s. 6d. per
head in direct taxation, local and national,
to enable it to do this, the Reids and Smiths
propose to still levy many semi-Protective
duties, to pay off none of the public debt, to
keep on borrowing for unproductive works,
and to keep direct taxation at about its pre-
sent level (in N.S.W. it doesn’t reach £1
per head, everything included) the shortage
being made good with borrowed money.
And being asked why they don’t follow
Britain’s glorious example, the general reply
is that the circumstances are different.
This sort of thing grows wearisome. And
on a sudden afterthought this paper
wholly withdraws the fiscal recantation with
which it commenced the present article. It
realises that it has always been a much better
advocate of the policy of following Britain’s
good example than George Reid has been,
and that no recantation is necessary.
Britain’s fiscal policy (the one that the Reids
and M‘Billions and the large and small
Smiths profess to hold in such reverence)
consists mainly of absoluteßevenue-Tariffism
which neither the N.S.W. Freetrade party
nor The Bulletin has followed, the latter
advocating straight, honest Protection, while
the former practised modified Protection and
lied about it. Also, Britain’s fiscal policy
embodies the principles of no foreign bor-
rowing, no borrowing for unproductive
works, and the levying of sufficiently
heavy taxation to pay off every year
a portion of the public debt. The Bulletin
has advocated these three great principles
for a dozen years, while the cheeky political
bounders who call themselves the leaders
of Australian Freetrade have trodden them
all under foot. The short and simple creed
of the N.S.W. Freetrader is: “Britain’s
fiscal policy is the secret of its greatness.
Let us follow about one-tenth of that policy
—just enough to let us get our cheap calicoes
in free —and darn the rest. The rest of the
British policy is expensive. We have a
great reverence for the wisdom of that part
of the British policy which brings money to
the pockets of M‘Millan and the rest of the
importing gang. The part that would take
money out of the pockets of M‘Millan and
the rest of the importing gang inspires no
respect at all.”
PLAIN ENGLISH.
More Raising of the Poor and Lowly.
It being one of the favourite cries of the N.S.W.
Freetraders that their importing policy “raises
up the poor and lowly ” and causes a wide diffu-
sion of wealth, and prevents the accumulation of
great piles of lucre in the hands of the few, The
Bulletin is moved to quote these figures from
that good English Freetrade statistician, Mul-
HALL, as to how wealth is distributed in one Free-
trade country (Britain) and one Protectionist ditto
(France) —the only two great States about which
this kind of information appears to be avail-
able. The estimates are up to 1896, and are
to be found on pages 100 and 132 of the “Indus-
tries and Wealth of Nations.” The British table
deals with adults, and the French one with
families, which makes comparison rather difficult;
still the results are interesting as showing how
the raising up of the poor and lowly gets on :
Great Britain and Ireland.
Adults. Total Wealth.
Very Rich People.. 327,000 £9,120,000,000 £28,000
Middle Class .. 2,380,000 2,120,000,000 900
Working Class .. 18,210,000 566,000,000 31
Children .. .. 17,940,000 Nil
38,857,000
Prance.
Wealth
Families. Total Wealth. per
family.
Rich .. .. 355,000 £3,440,000,000 £9700
Middle Class .. 1,260,000 1,920,000,000 1600
Working Class .. 7,301,000 4,330,000,000 594
Assuming three adults to a family, which is a very
liberal estimate, it appears from this good English
authority that the average adult working-man and
woman in Protectionist France is worth £l9B
against £3l in Freetrade Britain. Also, the work-
ing classes in the Protectionist land own among
them 4330 millions of pounds worth of property
against 566 millions, or one-ei<jhth, owned by the
equally large working class in Freetrade England.
It is unfortunate that Mulhall doesn’t give
similar information about other countries, but
even these two are interesting. If it is true, as
George Reid solemnly asserted when his famous
Savings Bank fiction was in full bloom, that it is
tariffs which raise or depress the poor and lowly,
then the present scribe (in case he has to hustle
with a pick and shovel some day) would much
rather have the Protectionist tariff which raised
the poor to £l9B than the Freetrade one which
only raised them to £3l. Apparently the French
working man’s family of self, wife and three
children has on an average £594 among the crowd,
while the British toiler’s family of the same size
has £62 (£3l for the old man, £3l for the wife, and
nothing for the children). And the difference is
made up by the rich in France having nearly 6,000
millions less than the rich in Great Britain.
This meeting concludes with cheers for the policy
which hoists up the poor and lowly.
+
Federating' the Railways.
The proposal of N.S.W. Railway Commissioner
Fehon that the Commonwealth should take over


the State railways at a value based on the average
profits of the last seven years, is bearing fruit in
letters like this to Sydney Telegraph. The
writer is a heavy and rather frequent corre-
spondent, who signs himself John Plumb :
The public are under a distinct obligation to Mr. Feiion
for the plain, lucid statement he has sjfc before them, and
the note of warning he sounds Should set them thinking
of the serious consequences that may arise from preci-
pitately rushing into any arrangement for federating
without due consideration and investigation of the pay-
ing or non-paying condition of the railways of the several
States of Australia. To take them all in a lump at
book value of cost would, as Mr. Feiion points out, be
disastrous to those States possessing paying lines, because
the already paying lines would have to increase their
charges to make good the loss on the non-paying lines.
New South Wales, possessing the railway system which
now returns a surplus over and above working expenses
and interest, has most to lose, and any addition to the
rates charged to the users of her lines for freight or pas-
senger purposes for the mere purpose of making good
shortages for non-paying lines in other states would be a
distinct hardship on her citizens.
The Victorian Railway Commissioner (Mr. Mathieson)
strongly opposes Mr. Fehon’s proposition—naturally he
would, for, as Mr. Feiion points out, Victoria would have
to wipe £13,000,000 off the book values of her railways to
bring her in line with the paying systems of New South
Wales, and the other States in like manner in varying
sums, as shown in Mr. Fehon’s statement, would have to
reduce or wipe off values to come into line with the
parent State.
The John Plumb kind of man can’t see
anything beyond N.S.W. and the adjacent
State. The trouble in this case lies with
Westralia. Mr. Fehon’s slipshod idea is to
pay for the lines on the basis of the last seven
years’ profits, regardless of whether these profits
were made by charging exorbitant rates (which
the Commonwealth would have to reduce),
by starving the repairs, by charging some of
the working expenses to loans, by creating a
temporary boom with borrowed money, or by any
other side issue whatever. It doesn’t even seem
to take account of whether the rolling-stock is good
or worn out. And by his brilliant idea the
Commonwealth would have to pay Westralia
about £14,000,000 for a railway system which only
cost it £7,000,000, and about £4,000,000 of this
would be because of W.A.’s great railway boom
in 1896, when its lines earned per cent., and
in 1897, when they earned over 9 per cent. These
were the big borrowing years. Now, the W.A.
lines, though still by far the most profitable in
Australasia, only return about 5 per cent., and
when charges are reduced by the Commonwealth
to something like the eastern level they
will probably yield only about 3 per cent.
Mr. Fehon’s system is calculated to produce some
remarkable results. If the railways are to be
taken over on a valuation, the only fair way is to
place them under Federal management for a few
years, let them be worked on exactly the same
scale of charges, and then take them over accord-
ing to the results they produce. To pay W.A.
£7,000,000, of which N.S.W. would have to pro-
vide over £2,000,000, as a bonus for charging exor-
bitant rates is too much joyfulness.

The Judicious Suppressions of George
Heid.
The latest great upheaval of G-eorge Reid in the
Freetrade interest —the one at Sydney on 4th
February—was a very tame affair compared with
the confident blast and blither of some previous
efforts. About a dozen fine, robust Calico J ames
fictions which were doing constant service only
two months ago have since then been exposed so
often that even the pachydermatous Reid has
dropped them. And the new fictions and misre-
presentations which took their place weren’t a
patch oh the old lot. Still, a few of them are
worth quoting :
The women employed in Victoria number twice those in
New South Wales. But we have more male hands in our
factories than they have. (Cheers.)
This isn’t a lie, only a suppression and a tacit de-
ception and a half-truth. Reid’s “Freetrade”
tariff came in force in N.S.W. on Ist January,
1896. Before then N.S.W. had been under a Pro-
tectionist tariff for nearly five years. When the
semi-Freetrade Parkes period ended (1891) Pro-
tectionist Victoria had 2323 more male factory
employes than semi-Freetrade N.S.W. The
Dibbs Protectionist period altered all that,
and in the last days of Dibbs’s tariff,
Protectionist N.S.W. had 5129 more male
workers than Protectionist Victoria. A few
years of Reid’s Freetrade so far destroyed this
lead that in 1899 more-or-less-Freetrade N.S.W.
had only 3022 more male factory employes than
Victoria, having lost 2107 of the advantage it
gained in its Protectionist days. Here are the
figures (Coghlan’s “Seven Colonies for 1900,”
pages 598-599):
Male Factory Employees in two States.
N.S.W. Vie. N.S.W. surplus.
1896 .. .. 42,908 37,779 6,129 •
1897 .. .. 44,333 38,671 5,662
1898 .. .. 44,673 40,631 4,042
1899 .. .. 47,063 44,041 3,022
The Dry Dog policy wasn’t long in starting to
destroy the big advantage which N.S.W. gained
in its brief period of Protection. These figures, of
course, relate to male employes only. If both
male and female employes are counted Victoria is
4424 ahead. Five out of every 20 of the Protec-
tionist State’s factory employes are women. In
this respect it tries to follow humbly and afar off
the good example of Freetrade Britain, where
eight out of every 20 factory workers are women.
If the importers’ party, which holds that Victorian
Protection is bad because it leads to five factory
employes in 20 being females, is prepared to say
that British Freetrade is 60 per cent, worse
because it leads to eight out of every 20 being
females, this paper will try to meet it on a fair
basis, so that a joint curse can be arranged.

The Indispensable Lyne ?
SI’ATE-Premier-plus - Federal - Internal - Minister
Lyne explains. He explains, this little politician,
why he hangs on to two Big Billets—taking
advantage of the Parliamentary vacation to defy
constitutional usage, if not law, and to subordinate
the great offices and great aims of government to
his petty ends. The explanation is simple, yet no
less imposing. Lyne is Indispensable. New South
Wales cannot get along without him. Nobody
can levy troops for foreign service without Par-
liamentary authority—nobody but Lyne. Nobody
can shove the right people into the lucrative
Sydney Harbour Improvement billets—nobody
but Lyne. And, above all, nobody can work like
the devil to put a coat of whitewash over the
awful hole in N.S.W. Treasury where
a cash balance should be —nobody but
Lyne. So Lyne remains in office—
just to save the country—and to save Himself.
N.S.W. Parliament ought to meet at once ; there
is far more than enough for it to do. But Lyne
wishes at all cost to stave off a meeting of Parlia-
ment —with the attack and exposure that would
follow —until his Federal seat is secure. He
will hang on to his Premiership till the
Federal elections —if the country will stand it.
He will postpone the meeting of N.S.W. Parlia-
ment till after the Federal elections—if the
country will stand it. Then he will leave his
trusty lieutenants, See and O’Sullivan, to sweep
up the mess he has made ; and he will be the Man
Who is Away—safely away in the Federal Minis-
try. That’s the programme : will the country stand
it? Unless country members make an organised
move, the country will have to. Where’s George
Reid? There’s lots of things The Bulletin
dislikes about George Reid ; but it ivould
like him to get one week in N.S. W. Treasury to
tell how the accounts are being cooked. Surely it
anybodyis competent to tell all about the cooking ot
accounts it is Reid. The Sydney dailies are silent.
The Herald is too slow to move ; the Telegraph
has just shown enough teeth to make Lyne under-
stand how nasty it will be if Somebody doesn’t get
a big billet. And Lyne is frightened to give Some-
body the billet, and frightened to refuse it. So he
hangs on, sparring for time—just “ a day or two
longer ” —and praying for Night or Bluchek.
The Gradual Mr. Evans.
Victoria’s Inspector of Penal Establishments,
just returned from 12 months’ holiday in Europe,
reports that the English penal system is the best,
and that the Victorian (his) system is in principle
very similar thereto. As dark cells are not in use
“at home,” they should, Mr. Evans thinks, be
abolished here ; also solitary confinement, “which
is neither reformative nor deterrent.” Victoria,
he considers, is moving generally on right lines,
though details will have to be tilled in carefully.
It will be observed, however, that the non-com-
mittal Evans says nothing about flogging, placing
in irons, or extension of or iginal term of punish-
ment without trial (at the will of a couple of
Victoria’s awful gratis beaks). And Mr. Evans
took a year’s holiday and travelled Europe
and America to find out this much ; while his
sub-assistant secretary, by consulting a few records
and reports, could have told him (l)that though in
the ten years ’BB to ’9B there was a 33 per cent.
decrease in the number of prisoners in Victorian
gaols there was a 25 per cent, increase in the
number of punishments awarded in gaol; (2) that
there was a 40 per cent, increase in the number of
cases of sickness ; (3) that no less than J/OO lunatics
were sent to asylums from the State’s gaols in
those ten years ; (4) that there were no less than
three attempts (one successful) at suicide in Pen-
tridge alone during the first half of 1900 ; and (s)'
that, in ’96, of 7752 prisoners 4582 were two or-
more times convicted as against N.S.W. 2290 out
of 9724. Further, with regard to Mr. Evans’s
opinion that nothing is to be learnt from the
methods of the Elmira (New York) Reformatory
—what about the fact that 82 per cent, of the
Elmira prisoners are so reformed that they never
come back, while in Victoria 84 per cent, do come
back ? And yet the placid Evans has the front
to say that “ Victoria is moving, generally, on the
right lines.” Still, his recommendation that soli-
tary confinement and dark cells should be abol-
ished is at least something. There are those who
think that the gradual Evans might decently be
abolished too.
That N.S.W. “ Surplus.”
The proposal of Cotton, M.L.A., that half-a-
million a year of the great surplus which N.S.W.
is to have under the Commonwealth tariff should
be applied to reducing railway rates, is being de-
bated by papers all over N.S.W., and debated
quite as seriously as if there was a visible prospect
of N.S.W. having any such surplus to reduce any-
thing with. Yet, as compared with South Aus-
tralia and Victoria, it seems that the three States
are really almost on a level, and one has not much
more chance of a big Federal surplus than the other
—which is to say that there isn’t any show worth
mentioning for even the best of them —which is
N.S.W. This is how the three panned out last
year, according to Cogiilan’s “ Statistics of the
Seven Colonies,” pages 22 and 26 :
Total taxation

Victoria, on this occasion, got to the end of the
year with a very small surplus after paying its
war-expenses up to date, while N.S.W. only
squared its accounts by putting part of its war
bill into the floating debt. S. A. paid its war bill
out of revenue, and just contrived to keep about
square. But even leaving this out of the question,
if the Federal tariff is made high enough to keep
Victoria square, as the State which wants most
money of the three, it seems that N.S.W.’s sur-
plus will be exactly £269,000, and the man who
proposes to take £500,000 out of that to reduce
railway rates is a genius.
The N.S.W. Public Servant and His
Troubles.
N.S.W. Public Service Board came out recently
with a set of new regulations, and the great
aggregate public servant —the Sydney one at all
events —smites himself hard on the ear and dis-
covers that he is both ends and the middle of a
good-sized ass. When Barton fought Reid at
last election in King division —where the civil
servant vote is rather strong —the only George
played a great stroke. He reluctantly admitted
that the N.S.W. Public Service Board was fixing
up a scheme of the most liberal character, which
practically amounted to every member of the
lower grades of the Public Service getting a rise of
salary every year. It was a beautifully liberal
scheme —so liberal as to be almost impossible
without wrecking the Treasury—but the Civil
Service vote went strongly for Reid, and he scraped
in by a small majority. Barton was too beastly
honest to wipe the floor with his fatted enemy
by saying that he had a scheme for giving every
civil servant two rises every lialf-yoar as soon as
he became Premier, so Barton went under.
Reid, who had previously got the support of
people not in the service by cutting down Civil
Service salaries and retrenching civil servants
wholesale, got back into his seat in ’9B by promis-
ing that money was going to be poured out upon
the service more liberally than it had ever been
poured before. Then after a considerable inter-
val the Public Service Board issued its new regu-
lations, and they proved so far short of what the
service had been led to expect that it almost wept
with rage as it thought of how it had been
taken in. The Public Service Board didn’t
even live up to its regulations either.
And finally it withdrew even these limited
concessions, and by its very newest regulations
the great bulk of N.S.W. public servants, when
they attain with difficulty to £2 17s. Bd. a week,
must remain there for life unless they can get
shifted to a new billet. Of course, the Public
Service Board is supposed to be a perfectly inde-
pendent body, over which the Premier has no
control. And when Reid announced, in his elec-
tion speeches in ’9B, that a body over which he had
no control was going to do something for the
public service—something for which Reid was in
no way responsible —it was just like the action of
the tail-collared brigade to rush and vote for Reid
in consequence. The fact that Reid practically
invited them to vote for him on the ground that a
body over which he had no power or influence,
and for whose actions he had no possible
right to claim credit, was said to be
going to do something, merely showed that he
reckoned the Civil Service was foolish enough not
to know any better, and the result proved that he
had accurately gauged its foolishness. So the
N.S.W. Public Service doesn’t know exactly
where to get satisfaction, and it is full of the
pleasing recollection that it has been, on the whole,
a very consistent supporter of the man who
created the Board which made all this trouble.

The “ Average-Expenditure ”
Foolishness.
Melbourne Argus of Feb. 4th, in a magnificent
but misguided effort to prove that the higher the
expenditure per inhabitant the greater the pros-
perity of the country, labored heavily in an over-
whelming sea of figures, and finally sank through
its own incapacity to correctly apply those figures.
According to the ex-freetrade —now revenue-
tariff —organ the expenditure per head per
annum in
New South Wales is .. .. £39 14 11
Australia (average) is.. .. 36 19 5
U.S.A. is 32 16 2
Great Britain is . . . . 29 14 9
These are Coghlan’s figures, and on the strength
of them the Argus labours heavily to prove two
things that won’t hang together : (1) That the
larger annual expenditure per inhabitant on food
and other necessaries in N.S.W. than in the Pro-
tectionist States only proves that the N.S.W.
man has more money to spend— not that neces-
saries are dearer ; and (2) that the larger expen-
diture in the United States than in Britain only
proves that necessaries are dearer — not that the
protected Yankee has more money to spend.
Unfortunately the Argus authority, N.S.W.
Statistician Coghlan, says distinctly, on page
419 of his latest “ Seven Colonies,” that “the stan-
dard of living is much the same in all the colo-
nies.” He also says that it costs £39 14s. lid.
per head per annum to live up to that standard in
N.S.W., while people can live up to the same
standard in the other (Protectionist) States for so
much less that the average cost of living is only
£3O 19s. sd. per head for annum all round. At
this rate the average of the six Protectionist
States must be about £36. Plainly the Calico
James party needs to get a new statistician. The
existing official N.S.W. statistician bumps its
theories in a dreadful fashion at every turn.
In its issue of 9th February Sydney Tele-
graph published a series of letters under the
heading, “Freetrader What it has Done for
England: Letters from Representative Men.”
The heading was, to some extent, a garish lie, in-
asmuch as a good many of the writers didn’t say
that Freetrade had done anything at all for Eng-
land. What they said was that in the period since
Freetrade was introduced ( i.e ., in the period during
which Britain was enriched by the great gold
finds in Australia, Maoriland, S’Africa, India,
and Canada; and during which the condition of
the whole world was immensely improved by rail-
ways, steam sea communication, and by the intro-
duction of steam machinery which enables one
man to do the work that required eight or ten
50 years ago) that during this period the condition
of Britain improved, in common with that of the
rest of the world. One or two held that Free-
trade was the cause, but, on the other hand, a
disastrous person named Davis, whose letter came
at the head of the list, said that the protected
American workman was better off than the Free-
trade English one. How on earth this lamentable
remark was allowed to get into print Heaven only
knows.

In the latest issue of United Australia, under
the heading, “The Federal Capital and Federal
Finance,” Medway Day strongly advocates The
Bulletin’s theory (set forth at length in “A
Policy for the Commonwealth ” and in many Bul-
letin articles) that the banks should be com-
pelled, as in Canada, to keep at least 40 per cent,
of their cash reserves in Federal Government
notes. As there are about £26,000,000 of coin
lying idle in the banks, this policy would make it
compulsory to exchange at least £10,000,000 of
this for Government paper, and the Federal
Government, without depriving anyone of a penny
or putting a single Government note in circula-
tion, would get the perpetual loan, without in-
terest, of £10,000,000 in gold. And if it also took
over the note circulation, which is quite a different
thing, it would, between the two, get the per-
petual loan of fully £13,000,000 without in-
terest. It could do this without trying any
novel or risky expedient whatever, but merely
by following the safe and long-tried example
of a rather conservative British community. Mr.
Day also cordially advocates The Bulletin’s idea
that money thus raised should be used to build
a Federal city, and to acquire a Federal Territory
much larger than the 100 square miles fixed as the
legal minimum in the Commonwealth Constitution
(The Bulletin suggested 5000 square miles), of
which Territory the Commonwealth should be sole
ground landlord. And by way of postscript he
says:
It is only fair to add that I do not claim to have origin-
ated these suggestions. They appeared in a section of
the Sydney press as far hack as December, 1899 ; and I
am surprised that they have not received the attention
which they seem to deserve.
This paper was the section referred to. It is
really wonderful how many people are willing to
use its ideas, and yet are too shocked to mention
the name of the disreputable publication they
were derived from.

Dear Bulletin. —Touching a par. in a recent
“ Wild Cat ” :
Hicks v. The Trustees, etc., Co., of Melbourne, testifies
eloquently to the joy of being a trustee. Hicks died Nov.
88, with many lands valued at boom prices, practically all
of which were held by mortgagees and banks, with the
exception of one piece bequeathed to his wife. Hicks,
with boom values on his mind, directed that five vacant
pieces of land were to be sold in a certain order first of all
to satisfy his debts, etc., and if they did not realise suf-
ficient, then certain other vacant and unproductive lands
not specifically bequeathed, were to be sold also. The
Trustees Go. allege they tried to sell as directed, but that
in 1889-90 buyers were not taking any vacant and unpro-
ductive lands, and while they were trying to sell the
vacant land first, as directed, the values of the other pro-
perties dropped to about nothing. Mrs. Hicks says
they should have sold everything else at any price,
and in any order, and her piece of land would have
been fjee and available. She therefore demanded its
transfer, which was declined. Then the legal corroboree
commenced. Defendant applied to the Court before the
C. J. for amendment of claim, which was refused, but, on
appeal to the Full Court, was granted. Trial on amended
claim before A’Bkckett (J.), case dismissed on plaintiff’s
evidence alone without calling for defendant’s. Court of
opinion the direction as to order of sale was a specific
trust. Plaintiff appeals, Full Court orders new trial, hold-
ing that all the vacant and unproductive lands should
have been sold and that the order of sale was not a
specific trust. New trial before C.J., who differs from
A’ Beckett and the Full Court too, holding that all the
properties specifically devised and otherwise should have
been sold, and that if they had, the difference between the
debts (£14,000) and an estimate of value in ’9l (£23,000)
left a margin for depreciation of £9OOO before plaintiff’s
specific devise would have to be touched. Therefore she
is entitled to her land. Defendant has given notice of
appeal. Result so far, two trials, three appeals, three
interpretations of the vital clause in the will, parties no
forrader and probably very sorry they did not toss up.
When the Great Commonwealth Drunk wound
up with a drunken smoke-concert to Imperial
sailors, it nearly broke the heart of honest Pastor
Shearston, the Sydney seamen’s advocate and
friend. And through the cracked valve came a
fearsome wail —• not intended for publication,
Shearston says; but it did get published, and
was red-hot. Shearston pointed out what
was perfectly true: that free booze, with the
Mayor presiding was simply an inducement to
ordinarily sober men to go and make beasts of
themselves; and a lot of them did go and make
beasts of themselves. The negative evidence of
people, including the Mayor, who “ didn’t see it ”
isn’t wortli a straw beside Shearston’s posi-
tive detailed statement of the free fights
outside the Naval Home, and the vomits
he wiped from the Naval floor, and the
books of sticking-plaster he lavished on his Naval
heroes. The smoke-concert was simply unlimited
swill—partly at the expense of the wretched
denizen of the backblocks (in the electorate of
Hume, say) who works from year-end to year-
end (when he can get work) and pays the
publican for his rotgut or goes without. The
Mayor and Co. should be thoroughly ashamed
of themselves for giving the Beast a
chance. Here has Shearston been educating
and moral-reforming his pets for years past
into a semblance of Cromwell’s Ironsides, —
covering up the Beast with pattern externals of
decorum and piety and never-touch-it, and
fondly hoping that the Beast was eradicated. Of
course the Beast wasn’t eradicated; your “first-
class fighting man ” —the kind of man that hires
himself out for a livelihood of fighting—must keep
his Beast if he is to fight. Shearston had simply
laid the Beast to sleep with texts and moral
maxims ; and the Beast woke up first chance.
The Bulletin agrees with Shearston that it’s a
shameful thing Mayor and Co. should ever have
given him the opportunity.

The latest item in the bright lexicon of the

in N.S.W. on Ist Jan., 1896, and statistics are
available showing the consequences to end
of 1899. During that period N.S.W. had, in
proportion to population, a smaller increase of
hands employed in manufactures than either
Queensland, Victoria, Tasmania, or South Aus-
tralia. It was the tail-end State —the Place That
Got Left, while Queensland was the front State.
N.S.W. has nearly three times the population of
Queensland, and yet it oidy increased its manu-
facturing population by 5806, while Queensland
increased the number of its manufacturing em-
ployes by 7464. These are the figures (to be traced

From Adelaide to The Bulletin :
There is evident in S.A. a certain tendency to throw
things at the soldiers the community was so proud of last
year. This applies only to the Bushmen’s Contingent—
not the Imperial article, but that financed locally, as well
as raised so. Blackmore, Clerk of Parliament, was, and
is, hon. sec. to the fund, and he has just reported to his
committee that the soldiers in Africa have been drawing
their pay there in full, while he has been paying
the greater part of it in Adelaide to their wives and
families, under orders left by the men for that
purpose. Commanding offler Hubbe has been killed, but
the Imperial paymaster declares that all the other officers
have told him that neither they nor the men left any such
orders. The position is a sufficiently serious one. The
said paymaster apparently accuses all the officers of a
combined fraud, or a combined loss of memory, the alter-
native being that he himself has got things mixed up in
an incredible fashion. Apart from his statement, the
whole thing is explainable as a very large and rather
fun M3' mistake. The Tommies enlisted for 4s. 6d.
a day, of which they left 3s. 4d. payable at Adelaide
to their families. When they got to Mafeking (so
returned members of the contingent declare) an order
was issued that they were to get ss. a day “Rhodesian
pay.” This now seems to have been meant as a rise of
sixpence only, but all hands appear to have taken it in
simple, good faith as meaning that their pa3 r was to be
9s. 6d. a day in all—3s. 4d. a day for their families and
6s. 2d. for themselves. Their troubles about details ! So
they had a good time, unconscious (as now alleged)
that they were taking the bread out of the mouths of
their wives and families. These latter, meanwhile, have
been getting their mouths filled all the same, at the rate
of 3s. 4d. a day, and the Fund now wakes up to find that
it has been double-banking to the extent of about 60 per
cent. As it is evidently quite impossible to let the
women and children starve, the orders are still honored
for the present. But an endeavor will he made to recover
from Tommy whatever he has not squandered in riotous
living, and he will probably be sued in the Small Debts
Court when he conies back. Also that Imperial paymaster
is requested to prove his words or eat them.
Said George Reid at Sydney recently :
But apart from the fiscal question, I cannot help being
gratified at seeing that Mr. Barton has taken up every
item of the policy which I have advocated for years. I
do not quarrel with him for that —I never mind an oppo-
nent doing my work —and when Mr. Barton speaks about
“ A White Australia,” may I sa3 r that I had the honor of
inventing that expression some years ago.
Apart from the fact that the term “ A White
Australia” was current in Queensland before
Reid was heard of in politics, the above statement
is quite true. There is hardly a good thing and
not many bad things in politics that Reid hasn’t
advocated. He advocated poll-taxing the Chinese,
though it was Rarkes who did it; and he advo-
cated one-man-one-vote, though it was Dibbs who
did that. He advocated keeping N.S.W. white,
though he allowed Hindoos to swarm into the sugar
districts absolutely unchecked. He advocated
Old Age Pensions, though he never established
them, but left that for Lyne to do; and he
favored a good Conciliation and Arbitration
law, though he never passed one. He also believed
in shortening the hours of the dreadfully over-
worked shop-assistant class, and Lyne did it.
Reid was in favor of sending a contingent to
the Boer war, and refused to send it —he “ laid
the papers aside.” He advocated Freetrade and
didn’t practise it. He advocated the abolition of
sweating, and had an alleged kindly feeling to-
wards a minimum wage law for that purpose, but
he never passed one. He cried out loudly for a
reformed Local Government system for N.S.W.,

either. he was solid on the necessity for
keeping the finances square, yet he had perpetual
deficits. He even advocated political honesty,
among other things ! Certainly George Reid
l as advocated most of Barton’s programme right
enough. But this weary land wants a man who
will do something.
