The old-time Japanese frivolity of “ Djin
Djin,” revived after a long interval of years at
Her Majesty’s (Sydney) is refreshing after the
respectable weariness of its predecessor. It says
a good deal for “ Djin Djin ” that it can bear up
against the general mediocrity of the Pollard Co.,
and appear a really good Xmas joke in spite of
everything. Like most Xmas jokes it is about
nothing in particular, but it is about it in a cheer-
ful and artistic spirit. It made its re-appearance
on a hot night, and the cool moss-green effects of
the Daimio’s gardens, and the sparkling waters,
and the frozen forest, and the cold glassy peak of
Fuji San are like the shadow of a big beer in a
blasted land. There are all manner of good effects
in “Djin Djin,” One of the best is where the
night is falling over Fuji San, and its spectral
hand steals out to hang the evening star on
the fading collar-bone of Day—just before
the great eruption blazes forth and pours itself
over the province of Hakidodo, or whatever it is
called, like the wrath of Heaven being emptied out
of a jug. Also, there is a sunrise effect somewhere
or other in the story, where the Morning softly
opens the great portal of the East and surrep-
titiously takes in its boots, which it had left out
to be cleaned the night before; and wandering
among all these things there is a large, incompe-
tent Japanese devil who is always bowled out,
and whose life is only spared at last on condition
that he marries a bent and yellow spinster who
appears to have a billet as lady-hindrance in the
Daimio’s family. This wrecked apparition, repre-
sented by Harry Quealy, ranks something above
the bulk of the co. by reason of her remarkable
dancing abilities. Also Miss Maud Beattie is tall
and stately and glittering in the clothes of the in-
evitable prince; and the ladies who represent Eng-
land, Scotland, Ireland, France and Italy are tall
and amazingly long in the legs; and Miss Gertie
Campion is a handsome vision as a princess; and
the wrecked mariner who comes on dressed in a
lift-belt and sings about his lost friend, Sam,
would pass well enough if it were not that Samuel
had a much brighter friend some years ago. On
the whole, however, the Pollard Co. appears to
much better advantage than in the lasi produc-
tion. The vocabulary of “Djin Djin” is evi-
dently selected from a more cheerful part of the
dictionary than that of the rather unfortunate
“Australis.”
• * m *
The most interesting features of “Aus-
tralis” —the dogs and monkeys, especially the
small Irish bricklayer-monkey who wheels the
barrow have been transplanted bodily into
“Djin Djin.” Also Miss Irene Franklin, the
lady with the beautiful arms who sings so power-
fully through a pleasing American nose, is still on
the programme, though her old song about eat-
ing “ chick-W” has given place to other ditties.
One of them is a doleful commonplace about some-
one who went back to the old home after a long
or short or middle-sized absence, and found that
mother wasn’t there, or else mother found that
he wasn’t there; anyhow, somebody was dead, or
absent, or had never existed, and some place or
other didn’t look the same in consequence; or else
it did look the same and they were shocked at its
continued resemblance to itself and expected it to
be otherwise. That sort of song should be dis-
couraged. A kinematographe of the Common-
wealth procession and the swearing-in is an
interesting reminiscence. Among them all they
keep “Djin-Djin” going to a rather late hour.
Present writer would rather sacrifice the swearing-
in. The ink-blot tail-coats of the serious-looking
men who are sworn in seem to spoil the gorgeous
color-effects of the Jap fairy-tale. Ancient fairy-
tale and modern black coat-tail don’t assimilate.
*# * *
Sydney Royal is closed at present, but
there is to be a great awakening on Wednesday,
when Nellie Stewart, Joseph Tapley, and Mus-
grove’s comic opera co. in general arrive with
the “Scarlet Feather.” Our Nellie’s return is
sufficient in itself to create a boom, and the
“ Scarlet Feather” itself, be it good, bad or indif-
ferent, matters little. Sydney hasn’t seen Nellie
Stewart for ages; but a good part of it will see
her next Wednesday even if it has to crush its
fellow creature’s hat on the stairs to do it.
# * *
One of the proposed theatrical happenings
of this week is a matinee in aid of the Children’s
Hospital at Sydney Royal on Saturday (16th).
“Bubbles” and “A Pair of Spectacles” are the
programme, and as it will be a good show for a
good cause, in a nice, clean theatre, the public
can’t do better than be there unanimously.
** * *
“ The Ladder of Life ” —said ladder con-
sisting of the M‘Brown, the M‘Jones, and the
M‘Smith, aided at a crisis by the M'Dougall—is
doing very large business at Sydney Lyceum.
This can’t be set down to its coherence, for it has
about as muoh as an intoxicated heretio who has
been racked by the Inquisition ; so it is probably
ascribable to the battles, and the tremendous
shooting, and the mountains, and the kilts, and
the steam-hammer. One unavoidable discre-
pancy, by the way, attaches to the latter large
iron gentleman. When the two tall wicked men
pull the lever the hammer comes down promptly
—thud ! Then they put the insensible heroine
under it, pull the lever, and go away quite con-
tented while the hammer remains up. Presently
the M‘Brown and M ‘Jones and M‘Smith pour
down from the roof like the gentle rain from
heaven, and shift the lady. Then the hammer
comes down. But for the beastly unpunctuality
of the bulky iron implement the drama would have
been finished.
** # *
The most interesting item of the perform-
ance of Victor, the illusionist and ventriloquist,
now pursuing the deceptive victual in the drain-
pipe at Queen’s Hall, Sydney, is probably Victor
himself. The man of picturesque appearance and
many smiles either enjoys keenly his own humour,
or else disguises his sufferings very successfully.
There is a streak of piety in Victor’s show, as
when the red-nosed marionette and the Paul
Kruger marionette and the marionette with her
face bound up like Madame Vine in “ East
Lynne” all unite in singing the “Holy City,”
taking a line each at a time. The pro-
gramme says that Victor shows wonderful powers
of conversing and singing in six different voices
while eating, drinking, and smoking. A fair
number of birds and children and other animals
come out of impossible places, and shopfulls of
goods are taken out of hats and other obviously
impracticable spots, while enough half-crowns to
buy them are picked off the shoulders of people in
the audience. Nothing makes an audience so mad
as when it finds it has been sitting all the evening
with 2s. 6d. on its shoulder without knowing it.
But the “woman who is burnt alive before the
eyes of the audience, ” isn’t, because there is a
hoop over her all the time. Also the burning
doesn’t create “awe and fear in the audience,”
though there are four lines of poetry in the pro-
gramme to say that it does.
*' * * *
At the Palace “ A Message from Mars ”
is now in its eighth week, and in the absence of any
news to the contrary, it seems that the tramp may
continue to have three whiskies and a biscuit on
Horace Parker’s premises for some little time
longer.
*» * *
The “Bush King” is down for another
week at Sydney Criterion. It is a splotchy, un-
decided drama that is upheld by the scenery, and,
to a certain extent by Hampier, and also by
various horses. Also it is the kind of play which
makes one wish, when in a bitter and bilious mood,
that this bright young country had never been
discovered, so that there could never have been
any bushrangers for want of any bush to range in.
There are times in everybody’s life when he
wearies even of bushrangers. “It is Never too
Late to Mend ” is to add one more to its long list
of revivals on Saturday.
** * *
The Professional Musicians festivated at
their rooms on Sunday night under the presidency
of Mr. G. Rivers Allpress, who opened the pro-
ceedings with a speech in which he rather neatly
juxtaposed the deaths of Victoria, Queen and Em-
press of India, and Guiseppe Verdi, King and
Emperor of the unlimited realm of imperial
melody. This speech furnished a motif for the
opening number—“ln Memoriam an instru-
mental sestet from the facile pen of Ernest P.
Truman, with horn passages that reminded the
listener of the echoes that wound their way
through fabled forests of Roncesvalles. This
number was received in silence, but the closely-
packed audience seemed dubious as to whether
they were paying a tribute of reverence to the
memory of Victoria or to that of Verdi. The
first item of the programme proper was Mendels-
sohn’s beautiful Trio in JD Minor, interpreted by
Messrs. Allpress (violin), Podenzana (’cello), and
Truman (piano). This was emphatically the treat
of the evening. Good old septuagenarian vocalist
Tom Rainford next sang “I’m a Romaner”
(Mendelssohn), and was rewarded with much
applause. Der Herr Truman then indulged in
piano soli, (a) “Eligie” and (b) “ Polichinelle ”
(Rachmaninoff). Mr. Henry Weir warbled “Too
late we met,” in which his tenor robusto was
pleasantly heard till he demoralised into a vibrato
finale that shivered the glasses on the tables.
Senor D’Alba (in memoriam, Verdi) sang “ In-
felice ” aria from “ Ernani,” and the proceedings
came to a musical termination with a ’cello solo—
“Berceuse” (Godard) —capitally played by Signor
Podenzana.
*x * *
There is often more real mirth in the small
shows that intend to be serious than in the large
ones which are full of a desperate resolve to be
funny. The Bulletin recently struck one of the
previous kind. Two fat ladies, called Clara and
Anna, display their ponderous proportions, in a
sort of schoolgirl costume, somewhere opposite the
Victoria Markets, Sydney. Clara is 295 t., while
Anna is a slender little blossom of 28st., though,
as she is only 21, there are hopes of her further
expansion. The stout ladies display considerable
anxiety to be prodded by members of their own
sex, so that the vast, green-sashed, 80in. waist may
be proved to be solid, and no suspicion attach to the
genuineness of the enormous ankles. Curiously
enough, Clara and Anna, who might be expected to
be distressed and to puff a good deal in the hot
weather, say they prefer summer to winter. The
Gatton tragedy, with a realistic background of
painted trees and buggy, is another feature of the
show ; there is also a wax murderer or two, whom
every woman in the audience points out to her
female acquaintance as resembling her husband.
But these things are serious. Clara and Anna, as
they heave themselves up like two affable geo-
graphical globes to greet the casual visitor, are
funny (though whether they know it or not is
another question), and in their casual way (if
people of their bulk can be casual) help to lighten
this careworn planet.
** * *
“The Casino Girl” strengthened with
sundry items from Williamson’s pantomime
(“Australis”) follows “ Florodora ” at Her
Majesty’s, Melb. But the end of the phenomenal
“ Florodora ” season is not yet. The receipts for
its eighth week exceeded the sum total of the first
week’s business, so they are likely to hold back
“The Casino Girl ” until April 9th. Nance
o‘Neil is due at Easter, and a month of the
massive lady will lead up to Italian opera in May.
** * *
The one notable happening of last week in
the Melbourne show world was “Ma Mie Rosette ”
(2nd act) at Her Majesty’s. An announcement
that Nellie Stewart, Brownlow and co. would re-
produce the naughty dream of the gardener’s
daughter at 2 p.m. filled every seat in the house
before the curtain rose, and George Darrell took
something like £3OO by this trump card of his
matinee. There have been two revivals of the
opera since the first production. May Pollard
succeeded Nellie in the name-part in ’95, and
Florence Perry made a quiet little attempt
to fill it some fifteen months ago, but these
only served to shake up the old fame of
the original show. The crowd came along last
Friday afternoon to see the Rosette whom some
had seen already and the others had often heard
of. They were glad they came. They looked for
an interesting performance and they got it.
Henry of Navarre’s chateau was not quite so im-
posing a scene at Her Majesty’s as it was at the
Princess’s six-and-a-half years ago, but the furnish-
ings looked smart and the incidental ballet
quartette worked most admirably. The average
quality of background girl in Williamson’s Co. must
be improving every year. From time to time a
vision of no previous importance steps to the front
and seems to invite the tender Florodoric ques-
tion, Tell me, pretty maiden, are there many
more at home like you ? ” Considered as a ladies’
seminary the co. is a pudding full of plums, with the
sweetest plums frequently lurking in unconsidered
places Thi3 by way of remarking that Miss
Engleheart, who played Segur, the page, a year
ago, had grown into a handsome and clever Cori-
sandre last week, whilst Miss Irene Outtrim was
a page—a page of—er—exquisite fiction.
* * * *
Six years and more have considerably
plumpened the artist who carried fresh milk to
Henri’s chateau on the night of Brownlow’s debut,
but she has not yet ceased to be a charming girl
m a beautiful golden wig. When she sat on the
stairs and sang that she was dreaming, the multi-
tude on the far side of the orchestra laid down
their opera-glasses and surrendered uncondition-
ally. Nellie Stewart was seen at her best in her
best character. Brownlow and Kenningham like-
wise took the proceedings seriously, and the
tableau was a convincing success. Present scribe
felt sorry for the three principals—the girl, the
Kmg, and the inopportune Vincent who was
doomed to die before daybreak. The disappoint-
ment in each case seemed so very sad, and there
was no third act to show that Rosette had only
been imagining vain things on a bundle of straw.
** * *
The usual performance at the Bijou was
turned off at the meter last Saturday, and the
house is just now being dusted, and made ready
for “ Puss in Boots.” Saturday next (16th) is the
appointed time for the Melbourne appearance of
that frivolous tragedy.
** * m
Who paid for the cable announcing that
Francie Adler had made a brilliant first appear-
ance at the Winter Gardens, Berlin, and was
“ accorded the honor of four recalls by a crowded
audience”? If the news is strictly accurate it
means that the ballad singing at Berlin Gardens is
considerably below the Rickards standard. After
J. C. Williamson had given Adler a good many
chances to shine, the plump little niece of
the Simonsens joined the Rickards co., and
she was warbling “Down by the Suhannee River ”
at _ Melbourne Bijou up to the time of her
going away. Australia didn’t mourn the loss of
her, though she had a certain quality in her voice
which another sort of girl might have turned to
better account. Madame Simonsen, of yore,
decided that Francie had all the qualifications of a,
first-class singer except the disposition to study,
and since then she hasn’t beeu much of a slave to
her art. Besides, the short-necked young charmer
is already threatening to become fat and breath-
less, so, taking one consideration with another
The Bulletin says “ Rats ! ” to that cablegram.
#* * *
George Musgrove’s gorgeously - dressed.
gantomime passes away from Melb. Princess’s on
aturday. _ “ Cinderella ” will then have asserted
its expensiveness on more than 60 occasions, and
the final performance may score one of the biggest
gates of the season, nevertheless the management
has certainly missed much profit by ignoring the
advice of that hard-headed old warrior, General
Opinion, at the outset. The voice of the
People is the voice of Boodle in these mat-
ters, but the management, seemingly, was
too haughty to hear it, for a time. Then
somebody awakened to the foolishness of clothing
a feeble panto, in rare raiment, and “Cinderella”
took a turn for the better. Signs of humor mani-
fested themselves in places whicli had become
notorious for gloom, and the result of this belated
endeavor to drive dull care away is a reasonably
cheerful show. Fred Ellis, one of the comedians
from beyond the seas, is an improved feature, and
there are others not too numerous to mention.
After Saturday the Princess’s will go into recess
for three weeks—then grand opera again.
At the Royal, where the balloon is, Mr. and
Mrs. Bland Holt are still hunting for the gigantic
jewel which went astray. Also the principal
robber continues to press a revolver-barrel against
Holt’s spine with an intimation that silence is
preferable to sudden death. “ The Great Ruby ”
has a name for being one of the higher melo-
dramas, therefore business promises to expand
rather than otherwise this next week or two.
*# # *
Now is the ninth week of “ Florodora ” at
Her Majesty’s, and the usual matinee will be
given next Wednesday. No doubt the future has
a last night in store for Professor Lauri, Grace
Palotta, Carrie Moore, and the mad effigy, but up
to now “ Florodora’s ” bump of prosperity sticks
out like a pumpkin.

Already certain of the old anti-Federal gang have arisen
to allege that the decline for the month of £13,828 in
Tasmania is owing to the £6B it had to pay for its share
of the Federal Government expenses, and that Queens-
land’s portion of the Federal expenses (£172) has caused
the drop of £38,504. The old anti-Federal crowd has a
fine gift of inaccuracy—not to say perjury.
The New South Wales expenditure for the
seven months to 31st January, 1901, is stated as being
£6,158,206, so the apparent deficit is £201,617. This com-
pares with the corresponding seven months of last finan-
cial year as follows:
31st January, 31st January,
1900. 1901. Increase.
Expenditure .. .. £5,865,133 £6,158,206 £293,073
Revenue .. .. 5,590,644 5,956,589 366,045
Decrease in
Deficit.
Deficit £274,589 £201,617 £72,972
This would look like a very great improvement if it
wasn’t for certain explanations which make it clearly
appear as a very great delusion. In the first place, the
above revenue includes £77,061 derived in one way and
another from the resumed area of Sydney, and when the
prices to be paid for the resumed property are settled it will
probably be found that the interest already incurred will
eat up the whole of the receipts. If this money is put to a
trust-fund (as it should be) and not treated as ordinary
revenue, then the deficit is rather larger than it was this
time last year instead of £72,972 smaller. Morever none
of this financial year’s war expenditure, and none of the
Federal celebration expenditure is included in the out-
goings. Including them the deficit is probably about
£450,000 or more, and things are certainly very much
worse than they were this time last yea'r. Also, the
seven months’ loan expenditure has been £1,490,014,
which is £161,732 more than for the seven months to 31st
January, 1900. Allowing for war, Commonwealth cele-
brations, interest on the purchase-money of the resumed
area, and extra loan expenditure, things in N.S.W. are
probably about £350,000 worse than they were this time
last year.


The Age of 24th October, 1900, writes re the

The Pioneer Tin Mining Co., of Bradshaws
Creek, Tasmania, have ordered from the Austral Otis
Eng. Co., South Melbourne, a large dredging plant for the
treatment of their tin deposits. The plant is a very com-
plete one, and includes vertical compound engine 400
h.p. suitable boilers carrying 1501 b. steam pressure, and
one of the Otis newest design 15in. gravel pumps. The
plant is to be completed and set to work within six
months.*

Messrs. Plummer Love and Co., 249 George-
street, Sydney, are large suppliers of all mining requi-

» * * *
Since the start of the Broken Hill Proprietary’s
new 5000-ton mill, most of the Barrier mines have been
engaged in remodelling their concentrating plants on up-
to-date lines, and wisely show a preference for machines
which have proved most successful on the difficult fines
and slimes. The machines that can claim this distinction
are the “ Luhrig ” Yanners, as supplied by Messrs. W. &
J. Lempriere, of Melbourne. The South’s new mill will
include a dozen or so, and the Junction Co. has also de-
cided to erect a number of Luhrig Vanners as an impor-
tant adjunct to its large plant. Other mines would do
well to follow this good example.*

mineral) in any part of Australia. Properties offered
must be subject to inspection. Cable address: “Option,”
Melbourne.*

The state of things in Queensland as compared
with last year is as follows :
Seven months Seven months Increased
to 31st Jan., to 31st Jan., Expendi-
-1900. 1901. ture.
Expenditure .. £2,320(770 £2,578,020 £257,250

The awful result is that Queensland is now £461,977 worse
off than it was at this date last financial year, and as last
financial year closed with a comparatively small balance
this one is in a fair way to close with £600,000 deficit.
Yet, despite the drought, there is no reason, except un-
speakably bad government, why Queensland should be in
any difficulties at all. The following is the revenue for
the seven months to 31st January in three consecutive
years:—
January 31. January 31, January 31,
1899. 1900. 1901.
7 months .. £2,444,115 .. £2,763,287 .. £2,558,560.
The revenue now is £114,000 more than it was in 1899,
and at June 30,1899, Q. had a very handsome surplus. But
there was a hoom in 1900, largely by reason of Tyson’s
death, and the expenditure was immediately bloated out
to absorb the revenue, with a cheerful assurance that
Tyson would die every year, and the result is disaster.
Despite the drought there is nothing the matter with
Queensland’s finances, except that the lazy, incompetent
Philp is now Premier instead of Dickson. So far loan
expenditure remains practically where it was last year,
but it is understood that Philp is going to start a great
burst of railway-building shortly.

» * • *
The local money market continues to cheerfully
meet all reasonable calls on it in the way of public loans.
Within a few days recently the City of Melbourne asked
for £350,000 at 3) per cent. (£175,000 for electric-lighting),
and was offered £565,500, or £215,500 more than was
asked for. Also, the N.S.W. Govt, asked for £500,000 at
3J per cent., and was offered £617,900, or £117,900 more
than it wanted. The N.S.W. Govt, got its money at
£99 16s. Bd. per £lOO, and the City of Melbourne at
£99 9s. Id. When the local market can thus dump down
£1,183,400 at short notice and at less than London rates
ithis country should be done with foreign borrowing.

It is remarkable that, spite of its former un-
audited a/cs., the Western Australian Bank got local
business faster than any of its competitors. In 1890 its
liabilities were £275,360, its assets £417,519 ; 10 years
later they were respectively £1,526,994 and £1,905,366.
*Of the five’totherside banks which Westralia can worry
for overdrafts, the N.S.W. has opened out by far the
-most vigorously ; in 10 years its liabilities in the State
have gone from £34,073 to £421,801, and its assets from
£133,012 to £666,206, Both the Union and the National,
however, show bigger figures each side (the Union’s Wes-
tralian liabilities keepwell over a million), but they be-
: gan earlier.

' The Universal Land and Deposit Bank appears to have
: got over the worst of its troubles by this time. It is a very
■ small affair with £6825 paid-up capital and £4392 reserves
—total shareholders’ funds, £11,217. In an enterprise of
• this size expenses are always proportionately heavy, a fact
- which makes the earning of dividends a matter of difli-
• culty. For want of more funds of its own the Universal
is burdened with mortgages and a bank overdraft, but
- when these are wiped off and the reserves have grown to
• three or four times their present dimensions, there should
he a good prospect before the co. Considering the cir-
• cumstances it does well to keep its dividends low, even
: though present earnings are comparatively high.

The Metropolitan Mutual Permanent Building and Invest-
ment Association has a paid-up capital of £144,774, of
which, if the assets were worth the value set against
rthem, £136,840 would be still intact. That is to say, the
• co. has, on paper, £257,918 worth of assets against which
i the mortgages, deposits, debentures, and all liabilities
. apart from capital, are only £121,078. But as all these
: assets yield just about enough to pay interest on the
: apparently small liabilities, and the working expenses of
; the business are at present almost wholly dead loss, it
i would seem that, on present values, a great part of the
« capital is lost. The co. is largely loaded up with unsale-'
: able and unproductive properties, and until it can find a
i market for them, and pay off some of its burdensome
mortgages with the proceeds, prospects are gloomy. The
gross revenue dropped from £11,629 in 1895 to £5525 in
AB9B ; then rose to £6438 in 1899 ; and fell again to £6087
in 1900.

S.A. Govt., which has been getting a bit anxi-
ous about supplies for its concentrating and new smelt-
ing plant, proceeds to develop a copper show close to the
Barrier railway line. It already runs a gold mine, pri-
marily to keep the Mt. Torrens plant going when there
is no stone available from private patties.
The Industrial and Provident Permanent Bene-
fit Building and Investment Society (Sydney) looks rather
better this year than it did last. A remarkable feature
about this concern is that it never seems to declare a
dividend —it just pays one, without even mentioning how
much per cent, it is, and as the capital keeps fluctuating
it is difficult to calculate the exact rate. But things
seem to be about as follows :

1900 .. 4271 .. „ „ = 3695 .. 7950
Owing to the constant withdrawal of paid-up shares, the
society is a great deal smaller than it was, the capital
being £60,065, whereas, in ’95, it was £113,525. The
total reserves are not very large, considering the
size of the institution, and they have diminished
somewhat through the payment in 1897, 1898, and 1899 of
dividends that were larger than the earnings—a proceed-
ing which is always lamentably reckless and wholly inex-
cusable. Still, 1900 shows a marked improvement, and
some little has been done to rebuild the damaged
reserves.

Dear Bulletin. —In my last par. (B. 12/1/’01) re
North Lyell Co., I freely replied to your previous par.
anent the amalgamation proposition, wherein you tried
to show that natural facilities and conditions favored the
Lyell do. taking the North unto her bosom, but “that
personal animus, &c.,” had been a barrier since the North
began its existence. I simply reversed matters, and
showed that the North, with its greater natural facilities,
would inevitably be the boss mine, irrespective of the
powerful influence of the Lyell crowd. I rather regret
you departed from your first argument, and introduced
new matter of a somewhat irrelevant character to your
first par., involving odious comparisons of the management
of two mines whose conditions are distinctly dissimilar.
I certainly agree with you that the Lyell Co. is a “ monu-
ment of good management ” as to running the show on
good lines now and working on the lowest labor basis.
But why ? Because the Lyell Co. started under good
auspices. The Bowes-Kelly crowd had money, and a mine
with a big exposed ore-body on which thousands had been
spent ere the Bowes Kelly people dreamt of touching the big
show ; and just at a critical moment they struck £120,000
of rich ore, which accelerated matters much, and which
they packed away on mules and sold to other smelting
works “without smelting one ton”—just as the North
people are now doing, only more economically. With
money it was quite easy to get good talent to run the
show and secure Sticht, the best asset the Lyell Co. has
to-day. Grotty agreed on everything up to this point,
but wanted the railway on level country, and strongly
objected to a veritable switchback —hence the “per-
sonal animus,” &c. Crotty knew the country, and his
strong individuality insisted on the better route which
against mammoth odds and opposition he has achieved.
Good management you say—yes, and better management,
too, in not allowing the Lyell co. to absorb the small fry.
As a matter of fact, they'did not want any outside show
until their ore became a low-grade proposition, and the
North had struck its bonanza. Things have changed, and
a huge monopoly is averted —say Yes. The North crowd
should have the‘“ Gilded Pill ’’—and if Asphodel is run on
good lines Crotty has a golden harp ere now. Crotty and
co. had practically not a bean when they went on their
own, and despite the powerful influence of the Lyell
crowd in London depreciating his efforts at every turn,
he triumphantly did the float on the eastern lode of
N. Lyell—which, by the way, will compare with the
Lyell sulphide body to-day. After many vicissitudes they
struck it rich and have spent money. Railway and
rolling stock, £205,428, brick-plant and bricks, sawmill,
large piers with cranes, &c., ready to make a start as soon
as the railway is finished by the best contractors in Aus-
tralasia—Baxter and Saddler. Railway, &c., very neces-
sary before they smelt a ton —say Yes. They also spent
£45,000 on the mine, which has an able manager (Muir),
to so systematise matters that he can run engine and
trucks into three different levels, which means great
economy in the future, and, when necessary, the main
line can tap the ore-body at the 100 ft, level with a main
tunnel; whereas the Lyell Co. is now arranging a main
shaft with which the North people could connect a branch
line up the Linda, and tap their now lowest level and
profitably treat their low-grade ore on their western wall.
I trust you will at least award the “ Orotty-Lonergan-
Madden-Muir” crowd the “Gilded Pill” for sticking to
their ship, and steering according to the varying vagaries
of financial head winds. ’Nuf sed. —James Coy, Gor-
manston, Tas.

A reliable Westralian correspondent writes in-
terestingly about Merton’s Reward mine, the property of
a lucky ex-Sydneyite :—Merton’s Reward is located some
20 miles from Mt. Malcolm, W.A. Unlike the big sensa-
tion mines of that country, but very much like the per-
manent mines of Hannan’s Golden Belt, there was very
little on the surface of this property to indicate the
existence of a rich enduring mine below. Frank Merton,
though not brought up to mining in his youth—he was
once an employ)) of Sam. Hordern—took to the bush
and prospecting in Westralia like a duck to water, and is
now, after six years’ experience, an exceptionally good
bushman and mining prospector. He had found seve-
ral fairly valuable mining shows before he discovered
that called after him. Only a few colors to be seen on
the surface, but it did not need much prospecting to
prove the existence of a mine. Within a few weeks
Merton had a crushing of less than 100 tons for over
7000 z., followed by a second equally good within a month
of the first. Within a year he had a 20-head stamp
mill running, and will probably have another 20
running in the next. Of course, he has had to fight for
his mine. He had no partner to start with, but somebody
soon found one for him. The verdict was against Merton
at the Warden’s Court; a “British jury” in Perth
unanimously found that he had a partner, but the Full
Court there finally decided that he never had one. All
through a year’s legal battle, Merton refused all offers of
compromise, except one—and the amount he offered
was, he stipulated, to be divided amongst the Perth
charities. But that was not the game of the other side,
and so the action went on, and cost Merton nearly £4OOO.
Winning mining law-suits, like newspaper libel-suits,
is an expensive business. Anyhow, Merton got back
his mine, and is working it all he knows how. The
veins are very flat, all lying about N.E. They are
stoped as they are followed down. He simply crushes
the ore in a stamp-mill and runs the pulp over copper
plates. Gold comparatively fine, but free-milling. Re-
cently one of these veins was struck in the hard settled
country considerably east of the other workings, sampling
soz. per ton. This looks good, indicating that the veins
are going to live in the hard ground. It is 3-Aft. thick,
and the ore is in two layers, the top' being sulphide, the
bottom oxidised, and a number of samples recently taken
by an outside expert go to prove that Merton’s own
estimate of soz. looks reasonable. Merton’s clean-ups
differ entirely from all others in Australasia. His boxes
are always beautifully plastered with amalgam—accounted
for by his not washing-off the gravel. He simply picks out
the big lumps of amalgam and throws the gravel back so
that after some time crushing there is a magnificent
accumulation. He has never scraped his plates since the
battery started over a year ago. They have now a coat-
ing in some places half-an-inch thick of amalgam, and
must be worth many thousands of pounds in gold. No-
thing catches amalgam like amalgam, and the ridge and
honeycomb appearance of Merton’s plates is a sight for
the gods. In most cases in N.S.W. and elsewhere plates
are so scraped as to largely interfere with their efficiency.
Merton lets the gold accumulate and takes the risk of the
plates being collared some day, lock, stock and barrel.
He manages the mine and mill himself because he has not
been able to get the man he wants for the work. He
made the charmingly modest offer of £5OOO a year to one
of the well-known Kalgoorlie managers. He pays the best
of his workmen £1 a day, started his feeders at 13s. 4<L,
and when they got a substantial rise they excused their
drunkenness on the ground that the rise had been the
ruin of them, whereupon Merton immediately put them
back to 13s. 4d., no doubt much to their satisfaction.

Mr. Mephan Ferguson, Engineer, Ironfounder,
&c., of Melbourne, Adelaide, and Perth, reports that the
great demand for spiral riveted pipes has necessitated the
increase of manufacturing facilities at his Footscray
works, and he is now in a position to supply orders at
very short notice, for all sizes of pipes from six to twenty
inches in diameter, with either patent cast-iron bolted
joints, flanged joints or the ordinary spigot and faucet
lead joints. Mr. Ferguson also manufactures boilers,
pumps, fluming, patent Nelyambo earth-scoops or tank-
cleaners, rabbit exterminators, refrigerators, ice-making
machinery, bolts, nuts, forgings, and all sorts of iron-work.*
• * * «
Paringa Consolidated, W.A. : One million
shares ; 750,000 issued. Chairman a sanguine potentate
named Lionel R. C. Boyle. Said they decided to work
the Paringa lease on account of its almost phenomenal
prospects and the prospects of its neighbours—Hannan’s
Brownhill and the Iron Duke, the latter of which profited
£25,000 from 600 tons of ore. A bore on the Paringa had
struck the Brownhill and Iron Duke lode at 590 ft. and it
went down in the bore to 30ft. As they were not quite
satisfied that it was this lode, they continued boring to
1100 ft. without finding any other “ which I think con-
clusively proves that it is this lode.” Alas, it was not
rich. The highest assay only went about 9dwt.; what
the lowest was he doesn’t say—perhaps under 9 grains.
“ However, we did not intend to let that disappoint us be-
cause the Brownhill lode was extremely patchy although it
is one of the phenomenal lodes—l was going to say of the
world.” He might as well have said it while he was about
it. “Before this lode was struck we had done a good
deal of work in the main shaft and I can assure you it was
a great disappointment that we had to leave the rich
places discovered in order to find out if we had struck the
Brownhill lode.” Why leave the rich places in a shaft on
account of a bore going down a long way from it ? “In
order to prove the accuracy of the assay of these rich dis-
coveries, we sent 10 tons —which is a very large amount—-
to the smelters and it produced 4|oz. of gold per ton.”
Writer cordially agrees —10 tons is a large amount, that
is, if it fell on the Chairman’s head. The end of it is that
somebody has lent the Paringa £20,000, and when the
mine is taken over for the money we shall hear no more
about it. The Brownhill and Iron Duke lode has been
struck at 470 ft. in the Northern Associated main shaft
about 250yds. from the Paringa bore-hole. Hence the
swollen-headed Paringa chairman should have explained
that the lode, in order to get into the Paringa at 590 ft.,
must afterwards become as flat as himself.

The Australian Medical Gazette for January
deals with the problem of increasing population
by diminishing the death-rate. It paints crut that
the decline in the birth-rate in Australia is duß
mostly to the artificial preventions of conception ;
and, while one cannot imagine any method, legal
or otherwise, of putting a stop to these artificial
checks, the Gazette suggests another method by
which the increase of population may be ensured.
It consists not in increasing the birth-rate, but in
diminishing the death-rate ; and this can be done
by preserving the lives of infants who die from
preventible causes. But how? The Gazette
proposes educating the persons who have charge
of them, “and to whose ignorance of the feeding
and hygiene of the young the appalling infantile
mortality is due.” “ The organisation forgiving
the needful instruction already exists in the public-
school system,” and the Gazette sees no reason
why all the elder girls at school should not be in-
structed in the care and management of infants ;
and very properly points out that most young
girls would take far more interest in this matter
than in history or arithmetic. The only apparent
difficulty in the scheme is the keeping up of the
stock of infants at home on whom to put in prac-
tice the theories taught at school.

A violent and well - merited explosion from
Queensland :
If Australian military affairs are to be lifted out of the
old rut of the British war-office system, ’twill need a
violent jolt to accomplish the same. What is required
for the Defence portfolio is a man of strong character —
one with decided republican opinions. There are two
offices in the Federal Cabinet which should be filled by
such men —Justice and Defence. These two departments
will come into touch with British influences, and it is in
the best interests of Australian national life there should
be no pandering to British systems from mere senti-
ment’s sake. Devious are the ways of defence methods
in Australia, assisted, I assume, by the British War
Office. I’ll speak for Queensland only. Three years
ago our Defence Force was armed with Martini rifles. A
re-armament was decided on. The best British regu-
lation rifle then extant was the Lee-Metford. For
some inexplicable reason Queensland decided she didn’t
want the best. Anything better than the old weapon
was good enough. So £15,000 went for third-rate Mar-
tini-Enfield rifles. Now, after little more than two years’
use, these weapons are to be superseded by the Lee-
Enfield, another wearied-out second-rate implement. Of
these 4000 were landed in Brisbane the other day. Next
comes word by cable that the British Govt, has decided
to re-arm its forces with the improved Lee-Metford. By
the time this is completed Queensland will probably have
re-armed again, as per usual, with some second-rate
weapon that perished years ago—unless the Federal
Govt, steps in. Who is responsible for this drop-and-
carry-one system ? What we want is a hard and fast rule
of “ the best weapon only ” when re-arming ; no more of
this Darwinian graduated evolutionary business, but a
drastic and efficient change. We waste a lot of money on
our defences and are getting a mighty poor return for it.
♦
Re the minii g-apprentice question :
The oft-recurring but never-settled question of mining-
apprentices has bobbed up again at Broken Hill; and the
serious attempt that is being made to do something
definite will certainly be followed with interest on those
scores of other fields where it has for years formed a
reliable fall-back subject for the crib-time “ pitch.” The
miner is a more-or-less skilled workman. For one thing,
he gets a good bit bigger pay than an ordinary laborer ;
though a doubt as to how much he gets for his skill and
how much for the risks he takes occurs straight away to
disturb the argument. Anyhow, there certainly is some
skill; but, notwithstanding that, and though lack of skill
in a man working as a miner may be frightfully disastrous
not only to himself but to others, there is no regular or
recognised system of training miners. On the face of it
this is illogical. But the miners (most of whom haven’t
had any regular training themselves) answer in this
way : “ Apprentice labor means cheap labor, for the ap-
prentice can do no more and no less than take the place
of a miner. True, a good miner is a more or less skilled
man ; nevertheless three-fourths of what a miner has to
do calls for no special skill at all. So a farm-laborer or
shearer with plenty of strength can do most of the work
as well as the miner, and generally can do more of it,
since the old miner, from the very nature of his calling,
isn’t usuallv a physically-strong man. Thus, even while
the new-chum is picking-up the skill, he is generally
worth as much to the co. as the already-skilled miner.”
All of which is in the main true ; a man like that can any-
how soon hold his own. But the modern mine-
manager, highly-trained himself, says this isn’t
good enough what is needed is to raise the
standard of miners’ knowledge. And that seems good
argument, too. The miners fall back on the ground
(which, of course, is contested) that there are at present
plenty of good enough miners, anyway ; and, fearful of
anything that might be tantamount to a wage-reduction,
say “ Let well-enough alone.” On the other side, the
managers aren’t going to risk any row to bring about a
change, however desirable, and as the “ coming genera-
tion ” of miners who are the most interested of all haven’t
got a say in the business, it is exceedingly unlikely that
in the end anything at all will be done. Still the real
object to be aimed at, the raising of the standard of
miner’s knowledge, is being slowly achieved through the
ever-increasing attendance at the technical schools ; and
it is through these places that it seems most likely the
best solution of this heavy puzzle will come.

Those excited Foreign-trade gentlemen who are
so solid on the fact that the stationary character
of Victoria’s population, as compared with the
vigorous progress of N.S.W., knocks Protection
kite-high, may possibly get a hard punch when
the census returns come to hand a few months
hence. All the population estimates since 1891
(the date of last census) are mere guesswork. It
is known for certain from the annual statistics
that Victoria has about 6000 more miners employed
than in 1893 ; has nearly 21,000 more factory em-
ployes ; has about 900,000 more acres under
crop, and has a small increase in live stock,
and all these things should mean more popula-
tion unless the land cultivates itself, and the cows
herd themselves and milk each other. Yet the
population is said (by guesswork) to have
decreased 7000 in the seven years. The
known increase of factory employes alone
last year was actually nearly twice the
alleged year’s increase in the whole population.
And, on the other hand, N.S.W., with a smaller
increase in factory employes, with 1,400,000
acres under crop, but with its Far West
pastoral industry so wrecked by drought that
its sheep have decreased about 20,000,000, is
said to have had an immense increase of people.
There may not be anything wrong with these
population guesses, but, in view of the things that
are actually known, they look a trifle fishy.
T O THE YOUNG PROSPECTOR.
Take your pick and use it handy,
Prospect every vale and hill,
Shun the granite as its patchy,
Watch the slate and diorite hills.
Trace the colors to their source among the hills,
Or to the washes in the plain.
Follow, trace and track the mineral
To the source from which it came.
Take the dish and swish it with a gentle swing,
Give it just that curly twist,
Washing out the sand and slimes,
Leaving all the gold behind.
Each grain of gold from a pound of dirt
Will yield ninety pennyweights per ton of dirt.
Take well your bearings and travel by
Your shadow cast —watching for the mineral rocks.
The gum for gold, and salmon for telluride ores,
Mulga for manganese, silver-lead ores,
Copper, antimony, bismuth and lead,
But don’t prospect the spot where the spinifex grows.
Travel light and easy be your task ;
For your couch take feathers from the scrub,
Or in the earth a hole you -dig to rest your hip,
And between the camp-fires sleep without a rug.
Notwithstanding this, The Bulletin subscription re-
mains at the old rate of £1 per annum and postage.

Cabled that the King will not make the
Duke of York Prince of Wales until after he
(Prince George) has visited Australia, and some-
one suggests in a Sydney paper that the Royal
patent, or whatever it is, should be sent nut from
England and conferred in Australia. Happy
thought! Send the patent to Waxcellency
Madden and tell him to make George into Prince
of New South Wales !
* * * *
Re the reference m Hobart Cathedral
prayers to the Duke of York as “Prince of
Wales,” Dean Kite writes to the Mercury that
though he was well aware that such Princedom
had not then been re-created, “he saw no objec-
tion to anticipating in prayer, by way of courtesy,
a title sure to be conferred.” York, you see, was
already Prince of Wales in Heaven ! '
* » * *
Queen-street, Melbourne, was not named
after Queen Victoria, whose accession was not
known when the square mile of Melb. was laid
out. Taking the streets from the westward, the
order first proposed was King, William, Queen,
Adelaide. The last was changed to Elizabeth.
Prayers for William IV. were offered in Mel-
bourne for three months after his demise.
* * * *
Those magnificent wreaths and floral
designs hung around the Queen’s portrait and
paraded on Melb. Parliament House steps for the
admiration of the multitude, after being hung in
florists’ windows for days and carefully advertised
in all the papers, faded into utter insignificance
beside the little circlet of daisies and white
geraniums—evidently home-made —that someone
placed at the foot of Victoria’s statue in the large
hall. It bore no card, no message, no name. It
was genuine ! And how disgusting was the
manner in which the glaring trade and other
advts. were plumped down at the Queen’s Statue
in Sydney, and whooped up in the newspapers !
People wondered much at how an item like that
about the London newsboy who, seeing in a paper
a scare-heading about the Queen’s death, rever-
ently placed upon it a little bunch of violets, came
to be cabled. It was wonderful. How it came to
be printed is still more wonderful until you come
to consider that everything sent over the wires
costs so much a word, and the members of the
cable-syndicate have a fine eye for the bawbees and
never waste anything that has been paid for.

As solemnly set out in Kilmore (Vic.)
Free Press the following cable was despatched
by Mr. F. W. Wortley, J.P., President of the
Shire, on behalf of the residents
T _., Chamberlain, London.
Kilmore mourns death Queen.
Wortley, President.
‘‘Wort icy President ! ” Ye gods ! President
Wortley, of—Kilmore!
But after all, someone or other in England may
possibly have heard of Kilmore, which is the locus
of a classic Australian story. Long ago, one
Larry Bourke presided at a meeting called to hear
the political views of a candidate who sought to
represent the potato growing district of Kilmore
111 . keg. Assembly. The ignorant and
primitive Larry couldn’t talk a bit, beyond being
able to convey that he wanted more whisky, or
desired another pound of beef put on his plate,
and he had gradually acquired some dim notion of
his oratorical deficiencies. So he hated un-
necessary, talk, and when at the end of the
candidate’s harangue, a man in the audience stood
up to cross-question the speaker, he was at once
knocked on the head with a bit av a shtick at
Larry’s instigation, and violently thrown out of
tjie' hall. Then Larry arose and naively enquired :
“Is there anny other gintleman would like to ask
a question ? ”

“Scotsmen ” volunteers for the Marquis of Tulli-
bardine’s Horse didn’t maintain their enthusiasm.

whether the sudden death of that personage would
leave his contracting warriors amply provided for?

* * * *
Nothing is more calculated to impress our
pushmen with the horrors of war than this doleful
baragraph in “a letter from the front,” published
by Hobart Mercury :
We are allowed beer here. It is a great treat,
but, worse luck, it makes me sick if I take more
than a pint. I suppose after being on biscuit and
bully so long it is too rich for us. I expect to go to
Durban or Maritzburg for a holiday, which I very
much want, as this work tells upon anyone.
The Mayor of Sale (Vic.) was in hospital
with a broken leg on the day King Edward VII.
was proclaimed, and if the local P.M. had not
arrived next day and made the proclamation from
the Town Hall steps Heaven knows what would
have happened. The blessed township would
have had no King, no “ Gorsave,” no trumpeting,
no nothing. It might even in time have degener-
ated into a disreputable, disloyal, back-blocks
wilderness. It was a narrow squeak !
A North Island (M. L.)
registrar reports that there
were 56 first-births in his dis-
trict in 1900. Of these 13 were
frankly illegitimate, and 29
took place so soon after marri-
age as to suggest quite a num-
ber of things. This leaves 14
perfectly respectable births (25
per cent.) out of 56.

ACHING ON THE SHELF.
HIS MOTHER : Doth cry about the toothache, Tommy. Be a man and learn to bear a little
pain.”
T OMMY: Oh, it’s easy for you to talk. When your teeth ache you can pull ’em out and put

Despite the Jingo daily-press cackle, there
has been no rush of N.S.W. bushmen citywards
to join the Federal contingent. There has been a
bad hangfire over the thing and the recruiting-
sergeants had to hover about the entrance gates
to the cricket ground last week, during the inter-
state match, sounding the weedy Sydney city youth
as to his desire for glory in S’Af'rica. The facts
that men thrice rejected previously, under more
rigorous scrutiny, are now accepted, and the small
percentage of rejections as published, indicate
that quantity, not quality, is the needed article
this time. As for the country element it is mostly
made up of country-town young men, not bush-
men by any means—a slouching, short-coated,
loose-lipped, flashy set, whose intelligence is best
shown up by one Mudgee gallant who said they
were going to fight them Boer arfcastes because
they would not give white men one-man-one-vote.
After all, though, the material is good enough for
its mission—the “ pacification ” of the enemy by
burning the home of his wife and children.
* * * *
A Ballarat resident who lost a son in the
action at Ottoshoop last Aug. has received as con-
solation a Royal application for a photo, of the
deceased. With prophetic vision he had for-
warded a copy some weeks before. Similar re-
quests have been made by the Royal private sec.
to other grief-stricken parents of Australian
heroes. This photograph business is the latest de-
velopment of the gentle art of “kidding ” the poor
Australian. Each mourner addressed is foolish
enough to take the application as an evidence of
special sympathy, whereas it is in every case
merely an item of an automatic, cold-blooded
business system—recalling the smart Frisco hotel-
keeper who, when Mr. and Mrs. Jones arrive at
his caravanserai, immediately has a huge and
lovely bunch of flowers sent to Mrs. Jones’ room
with his compliments inscribed on his card. Mrs.
Jones is delighted, and thereupon remarks out
loud ‘ What a nice man the Major must be!”—
he has evidently heard of her beauty and talent
and exquisite taste in dress. Of course he hasn’t,
and so long as Mr. Jones pays his bill the Major
never loses any sleep about the Joneses. The
flowers are ordered by the cwt., and it is a junior
hotel-clerk, ora bell-boy, who always attends to
Mrs. Jones’ bouquet. All that the crafty
“ Major ” does is to organise the aforesaid method
of pleasing Mrs. Jones and see to it that the
flowers are bought fresh—and cheap. His troubles
about Mrs. Jones !

The drunkenness and rowdyism of the
“Imperial” contingent were directly blameable
upon the officers. Bobbers lead an unnatural life
in camp or quarters, and when discipline is
relaxed, as it certainly was here, they took ad van-
tage of it. That gun accident at Moore Park
serves of itself to show the lack of proper super-
vision by the officers. Firing is strictly forbidden
unless under the control of an officer, and in 'the
presence of a surgeon. Neither officer nor surgeon
was present. The promised inquiry into that
affair, by the way, has been burked. Why?

S. A. Premier Holder was right, both in
reason and as a matter of taste, in refusing to
have a public accession-proclamation at Adelaide
while the body of the late Queen was still above
ground. Holder, doubtless, knows well that it’s
not Edward VII. the local bigwigs are troubling
about—but about a chance to don their full official
frippery, and make tawdry asses of themselves
before an open-mouthed public. Little Sajnmy
Way, for instance, won’t be easy till—staggering
under a burden of gold-lace—he lias had a chance
of emulating in this respect the mellifluous and
cocky—not to say peacocky—Madden of Victoria.
After all Carlyle was right—the world is mostly
Clothes!
The soldier leaned upon his sword,
And wiped away a tear ;
Exhausted by the gallant fight
He’d made to hold his beer—
That long, delusive beer.
He placed a quart upon parole
Beneath his bandolier ;
Alas ! it beat him to detain
The Boers’ rebellious beer—
Their extra-mobile beer.
“ This tells upon my health,” he groaned,
“ And makes my head feel queer.”
O, it grieves him to be beaten by
A quart of rebel beer—
Strong, freedom-loving beer.
So, when he’s invalided home,
We’ll greet him with a cheer ;
For he was floored unfairly by
The Boer’s sharp-shooting beer—
Their damned, explosive beer.

GOD SAVE THE KING !
* * * *
Notice in a Albury (N.S. W.) shop-window,
on the eve of the Queen’s burial: —
Our estibleshmint will be closed during the unevarsal
morn Saturday.
And here is a notice from the window of a Prahran
(Melb.) butcher’s shop :
In consequence of the funeral of our Late Lamented and
Dearly Beloved Queen, this Establishment will be closed
next Saturday, and also on the following Wednesday for
the Butchers’ Picnic.
* * * *
The belief that the Duke of York will
actually visit Australia has revived “loyalty” in
Melbourne, and firms that had sent along big
orders, in anticipation of a York shivoo, begin to
smile again. One house, that had a shipment of
terra-cotta effigies of York on the water, was
driven into rabid anarchism by the prospect of
having to sacrifice its goods as road-making
material.
* * * *
The Bulletin’s prediction re the working
of the “York” visit is justified by newspaper
results. The opportunity for “sacrificing sadness
to duty, and all that sort of thing,” has been duly
turned to political account, and the spirit of Jingo
journalism is decorously jubilant. Says the
Argus: “Now that we have the actual decision
we are at liberty to say that it is in accord with a
lofty conception of Royal service. It can easily
l»e understood that His Majesty is personally loth
just now to part for several months with his son
and successor. But the visit of the Duke and
Duchess at this time, so soon after the good
Queen’s death, will have the greater influence in
cementing the relations of the Commonwealth to
the throne and the empire. . . Just how far
the pageantry and festivities will be affected by
the event we all deplore has yet to be deter-
mined.” The Duke may be trusted to take care
that the sad event will spare him a good deal of
useless boredom.
* * *
Dr. Barrett (Vic.) on the population pro-
blem: “Women are being edu-
cated to do that which men can
do, and are leaving undone the
very thing which men cannot
do, namely,, bear children.”
THE “ PICTURE ” SOLDIERS IN
AUSTRALIA.
What price “ returned ” kharkinow?
Several men got into the sth and 6th Vic.
contingents by swearing they were single, although
they had wives and families. They were scared
out of Melbourne with threats of prosecution for
perjury—even the hero who produced a note from
liis spouse, saying : “For God’s sake, take Bill.”
When Australia is wrung pretty dry of her young
blood, a married-men’s contingent will be a great
success.

Australasian contempt for our own people
to find in the London Times advts. for
a Sydney Town Clerk, a Dunedin City

“Melb.”: Last night I heard
Mr. Baker, a missionary, make
some extraordinary statements
about his financial operations in
Australia. He was a shorthand-
writer, carpenter, and lawyer in the
Transvaal. At lawyering -he did
well. Then ha turned missionary,
and employed a number of others.
Before coming to Australia he sold
his farm for £l2OO. Two-thirds
he left for the support of his
brother missionaries in the trou-
blous war-time, and brought £4OO
to Australia. However, he sent
£3BO of this back to Africa, and
determined to plunge in here wholly
on Faith. This carried him
through gloriously for 15 months all
over Australia and Maoriland. _ He
never took up a collection for his own
benefit. One admirer in Dunedin
gave him £lOO. Why don’t the clergy
'try this system ?
Alick Forrest’s son, who is also Bigjohn
Forrest’s nephew, is to go to S’Africa with the
next W.A. contingent as a real live lieutenant.
Said son and nephew is an estimable small boy of
17. Local report has it that the officer in com-
mand of the contingent was communicated with
on the subject of finding a vacancy for this youth
fresh from school. He replied that there was
none, and was promptly floored with an intima-
tion that he must find one. So he found the
vacancy, and Small Boy Forrest goes out in com-
mand of men old enough to be his father. And,
in the irony of things, it may be his luck one day
to be in command of some detachment which will
get a chance to end the campaign at a blow by
capturing De Wet, and which won’t do it because
De Wet is hardly the man to be taken by any
influential person’s small boy. Were it possible
to ascertain how many years of war have deso-
lated the world because the chance which might
have ended a campaign at a blow fell to some
influential person’s small boy who had no right to
be filling a grown man’s billet, this globe would
probably be a very much enraged sphere indeed.
* * * *
In a certain N.S.W. country town recently
a lawyer, considering himself aggrieved by the
editor of the local paper, walked up to him
in the street and punched him on the jaw.
The scribe, for his own reasons, did not
retaliate ; and this coming to the ears of
a local Govt, official—who had also an
“edge” on the paper-man —he got a
horsewhip and ambushed the editor, but
received, to his huge satonishment, such
a pounding from the ink-slinger that he
was laid up for a week.

English judges appear to be mak-
ing a fairly vigorous attempt to cope
with a prevailing epidemic of fraudulent
solicitor. The cable has reported sen-
tences of imprisonment ranging from 12
years to 12 months for sundry lawyers
who misappropriated trust moneys, the
fraud in each case being on all-fours with
the offence which ranks as a common
or garden kind of ‘ ‘ misdemeanour ” in,
say, Melbourne. And this reminds The
Bulletin that Yic. Crown Law Dept, is
continuing its policy of masterly inactivity
re lawyer Grey, True, friend Grey is not a
more unprosecuted criminal than many other
learned Victorian embezzlers, but as his case
happens to be specially conspicuous The
Bulletin mentions it once more.
A deafening row is in progress in South
Australia about the unequal distribution of peace-
medals to the State-school children. Only chil-
dren aged between five and thirteen received
medals, and shindy, disagreement, and heartburn-
ing have ensued. Now it is proposed to get over
the difficulty by giving peace - medals all
round. Considering that De Wet’s army
has carried the war into British territory,
and Kitchener is demanding 30,000 more
troops, and further contingents are being sent
from here, this peace-medal idea is humorous.
Just to “ save its face,” as the Chinese say, S.A.
Govt, should explain that the medal is intended
to commemorate the close of the Crimean cam-
paign.
# ¥ * »
The very latest phase of the loyal condo-
lence and congratulation business is set forth by
the Mayor of Brunswick (Vic.), who actually
instructed the town clerk to write to the
papers tendering his thanks to the Brunswickers
for their due and proper observance of the day of
the late Queen’s funeral. The Mayor didn’t
actually refer to my ratepayers of Brunswick —
but no doubt he thought it.
* * * *
N.S.W. District Court Judge Gibson,
while sitting at Sydney last week, was so
oppressed by the heat that a Court underling was
despatched for a Japanese fan, which his Honor
waved and flaunted in a manner worthy of the
Mikado. Doubtless the recent turning of his
Honor’s Court into a forum where civil, criminal
and marine justice is in turn administered, with
taxation appeals thrown in and industrial arbitra-
tion in prospect, has made it necessary for him to
be furnished with all the proper accessories of an
Australian Pooh-Bah.
* * * *
Apparently the Law knows several offences
unknown to the casual citizen. A Melbourne
girl, who t’other day sent a wire to her ex-“ boy,”
signing an assumed name with intent to deceive
him into meeting her, was flabbergasted by being
fined £2 for “wrongfully and unlawfully signing
a telegraphic message.”
* * * •
Already Mayor Gillott’s custom of appear-
ing for litigants in some cases at Melb. City Court,
where he sometimes adjudicates, has led to lawyer
Gaunson charging the police with unduly favoring
the Mayor’s clients. The charge of partiality was
refuted on other and proper grounds, but—justice
should, like Caesar’s wife, be above suspicion.
* * * *
The damsel who, t’other day, fired several
revolver-shots at a Melbourne rail way-porter, was
“dissatisfied with his treatment of her.” Mrs.
Fraser’s historic shot is becoming exemplary, and
soon now, in Melbourne, it will be to the unwill-
ing swain, “ Your love or your life ! ”
» * * *
When that Carlton (Vic.) husband rebuked
his wife with a claw-hammer the other day and
subsequently slashed his throat with a razor, the
eternal lodger—the alleged cause of the trouble-
observed “that if he ever attempted suicide he’d
make a better job of it.” He kept his word. Last
week the husband was fined £2O, and the day fol-
lowing the lodger razored his head nearly from
his body, leaving behind him a brief note:
“There is a certain party in Melbourne who
knows why I have committed this rash act.”
‘ ‘ Rash act!” The reporterese language had soaked
into his soul.

A Queensland Minister of the Crown, after
a day’s schnapper-fishing, wired the fat J.P. of his
electorate: “Meet schnapper to-night’s train.”
On receipt, the intelligent J.P. rushed into the
township, and ordered rooms and a hot supper for
Mr. Schnapper, then trotted the buggy to the
railway-station.
The casual Australian again. At a Carl-
ton (Melb.) lodging-house, t’other day, one Muller,
who had “delusions,” went into the yard and
began hacking at his throat with a razor; where-
upon a woman, attracted by “strange gurgling
sounds,” piuckily grabbed Muller and took a fall
out of him ; but as she was not strong enough to
hold him she called to three men working close by
to come and help her. But they only “ turned
their heads away and gammoned to be deaf.”
However, a passer heard her cries, and helped her
to disarm the would-be suicide, who was then
taken to hospital by the police. Meanwhile the
three workmen calmly proceeded with their occu-
pations. “If people want to slay themselves or
others, well, let ’em —we’ve got to get on with our
graft! ”
* * * *
The greatest triumph obtained by the
pietists in Australia is stopping the Melbourne
Sunday street-watering.
While the Perth (W.A.)
tramways were yet in course of
construction, a certain local
doctor, while driving on his
professional rounds one stormy
night, ran his dog-cart into a
heap of road-metal which was
lying beside the track, and
which, he alleged, showed no
light. He claimed damages
against the Tramways Co.,
who cited a certain city official
to testify that on his way home
that very night, and shortly
before the doctor's accident, lie
had seen a hurricane-lamp
alight and securely placed on
the road-metal aforesaid. The
witness related that after his
arduous duties of the day and
a visit to the now historic
Mayor’s parlor, he went to his
club, where he remained awhile,
and then hailed a cab, in which
he was driven home. “Did
you on your way home,” asked
counsel, “ observe a lighted
lamp resting on the heap of
metal?” “Idid,” replied the
witness positively ; “in fact 1
saw two lamps.”

* « * *
Adelaide City Council, bossed by a Tory
majority, has declined to close the metropolitan
markets, which it owns, on Saturday nights, and
fall in with the overwhelming majority of shop-
keepers who now close at one o’clock on that day.
Even many of the business-people interested
petitioned for Friday night, but without avail,
and the markets are now the only stumbling-block
to the consummation of the great reform for which
Kingston worked hard during five years.

my ease anywhere out o’ gaol.” Three months.

J\fOT when I lay and watched the lovely dimples
at her neck,
That came and went and came again like ripples
on a heck;
Nor when the music of her voice put all the birds
to shame,
That stilled their mellow melodies to hearken whence
it came;
Nor when her eyes by passion fired, flashed gener-
ous desire;
Nor when she bound me in her arms and set my
brain a-fire;
Not then I felt so sweet a pang of joy and love
and fear
As almost rent my heart in twain when first she
called me “Dear.”

That man who said no woman ought to live in
Sydney in January, February, or Maroh, was like
the rench girl—he “knew something.” Every-
one who can has left town ere this, but there are
a few who remain, and last week, while the spine-
less, insinuating, moist north-easterlies were
ravaging the city, many women believed they
wouldn t last much longer. Last Sunday women
were faipting wholesale in the churches. Indeed,
it seems as if the art of fainting, gone into disuse
lately, was about to be revived.
The wife of a leading doctor wilted and panted,
and wondered could this be death—this utter inab-
ility to move a finger. Her husband came into
the room and found her mentally making a long
farewell. He looked at her critically for a moment,
then crash went all her dreams. “My dear,
you’re in perfect health,” he said brutally. And
so she was, but he shipped her to the mountains
next day, all the same, out of the way of March’s
nasty little ways.
‘ Djin-Djin,” revived at Her Majesty’s on
Saturday, had what is left of Sydney in its dress-
circle. Mrs. Philip Morton in black with a low
square yoke of black net, was with Miss Irene
..larks in white and elbow sleeves and purple
flower over one eyebrow. Mrs. Lassetter was in
white, covered with black net. Nobody has the
courage to wear aught but black or white yet, but
the weather keeps this quiet dressing in counten-
ance, and we are thankful to be saved anything
hot to look at.
It was a sweltering night, so people needn’t
have gasped so at two pretty society women in the
front of the d.c., waited on by a squire, and
sipping something out of glasses in the interval.
It was a most remarkable first night. Everyone
knew his lines backward. Indeed, the co. seemed
more at home in the play that was just put on
than in the play that had just been taken off.
Tnis was because the Pollards played “Djin-
Djin ” for three years, and fitted into their old
parts uncreasingly.
Maud Beatty filled Flo Young’s old part—or
part of it—as Prince Eucalyptus, and flaunted a
floppy scarlet silk blouse with buff tights. lam
glad to say. Maud doesn’t eat her words
as much as in the past. She gets a chance
to look handsome and well-built, especially in a
black satin suit heavy with silver. Tiny Gwennie
Walker roused an encore out of the drowsy house.
So-and-So and Such-and-Such ” made as great a
hit as ever. All the ballets were well-frocked, if
you can speak of what the ballet wears as a frock,
and neither the scenery, nor the dressing, nor the
dress-circle had the-tired look one so often en-
counters at revivals.
Saturday saw the ena 6f Grand Opera. After
the curtain fell on the last act of “ Tannhaiiser,”
the insistent recalls brought Elizabeth from her
bier. There stood a queer quartette! Louis
Arens in Tannhaiiser’s rags, Cecile Lorraine in
Elizabeth’s funeral robe, Miss Crichton behind a
huge bunch of white roses in Venus’s filmy
clinging draperies, and in the middle of the group
Herr Slapoffski, stepped up from the orchestra,
wearing a black dress-suit and a bald patch.
Slapoffski spoke on behalf of Musgrove, and
congratulated Sydney on its warm appreciation
of good music. Then the quartette took arms
and pranced about to left and right like frisky
schoolboys till the curtain fell.
Sydney is hugging itself now, I can tell you.
It has found that it not only admires but loves—
even to the extent of seven-and-six—Wagner’s
operas. “ Lohengrin ” and “ Tannhaiiser ” were
the successes of the season, along with “Carmen.”
But we don’t say much about “ Carmen,” because
anyone can like Bizet, whereas Wagner takes
something like genius to appreciate him, we are
told.
Unfortunately for Sydney, preening itself on
its deepness, “Lohengrin” and “Tannhaiiser”
are just the very ABC of Wagner. Wait till
another group of Wagner operas are offered. But
no. I believe Sydney will love them too—or say
so. “Tannhaiiser” was really magnificently
staged. Such a gorgeousness of costume has
never been seen in Sydney before., In the won-
derful Second Act, when the audience assembles
in the Hall of Song, it seemed as if the wealth of
detail would never end. Mantle after mantle
came sweeping past, each more luxurious than
the other, and all exquisitely artistic. The color-
schemes in the grouping of this great act took
one’s breath away. There was scarcely a color
that was not introduced into some man or
woman’s raiment, but so carefully was that audi-
ence stage-martialled into its seats, so aesthetically
were the right people put next to the right people,
that the effect was the greatest triumph of stage-
shading ever achieved for a Sydney audience.
There was one flaw in the dressing of Mus-
prove’s operas, however, which arose from the
inability of a tenor to kneel to a certain contralto.
He suggested to her, “Why don’t you have a
couch in, and lie on that instead of on the straw?”
She indignantly refused to introduce a couch into
a prison cell. “Well then,” said he, “I’ll ’ave
to ’ave ray close let out at the knees.” It seems
they were so tight that he couldn’t assume the
correct attitude for “Home to our Mountains,”
and he thought if the contralto would seat herself
on a couch he might stand over her instead of
going on his tight knees on the straw.

If anybody were giddy enough to offer a prize
for the most original Mourning, I would like to
recommend a man in William-street, who set out
his window with yards and yards of black sausage
when the Queen died. He also put away all the
festive red cabbage, the jolly red German sausage,
the white and scarlet ham, and the block of bright
worldly-looking corn beef, and gave his clients
nothing to look at that wasn’t pure lugubrious
black. A windowful of black food doesn’t look
like grief when you come to write about it, but I
suppose it s a case of the shoemaker to his last, and
the sausage man to his black-pudding, and every-
body making mournful so as to best advertise his
own particular business.
After that I would recommend a local public
school, that also combined refreshments with
grief. Ihe children there were asked by their
teachers to put on some sign of mourning, a black
ribbon, black bow, anything to indicate their
awful woe at the death of Queen Victoria.
Next day a whole class came to morning-school
wearing simple little, black, stiff bows and ros-
ettes. These seemed to be made of innocent black
beby ribbon. But as the day wore on teacher
noticed that the mourning seemed to be gradually
disappearing. By afternoon school-time scarcely
a bow or rosette remained. At last a school-marm
caught a little girl in the act of biting a junk out
of the middle of her rosette, and then the sorrow-
ful truth came out. Which was that the mourn-
ing was made of thin, black liquorice-sticks,
twisted into bows, but rapidly licked out of shape,
and turned into funeral meats as the day wore on
and the grief wore off.
A local paper says that “So eager are the Vic-
torians to join the sixth contingent, that married
men have been declaring themselves to be
bachelors. Yes, but what Sydney is worrying
over is an unmarried society man who goes away
soldiering while still a bachelor.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
The night the Indian troops saw the Panto-
mime they neither smiled nor frowned, but appa-
rently thought a lot. To admirers of veiled
Oriental beauty Nellie Stewart’s decolletage and
hose methods must have seemed—well, extremely
Western,’ to put it mildly.
“ Same evening, the stage-bit most enjoyed by
English Tommies was the scene where two rabid
man-catchers fall in love at first sight with a red-
coated sentry-boy. In real life the older the
woman the worse her attack of ‘ scarlet fever ’
When our own boys came back from S’Africa they
suffered terribly on Welcome Day from the public
embraces of shrivelled charwomen and fat monthly-
nurses. That day the unassuming hero of a hun-
dred street hugs testified that not one of his
assailants was under 60. At last the goaded
youth resolved for the future to name his own
poison, and bailed up the next pretty 16-year-old
he saw for a kiss. A scratched face was all he
g9t, and for the rest of the march he abandoned
himself to the tender mercies of Girls of the Verv
Old Brigade. 3
“ Snazelle’s songs and yarns greatly help out his
pictures. His Sydney celebration scenes fill up
an aching void in the hearts of those who weren’t
in at the Death of the separate ‘ colonies.’ Lord
Hopetoun is there to the very life, with all his
graceful bows and winning ways thick upon him.
Said winsome potentate evidently still preaches
in his own person a sermon on the text that
physique is deceitful and beauty is vain, but grace
and charm do the trick every time.
“At the pantomime, Cinderella’s sisters bur-
lesque various dress fads of the moment. Off to
the Prince s ball, the fat, red-headed one shows
how elephantine the average woman looks in the
prevalent overall pelisse of floral brocade. Same
gusher sports an enormous gold heart pendant like
those affected by our Smartest. The inscriptions
on said dangling trinkets are a study in them-
selves. A popular society widow airs the date of
hubby s death on hers, and the pathetic souvenir
may be seen whirling on its chain as the owner
waltzes !

Brownlow made things hum. Plenty of fire in

of JN avarre, ’ but since then he’s made many attrac-

Cecil Engleheart and Co., tired of being quizzed
for blurting out ‘Henri ’ as ‘ Ong-ree,’ now try to
s bP ( over the asses bridge with a vague murmur
of ‘Arnry.’ As the pretty milk-walker, the
Stewart favored the shortest of skirts, smart tan
shoes and hose, pale blue frock decorated with
raised pink roses, white chemisette, golden wig
and rustic hat piled with pale blue bows. Her
ball-gown of tinselled white was ruched with car-
nation pink; and when she left her uneasy bed in

flirtatious host, she wore filmy white over pink (to
represent the wearer’s skin)' and pink foot and

cause she always produces her flesh-effects by
such rosy methods. As the Panto. Prince in

veals legs and arms fleshed in warm-blush silk.
But what bridegroom wouldn’t blush who found

metallic helmet and under-vest!
Grace Palotta has indulged in a new gown
for her earlier Florodora scenes. It is pink like
its predecessor, with three tiny skirt frills edged
with black, and a suspicion of black baby-ribbon
cropping up m the wide, turned-down collar and
transparent white vest. No throat - band for
Grace s round, white neck is a string to her bow.
She still clings to her big pink hat turned up in
front with pink and red clustered under its brim
and permits only tantalisingly brief glimpses of
her black hose and shoes. Williamson’s dainty
twmkler is twice as popular with women, anyway
as the star of the Musgrove galaxy. When
Nellie is giving her services gratis at a benefit,
petticoated patronesses condescendingly sum her
up as ‘a wonderful woman,’ while they gush over
the Palotta as ‘lovely,’ and ‘sweet,’ and ‘so
natural.’
“Among the audience on Friday were the
Dartos—Aida looking young, blithe, and very
Irenchy of figure in all-pink. Hubby’s face is
handsome, but he’s too short-legged for perfection,
lhey are inseparable companions, partly because
they don t speak English, and Melbournians are
such duffers at foreign languages that unless
Aida chatted to Darto ’ and vice versa, their
poor tongues would grow rusty with disuse. Their
companion at the matinee was Nellie Stewart’s
little daughter.
“That burlesque, ‘The Dart Ohs,’ by Lauri
and Carrie Moore, didn’t seem quite so laughable
to the Originals as to outsiders. .The caricatured
couple managed to smile and applaud, but poor
M sieu s mirth looked as hollow as that of a small
boy at a juvenile party when a facetious hostess
suddenly withdraws the tit-bits of the feast just
when she starts to hand them to him and remarks
archly, ‘ Tommy doesn’t like cream puffs ! ’
“ The two best-dressed matrons at the Darrell
matinee were Mrs. Lawson in a white foamy

black garnishing. Mrs. Norman Menzies fluttered
in. and out of the manager’s box in a white frock
with transparent neck and sleeves. Just beyond
tho box sat Grace Palotta (early in the afternoon)
in a black grenadine frilly gown and a sweeping
picture hat. In the d.c. at the matinee very little
black was worn—quite a blaze of Eastern colors
carried the day.

phatically she has no wish to return to the stage
that domestic bliss has spoilt for her all the old
