" Key. Father Robinson, the guide and philo-
sophic friend of Amy Castles, has begun booming
the voice of a younger sister—Dolly—whom the
earnest, clerical showman describes as much
cleverer all round than the Amy we were formerly
told to adore. He had a host of theatricals sing-
ing a Mass recently at his little Camberwell
church, with Dolly as soloist. Of course the
theatricals (whom he entertained right royally)
agreed with the pastor as to the qualities of
Dolly s voice, and Rev. George was immensely
elated. The Melb. paying public heard this new
(alleged) prodigy at Sophie Osmond’s benefit last
year, when the poor child was a ridiculous disap-
pointment.
“ Judging from the Frankfort press notices of
his first piano-forte recital Percy Grainger (now
18) remains true to the teaching of his first tutor,
Herr Pabst, who introduced the golden-haired
cherub to Melbourne notice some seven or eight
years ago. The Pabst pupils mostly hit the piano
with measured beat and slow. They were effec-
tive, but cold. Pabst exhorted them to dwell on
the importance of every note, and the star
youngster, Percy, took his master, his music and
himself with all possible seriousness. When a
woman at the Exhibition Building once asked him
if he didn’t find piano-playing very hard, he re-
buked the thoughtless creature with the
reply that ‘ Everything is hard if you do it
properly.’ Like boy, like man. The Frankfort
General-Anzeiger says : ‘ Herr Percy Grainger
proved himself a player of great technical merits,
whose style is noble rather than emotional.’
1 hat’s the golden-haired nipper grown into a
Herr. By the way, Percy promised to be always
a pretty fellow, but the notices don’t mention
whether he is calculated to fetch the girls other-
wise than with the features of his performance.
German critics are never worldly and flippant.
“A fair charmer denies (to candid inquirers)
the truth of her reported engagement to that lean
and gilded Sydneyite. The popular belief is that
the beauty is waiting for the May consignment of
soft goods from ’Ome.
“ The marriage has just been announced of Mr.
Fred. M'Evoy, the wealthy N.S.W. squatter and
familiar Collins-st. figure, to Miss Violet Coral
Healy—better known as Violet Daniels of the
Princess Co. The wedding took place quietly in
Carlton some months ago, though the notice has
only just appeared. Mr. M‘Evoy was married
many years previously to the beautiful and
brilliant daughter of the veteran Dr. Crooke, then
of Melbourne, now of Sydney—a brave old man,
who at 90 years still practises his profession. That
lady died about three years ago, and a very pretty
girl, in the person of the new Mrs. M‘Coy, suc-
ceeds to the gorgeously-furnished homestead and
other luxuries at Tarrabandra, Gundagai.
“ Apropos a recent wedding. It is noteworthy
that the gilded bridegroom induced his first wife
to become a Catholic, and the ceremony took place
with 6clat at St. Patrick’s Cathedral. But in the
second instance things were reversed, and he
yielded to the faith of his Protestant bride.
“Mrs. Marina Murphy—the ardent supporter
of Suffrage, and the only Sassiety leader to plague
her friends about Woman’s Vote—has just now
the three daughters of the Dowager Countess of
Seafield in tow, and all that remains of the Naice
crowd in the city during the hot season are paying
their devoirs very respectfully to the three wild-
eyed, pale-faced, auburn ladies. The elder, Lady
Caroline, only is ‘ out ’; the other two are in knee-
length frocks, and wear their Titian tresses in
straight sea-weed simplicity. All go in deep half-
mourning for the late Queen —no jewellery, very
despondent dress, and they only wake to en-
thusiasm when they begin to talk Maoriland,
which has been their home for years. They love
the Maoris because they’re ‘so awfully quaint,’
and Papa Seddon because he is ‘so very good to
them.’
“Gushing folk are surprised that the sacred
pen used for signing the Oath of Allegiance was
only a very common every-day one. ‘ Why didn’t
you procure something paiticularly fine?’ asked
someone of ex-Premier Turner. He turned his
quiet eyes on the speaker and said in his simple
fashion, ‘I didn’t think.’ He seldom does, you
know. Someone else suggested (when it was too
late) that each of the five officials signing the
document should have used a different quill, so
that in each of the five families the precious instru-
ment might have been preserved. But the Premier
‘didn’t think’ of that either. However, five
duplicates of the document are to be reproduced
on vellum, signed by the five in each case, and
kept as a souvenir of the ceremony.
“Surgeon O’Hara’s handsome son Clive
‘Clive the Conjuror’—has gone to London.
He wants to see everything of the very
latest in the magic and illusion line ; to get ideas
from which he can get new ideas of his own, and to
form a style somewhat different from Dante’s, but
equally attractive. The handy young Adonis has
all Dante’s tricks of skill at the tips of his fingers,
as all know who have seen him in public or
private. He is certainly clever enough to shine
as an imitative conjuror, and if he possesses the
inventive faculty Clive will go up top in a profes-
sion where a killing appearance is half the battle
The Toorak girl who called him a dream was
speaking for a good many of them.
“ A leading merchant’s family had another way
of amusing themselves at the sea-side. The girls
donned very frilly bathing-gowns, mounted their
horses (unsaddled) and cantered along the beach
to the amazement of all the sand-diggers. Then
they dashed into the sea, and drove their steeds
through the dancing waves, while the watchers
professed to be thankful that for a few moments
at least the sea had hidden the straddled
extremities of the tomboys from view.
“ The prize daughter of Pooh Bah takes things
into her own dimpled hands and gives swaggah
luncheons at Menzies’ every now and then. Said
that a chaperon (in loco parentis) was suggested
for the last occasion—a few days ago. But the
plump and level-headed charmer put her veto
upon it, proclaiming that chaperons were only
meant for church services and dangerous festivi-
ties. To the pure luncher all things are quite
respectable.
Yours affectionately,

The fruit crop is very good this year, and
the prudent housekeeper is busy looking after the
year’s supply of preserves and filling up shortages
in Jam Jars. There is but little satisfaction in
using a common, badly-fitting, badly-made jar,
which, moreover, is liable to break when the fruit
is poured in. Some new patterns have been intro-
duced this year by M‘Lean Bros, and Bigg, Ltd.,
Melb. and Sydney. Notably the Gilchrist —this is
a large-mouthed jar, with porcelain immerser;
and tne Canton jar, a clear, white glass, with easy-
fitting spring top showing the contents to the best
advantage.*

“Steele Rudd ” (A. H. Davis) has an idea
of appearing as an entertainer. He proposes to
deal out “On Our Selection” —to country audi-
ences for a start.
Longevity evidently runs in the Musgrove
family, for Lewis Lewis, “ Muzzy’s ” grandfather,
lived to the remarkable age of 106, while his
father is still to the fore.
Josiah Boothby, once S.A. Under-Secre-
tary, holds a record in having superintended that
State’s census work for 61 years. He is a brother
of S.A. Sheriff Boothby.
Recalled that Downey, the big London
photographer of years agone, made his business
by securing the only known photograph of Queen
Victoria with a smile on her face.
The Queen never forgave Thackeray for
his “ George 1V.,” or Henry Reeve for publishing
the Greville Memoirs. Reeve treasured up a
snub he had received from Prince Albert.

of convictions to his credit than any other man in
the .Force, and not “drunk and disorderlies”

Among the N.S.W. Federal candidates is
a man who once carried two policemen off Rose-

versary.
Chief - Justice Onslow, who, after a
chequered career, retires from Westralian law
affairs, is grandson of old Alex. Macleay, Colonial
Secretary of N.S.W. in the early part of the
century.

the other day that there had only been three great
fenerals in the world—Julius Caesar, Napoleon
lonaparte, and Lord Roberts. Did Roberts come
from Ireland too ?

The comic element obtrudes itself gro-
tesquely in N.S.W. Education Department. The
Education Minister, who is a brewery shareholder
and a jolly good fellow to boot, has signed a lot of
cards cursing drink, for the walls of the State
schools.
During sculptor Mackennal’s visit to Bal-
larat local potentates revived the idea of a statue
for Lindsay Gordon, “who rode some famous
races on the'Dowling Forest course.” The illu-
minati of Ballarat remember the rider, not the
writer.
No rose without a thorn. Federation has
brought down from N.Q. that awful person W.
S. Dowel, who fills in time pegging out the coun-
try, writing stodgier letters than even “ Wingen”
Abbott, and announcing his candidature for vari-
ous things.
Kingston has given even his most intimate
triends in Adelaide to understand that it is useless
attempting to secure his influence in the aspira-
tion after Federal billets. “ I know nothing about
appointments,” he says—“they’re not in my
department.”
At the Sydney Jewish Memorial Services
for the Queen the chief officers of the Synagogue
assisted Rabbi Davis. These were the president
(Mr. George Judah Cohen), the vice-president (Mr.
Justice Cohen), and the treasurer (Mr. Neville
Cohen). Mighty is the mana of Cohen.
Obit. : At Cooma, Mr. Robert Dawson in
his 89th year. He came to N.S. Wales with Gov.
Gipps in 1838, since when he has been a Monaro
pastoralist. He entered the Government service
m 1847 as Police Magistrate, and retired in 1887.
He was father of the Sydney City solicitor, Mr.
Percy Dawson.
Albert Edward is calling himself King
Edward for the same reason that the man whose
name was Nicholas called his son John, because
he (the father) wasn’t going to be called Old Nick
in the future. Fancy having to live up to the
excellence of Albert the Good! Royal Bertie
knows better.
Ben Bridge, the doughty ex-horse-snarer
—lecturing in Inverell district (N.S.W.) —has been
spreading himself hugely over the story of his
life and adventures. “In crossing the desert,”
incidentally observed the veracious Bridge, “I
had a few goes-in with the blacks, but got off
best. Upon one occasion I put my coat and hat
up, and in the morning the coat had seven spears
through it.” Only seven !
W. Y. Robinson, Clerk of Vie. Leg.
Assembly, in a recent lecture said that for sus-
tained oratory Higinbotham has been the greatest
speaker in the Victorian Parliament, and that the
ex-Chief-Justice’s highest oratorical flights were
made in connection with the Darling Grant,
Many old Parliamentarians—including Berry,
J. M. Grant, M'Kean, and David Blair —have
told the present scribe the same thing.
Professor Woodhouse, who takes the Greek
Chair in Sydney University, is a notable man.
He did a lot of work in Greece, apart from mere
Greek literature. Some of these first-hand experi-
ences he has recorded in a volume. Otherwise,
he is an unpretentious, quiet, scholarly man, with
a “no-occupation” appearance.
David Sy rue’s strenuous efforts to drum up
a sensational “shilling” fund to raise a Melb.
statue of Queen Victoria has not caught on with
dramatic suddenness. The fund went very
limpy, and at the end of the first two weeks only
about £llOO had been collected, nearly the whole
of which was contributed in £lOO blocks by six or
seven wealthy firms.
Waxcellency Madden’s real weakness is an
unbounded belief in his literary abilities. He
believes he would have shone as a man of letters
had he taken to literature instead of to law, and
he rejoices in great events mainly because of the
opportunities they give him for discharging large
masses of turgid English, composed in sweat and
pain, at the heads of defenceless crowds.
“ Smiler ” Hales floats on to the lecture-
platform atop of the wave of success of his Cam-
pain Pictures,” as he calls them, in a letter to a
friend. “Smiler” always did spell beautifully.
But he, anyhow, won’t blush and stammer ; for it
isn’t quite a new game. And his voice is as
strong as his imagination. In fact, lie made
arrangements once to go a-touring as an elocu-
tionist.
Miss Mackay, at 72, was buried last week
at A .\tverley. Everybody knew her talented,
saw-voiced brother, old Angus Mackay, of Mel-
bourne side, who founded Sydney Daily lele-
graph, and seemed to live for newspaper enter-
prise. His instinct was right in starting the D. 1.
but his means were foolishly inadequate, xet he
held on. A peculiar thing is that nearly every
man who worked for the politics originally out-
lined by Angus Mackay is now either dead or
laboring in a hostile camp.

You get a good idea of what is behind the
reputation of Kingston as “a strong man” when
you watch him going laboriously from State to
State, even to far-off Perth and isolated Hobart,
grafting day and night over the details of the local
Custom-houses and their business, and sizing-up
every leading officer. Kingston is a sticker
because he doesn’t put his foot down very often
until he has well tried the place with his black-
thorn.
The rumored settlement of all the diffi-
culties between Williamson and Musgrove gains
strength by the announcement of the withdrawal
of the iegai proceedings instituted a year or two
back. Furthermore, Nellie Stewart (of the one
company) and Brownlow (of the other) acted
together as principals in their old parts in “Ma
Mip. Rosette ” at Darrell’s benefit. Indications
are that the old firm will sooner or later be boom-
ing along in the old way.
An old-age-pension applicant at Bruns-
wick, Vic., claimed to have been worth far over
£1,000,000 fifteen years ago when he was a
squatter. The proceedings of the Court are, of
course, absolutely secret, but the investigating
magistrate stated afterwards that the old mm
submitted strong evidence that he spoke the truth.
Who knows how many of the rich men of to-day,
who rail against such pensions, may be applying
for pensions themselves in another 15 years? *
Holder, when he returned from Sydney,
said in his wrath : “If I had pushed my claims to
office as some others did I might have been a
Federal Minister.” Whereupon Kingston wired
asking whether, seeing that the question was be-
tween him and Holder, the meaning was that he
had pushed any claims. Holder responded that
his remark “had no personal application.”
Kingston now wonders what on earth the astute
Premier meant if he .didn’t desire to be personal
to some one.

What price Crick, K.C. ! It is rumored
that if the N.S. W. Cabinet is reconstructed Crick
will take the Attorney-Generalship. Wise will
go into Federal politics—-if he can get there via
Canoblas. Crick is entitled, as a solicitor of more
than five years’ standing, to go to the Bar when-
ever he likes to apply. He could then, as Attor-
ney-General, take silk as soon as he liked and
with the K.C. almost an extinct animal in N.S. W.
owing to the reluctance of leading juniors to take
silk, Crick ought to get a very big practice indeed.
Died at Geelong, on 3rd Feb., Denis
O’Brien, who first handled the original consign-
ment of sparrows sent out from England to the
late James Austin. O’Brien offered a guinea
apiece for them with a view to wringing their
necks. Austin indignantly refused, the result
being that many Victorian farmers would since
have given numerous guineas for the privilege of
wringing Austin’s neck. A coincidence was that
deliverv of this consignment was accepted by one
E. R. " Sparrow, then a clerk in the office of
Austin’s Geelong agents.
“It does not appear to be generally
known,” writes a Sydney D.T. correspondent,
“that it may be many years before we have
another ‘ Prince of Wales.’ The title can only be
given to the eldest son of the reigning monarch,
so that the Duke of York, who was not born while
his father was reigning, cannot take the title. For
the same reason little Edward of York can never
be Prince of Wales, and unless he comes to the
Throne before his son is born, there will be very
few people alive now who will hear the title
again.” But now it is cabled that York is to be
p. of W. —after he visits Australia.
It is general gossip in Melbourne that
there is an estrangement between Kingston and
Holder. Apropos, Kingston has fewer enemies
on hand just now than at any previous period of
his career. Ever since Way, C.J., foolishly
intrigued re Privy Council Appeal matters there
has been a coldness between that ambitious little
potentate and Kingston, but they met in Sydney
only recently, and more or less made it up. And
two other old-time enemies, Baker and Symon,
have buried the hatchet with the ex-S.A. Pre-
mier. “ Bedad,” said a West Adelaide supporter
of Kingston’s recently, “divil a bit do I knaw
pwhat’s cornin’ over Charlie lately ! ”
Reid, K.C., is going into Federal politics
and consequent poverty, just as he was really
beginning to get on at the N.S.W. Bar. Re the
proposed fund “to make him independent.” The
same phrase was used with regard to Dibbs when
he was in low water, and at the time when he was
a favorite with the “commercial classes.” At
that time Dibbs had done those classes signal ser-
vice by his bank-panic legislation—yet the “ inde-
pendence ” move only panned out about £3500.
It is hard to believe that Reid, who clapped an in-
come-tax on the “commercial classes” will get
much help when the hat is being passed round.
The popular and clever “Jack” Wagner,
son of recently-deceased squatter John Wagner,
wealthy retired pioneer Vic. coach-proprietor,
was educated at Oxford, and became a barrister.
His father wished him to make a name at the
Sydney bar when he came out from England about
seven years ago, and started him under the rngis
of Jack Want; but the young stager was suffer-
ing from an unlucky love-affair, he had acquired a
taste for theatricals at Oxford, and he could not
settle down to the dull business of the law. The
stern parent was wroth accordingly, and practi-
cally disowned him. The youngster then started
off for New York, where he began writing lyrics
for the operatic stage, and he is now literary
editor of New York Sun. It is to be hoped that
the amiable youth has not been disinherited.

Concerning H. B. Higgins :
Dear Bulletin, —Notwithstanding “ MacDonald’s ” gibe
(B. 2/2/’l), plucky, brainy Higgins is one of whom true
democracy should be proud. Also, I fail to see that Hig-
gins’s attitude in the Marshall Hall case involves any
reflection on his democratic principles. This scribe has
occasionally had reason to differ from Higgins, notably
in connection with the Federal movement, but cannot
understand the pettiness which induces such a line of
argument as “MacDonald’s.” “MacD.’s” allusion to
Higgins’s status in Vie. Parliament is an actual compli-
ment, because to such a depth has Vic. political life
descended in its time-serving and truckling to the clamor
of the hour that H.’s non-conformity sets him high above
the groundlings of his Parliamentary surroundings. Why,
measured by “ MacDonald’s ” standard, the political life
of the great Higinbotham would also be termed a
failure. Long may Australia continue to produce such
men—men who will fearlessly and unswervingly stand up
for what they believe to be the cause of truth and justice.
—Another Mac.

Aboriginalities.
Letter recently published in Cairns
(Queensland) Daily Argus from a lady who
wished to thank a policeman for kindly assaulting
her husband:—
®* r > I n reference to the paragraph in last Friday’s Post
wherein Constable Maher is charged with a “ cowardly
assault ” on an old man, I shall feel obliged if you will
publish the following true facts of the case. My husband,
who is not by any means an “ old man,” unless you call
39 years old, was the victim of the alleged
assault, which Constable Maher committed on
him at my request. My husband had struck
me with his fists on three different occasions on
that day, in consequence of which I was compelled to
appeal tq the police for protection. My husband richly
deserved the thrashing administered by Constable Maher,
which did him a lot of good. Constable Maher only gave
my husband four cuts with a cane across the legs ; he did
not touch him on the stomach. If there is any enquiry,
I am perfectly willing to come forward and substantiate
my statements, as also will many others who knew the
true facts of the case.—Yours, &c., Mrs. .
There is something patriarchal about the form of
Government under which a lady can ask a police-
man to wallop her husband on a racecourse or other
public place, and the old man is walloped forth-
with.

“Karri” : When our C. of E. (and only)
parson is absent the local policeman acts as his
locum tenens. The said reverend bobby was
struggling hard with the service t’other Sunday
evening, when above the stentorian sound of his
murdered English distinctly was heard the cry of
“ Murder ! Police ! ” Promptly the celebrant
dived through the vestry-door, and the audi-
ence, to the last dog, jumped out of the main exit
—when, by the pale moonlight, we watched a
short but lively struggle between the surpliced
bobby-parson and a whiskeyed sleeper-hewer. The
sober man prevailed, and men, vestments and all,
retired to the little stone cabin round the corner
Ten minutes later the sainted trap returned and
finished the service. Solemn fact !

The proprietor of a West Victorian
paper has hit upon a novel method of retaining
his subscribers, When an angry sub. writes
withdrawing his support the prop pastes the
letter on the front-office window, with a sarcastic
comment of his own below it As the average
country subscriber” is usua ly a bit shaky both
in spelling and caligraphy the remedy is very
effective, especially when the alternative is a per-
sonal notme to the irate newspaper man, who is
a bit handy with his fists when necessary.

“Dave’ : The free, unfettered life en-
joyed by the Australian selector was never made
clear to me until one dav, empty-stomached, I
trudged into Bill Bent’s place. Bill’s wife and
six kids were seated in a row on the grass grinning
hard. The wife, when aware of my approach,
held up a long, brown finger. I halted and stared,
There was Bill crawling stealthily over the ground
towards a big tree at the foot of which about six
inches of a goanna’s tail were visible. The family
became unmanageable, and as Bill got within
grabbing distance, Mrs. Bill had to hold the lot
down. When Bill did grab you should have heard
the shine! After Bill had tired of “running”
Mrs. Bill (who wasn’t at all in a fit state to be
chased) and the kids with the goanna he chained
the vermin to the verandah-post, and getting
down before it on his stomach, just out of range,
spent a pleasant afternoon “seeing it’’—every-

Near St. George last year one'who had
been tramping “through hell across the plain”
looked up and saw a brother dangling f om a tiee
by a bridle-rein. He went pale, and hastened to
the township. Days after, when he read of the
police finding £97 m notes on the body, he turned
pale-green. “Every man’s chance comes right
enough,” he said ; “ that was mine. Damned if I
don t feel like kickin’ meself ! ”
“ Via ” : If “ Vale ” (B. 26/l/’l) ever gets
down a mine about 2 a.m. he will understand
Stevenson’s idea. Stones drop without apparent
cause, weird crackings are heard, and there seems
to be a tremor as if the earth was turning in its
sleep. Ask cattle-men what is the worst hour on
a cattle-camp, when the devils seem to stir the
brutes to stampede and “ring” without known
cause, and the same hour will be mentioned by
the great majority of them. The movement is on
right enough—but the cause ?

Tommy Tanna the spieler is by no means
the latest Kanaka development. Up North they
now have the full-blooded Kanaka girl who talks
shyly about being engaged to “ A good Christian
boy,” and shows her three-guinea engagement-
ring. One of these dusky beauties was married
the other day (by a C.E. clergyman) in white silk,
orange blossoms, and lace. At the wedding-feast
the infant son of the bride by her own uncle sat
on his grandmother’s lap and viewed the proceed-
ings with evident approval. Yes ! the North is
on the high road to—something or other.

“T. 0.” : Happened to be loafing for grass
along the north bank of the Murrumbidgee in the
summer of 80-1, when late Rev. J. B. Gribble was
mustering Riverina for blackfellows, and drafting
them into the old Coolibah homestead, which he
had characteristically commandeered for that
purpose. One day when the good man was out
foraying the stations for tucker, two bullock-
drivers on the south bank enticed a couple of
lubras to swim the river; and the landlord of an
adjacent pub., with an imprudence equal to his
callousness, supplied liquid refreshment to these
sons of Belial and their guests. (I was on the
northern bank, remember, and the river be
tween me and the liquor.) However, Gribble
returned, and ,his flock, like the servants
in the parable, came and told their lord all
that was done. Whereupon, Gribble’s buggy
merely touched the tops of the claybanks as he
careered down to the punt and up the other side
of the river to the bullock-drivers’ camp. Those
vessels of wrath, now penitent and apologetic,
hoisted their somnolent wild-flowers into the
buggy, and the apostle scorched back to his
Mission, where he immediately administered a
powerful emetic in each case. Afterwards he
vehemently and successfully pressed a charge
against the publican. But the idea of the
emetic was what fetched us; it was so like
the man as we knew him.

Four men struck a station, and one
up with a pillow-slip for some flour.
Got a pannikin-full; eyed critically the
small lump in a distant corner of the bag ;
then, turning to the storekeeper, inquired,
“Say, boss, is this to be taken before or after
meals ? ”

Maori, Wiremu?” Maori (confidentially): “No
plurry good. Me try whole year teachem Maori
—no move. Swear at him in pakeha—go like
h—!”

“ G.C.E.” : In yelling for Mes-
ton’s scalp because he says he saw lyre-
birds 600 miles west of Brisbane,
M'Cooey and Co. only yell because
Meston has seen more than they have.
Eruson’s “Australian Birds” (a recognised
authority) distinctly says that lyre-birds are
found in Queensland as high as latitude 26,
Brisbane being ljdeg. further south.

One George Barnes recently brought into
Quirindi (N.S.W.) some bones, apparently of a
mammoth herbivorous animal. The remains are
in good preservation, the enamel on the teeth
being perfect. The incisors are curved, with a
chisel edge, and one is sin. long by 3in. in circum-
ference.' Two immense molars remain in the huge
jaw-bone, and one of the tusks is 14in. long. The
remains were found at a depth of 35ft. in sinking
a well on Colley Station.
* * * *
Decided recently by a conference of stock-
board delegates at Muswellbrook (N.S.W.) that
the bonus on rabbit-scalps only leads to the in-
crease of the pest. This, if true, would hold good
with reference to the bonuses on the scalps of
wallabies, dingoes, etc. But if the English de-
mand for rabbit keeps on growing the rabbit
will probably be “stamped out” by the multipli-
cation of canning and refrigerating establish-
ments all over the continent.
The statement that last year 17,000 tons of Aus-
tralian rabbits (equal to 600,000 sheep) were sold
in England raises the question as to whether it
would not pay in some of the arid districts of the
West to breed rabbits rather than sheep. The
rabbit-returns would at all events be the more
reliable.

“ Gilaomi” : One day met a N.S. W. farmer
who lived five miles from nearest township, his
face beaming with pride because he was taking
his 15-year-old daughter to see said hamlet. “ She
never bin off the farm afore,” he volunteered—-
“ never seen a town.” Paused another day on a
mountain-spur to drink in the magnificence of the
landscape. Young man coming along stopped
beside me and observed, “Well, isn’t this a’ole
for you to be in ? No people—nothin’ to look at !
A town— that’s the place ! I saw one once-
passed through it with the bullock-dray.”

* * *
“AND THE SHOUTING”
(Job xxxix. , 25).
WITH INCIDENTAL NOTES AND
EXPLANATIONS.
Dearly beloved, —Mankind may
be divided into two classes —the
shouters and the shoutees. I
purpose to deal, on this occasion,
with the former class only. We
can classify their efforts thus
The Shout Positive : “ Going
be dashed ! We’ll have another
first.”
The Shout Imperative:
“You’ve all got to have one
with me ; no blanky backing out
now.” (Note : Though hardly
Chesterfieldian, the tone of this
invitation is seldom resented.)
The Shout Dubious : “ Well, if
you chaps care for another .”
Usually they don’t —in the
circs. (Note: To be used with
discretion. Some men are quite
destitute of the finer feelings, and
would reply, “By Jingo, don’t we ! )
The Bummer’s Shout: Asking a man to have a drink
when you know the publican won’t “stand it.” Your
victim will generally shout himself, if you select him with
discrimination.
The Shout Tentative : Interviewing the landlord before-
hand in order to see if he will stand it.
The Shout Wicked : Shouting soft stuff for a teetotaller
when fellow, human beings, a few yards away, are yearn-
ing for a true Christian who will take them into the wet.
The Shout Wasteful: Shouting for the publican without
an object. , __ _
The Shout Hesitant: “Up to me to shout? Why I
stood a blanky round only ’arf anour agow ! ” Chorus of
ornamental negatives. “ But, blime, I’m stiff as a crutch,
I tells yer. Of all the spongin’ crowds as ever I see- O,
W ell, if ! ” (Note, common among lower-grade
spielers.)
The Shout Commercial : Standing a man a drink for
business purposes. (Note. —Young travellers for tea,
tailors, machines, etc., must be cautious. In every coun-
try town are men who shepherd agents for booze.
“ Represent So-and-So, Tailors, do yer ! Why, bless me,
I sez to the missus on’y last night, ‘Why,’lsez, ‘if I
mustn’t send to Sydney for some more clo’es,’ I sez ; ‘ and
she sez,’ etc. An’ I’m a man as c’ud put a bit in yer way
now,’ and so on.)
The Publican’s Shout: “Flinging a sprat to catch a
mackerel.” (Note.—Almost obsolete : mackerel are very
scarce nowadays.)
The Shout Slinking : When a man withdraws another
by nudge or wink, from the crowd. Feebly discussing
dog outside pub.—say on Sunday morning. They saunter
round the back way. Crowd listens to jingle of bottles,
or gasp of beer-pump, and then spit with difficulty and
desire to be struck pink and wonder what the blanky
country’s coming to. The few solvent ones dare not pro-
pose “ a shilling in,” because the pool wouldn’t stand the
dead-weight.
The Shout Calculated : Shouting for a man who has a
cheque to melt, and being ever on hand afterwards when
he goes in for a drink. (Note. —Must be managed with
skill. When he asks you the fifth time running, make a
feeble show of resistance—“ Oh, you’re too blanky strong,”
etc.) Then to show what a reckless, generous devil he
is, he’ll shout half-a-dozen times more during the
evening. Also warn him against other bummers ; the
longer his money lasts, the longer you’ll get free booze.
And if you try to tap him for stuff, let it be early in the
acquaintance—while you are still a good fellow, and
before he has begun to calculate how much longer he will
reign.)
' The Shout General“ Come on, you chaps !—come on,
mate ! —hi, mister, you with the leggin’s, come an’ wet it
alongerme! Fill ’em up all roun’, boss, an’ ’ave one
yerself !” (Note : Quite obsolete in N.S.W., never known
in South Australia, still rampant—even obligatory—in
parts of Queensland.)
Shout Solo :—One on your ace ; in plain* English -
drinking with the flies, or a Jimmy Woodser. Very com-
mon in inside districts. Not positively immoral—unless
practised by others. In your own case, permissible if (a)
you’ve only got a sprat. (If you have credit you must
always shout —you have’nt got to stand the racket)
If (bj you’ve been ignored in a shout, (c) If you’ve just
shouted for the other man and he hasn’t returned it.
(d) If you’re followed by another one not invited. (Note)
A class of bummer, on seeing you enter the bar, will follow
and deferentially ask your opinion of the Chinese war.
Don’t be bluffed ; give’ it calmly between pulls at your
poison and remark as you put the glass down, “ Not bad
tack that!” In return for your courtesy he’ll give you his
views—on blanky, miserableblankers, probably—together
with brief but direct references to a state of future
punishment. But he won’t shepherd you any more.)
General Remarks. —To those in doubt, don’t shout.
Yet wise shouting is profitable—especially if you take
soft stuff yourself. That gives the impression that you
shouted from good-fellowship, not merely-because you
wanted one yourself. But the man who shouts lavishly is
a fool, and will be so esteemed. In time, he becomes a
shoutee only, and, for that man,life has r'los?-1.
Cecil Poole.
* * * *
Pall Mall Gazette tells a gruesome
Queensland snake-story with positively delightful
patches. A grim, hairy, professional snake-killer
is made to relate how fiis “ master ” killed a ser-
pent on one occasion. Next day a female snake
came and killed the baby in the cradle by way of
vengeance. They buried the baby. “Before
grass had grown to cover the grave my master
was dead himself. We found him by the ti-tree
fence [note local color] swollen and purple. Beside
him lay a dead female snake. . .We sent off the
best rider on the station for aid, but lie only re-
turned ,in time to lay my beautiful missus
beside the others, a baby in her arms that had
never breathed—four lives for one ! When the
day comes that I fall over the edge of the earth
into the snake-pit I shall be food for the hungry
brood, but till then I shall kill all snakes for the
sake of my master, my missus, and the two
babies !” And yet the P.M.G. is a respectable
paper !

“ A.C.H.” : “Be cat-sagacity (B. 29/12/’00).
I used to miss some of .the milk from the
jug, into which the milkman was supposed to put
a pint every morning. Suspecting the cat, a jug
was substituted with a neck too narrow to admit
pussy’s head, but still the milk disappeared. So I
kept watch, and saw the cat come along, dip a paw
into the jug, lick it clean, and repeat the opera-
tion until satisfied.

The threat of the Omata (M.L.) settlers to
run a road round the old toll-gate, which they have
vainly petitioned the County Council to remove,
recalls the rumpus over the oldKaiwarra toll-gates,
about a mile out of Wellington. After vainly re-
questing the removal of the gates, Kaiwarriors
heaved them into the harbor one night, but with
pig-headed obstinacy the gates were replaced, and
two policemen stationed in sentry-boxes to guard
them. But, next morning, the gates were in the
harbor again, and the sentries were found tied
firmly into their boxes. The authorities then
gave in. .
A phenomenon (apparently connected with
the drought) has been noted in several towns of
N.W. Queensland. The crows, which used to
swarm in the localities, have disappeared, while
great numbers of galalis, rosellas, finches, and
even bower-birds have ventured into the streets of
the townships, to the joy of the youngsters, who
amuse themselves by trapping them. Writer
believes this is an absolutely unprecedented occur-
rence in Queensland. *
Country town, in mourning for Queen’s
death. Shops all draped, except one—a coach-
builder’s, outside which a drunken blackfellow is
stretched full length. Passer-by to coachbuilder :
“ You miserable - ! Too mean to buy any
mourning, except shouting a nigger a beer to make
your place look black ! ”
From the Australasian’s recent obituary
notice of the late Ricketson of Baratta :
“ Travelling stock, then as now, gave trouble, but he ”
(Ricketson) “ brought to reason a recalcitrant cow, which
refused to face a stream, and was in danger of stamped-
ing a mob, by seizing her by the tail and swinging her
high off the bank into the water. She swam to the other
side, the rest followed. This feat of strength is still
talked of in the Riverina.”
Just so! The man who can tell a tale, and the
paper that can publish it, about a man swinging
5 or 6 cwt. of cow (Ricketson didn’t deal in “ scal-
lywags ”) “high ” off into a stream do get talked
about—especially in view of the gentle sloping
nature of Riverine crossings. Doubtless Mr.
Ricketson gave the “moo” a couple of turns
around his head before “swinging her high off.”
“ The rest followed ” was a natural sequence.
* * * *
Writes Mr. W. Itodier to The Bulletin :
“ Oobar ” (B. 2/2/’l) asks “ What is Rodier giving us ? ”
Well, lam giving him solid facts. As to there being no
rabbits in the Oobar district, let me tell “ C.” that there
are still bunnies enough in the district to breed-up and
eat out the runs should we get 2 or 3 good seasons follow-
ing. Besides, “ C.” should remember that there are other
parts of the State rabbit-infested, and that Tub .Bulletin
goes everywhere. “ C.L.” bears excellent testimony to
the value of my plan, but errs in supposing that it is not
applicable to large areas. That is just where it suits best.
In fact, if fairly tried it will be found the only plan suit-
able to large areas of poor land. As the real cause of the
rabbit pest is the killing of the bucks (thereby causing
excessive reproduction) it stands to reason that places
which do nothing, and, therefore do not kill the bucks,
don’t contribute to this result. Assume that throughout
the whole of Australia no rabbits whatever were killed,
then Nature would assert herself and the rabbits, in time,
would become equal in sexes and be no menace to the
country. But as it. would take a long time for this to
come about, the better way is to assist Nature kill all
females and ni m lies. The increase of rabbits is directly
attributable to those misguided persons who use gin-traps,
poisoned pollard, &c., and not to those who do nothing,
as that is better than doing wrong. I made this plan
known as far back as ’BB and have ever since followed it
with complete success.
William Morris Hughes, M.L.A. for
Lang (Sydney)
(THE LONGSHOREMAN'S CHAMPION) ,
Is the man who drafted N.S.W. Early Closing
Bill, and received the thanks of both early-closing
associations for being mainly instrumental in
making it law. Mayor Graham said that it was
largely owing to his incisive speech that Premier
Lyne dealt as effectively as he did with bydney
City Council. Mr. Hughes is one of the best
speakers in N.S.W. Parliament. He does not
speak often, but when he does his discourse is
crammed with the meat of fact which gives friend
and opponent much to chew and inwardly digest.
He speaks forcibly and persuasively, and yet,
when occasion demands, can let loose a tongue of
satire which scorches. His speeches on the first
Vote of Censure .on Reid’s Administration moved
by Dibbs, on the Plague, Federation, Earlv
Closing, and Municipal Reform are well worth
reading, and are among the things said in Mac-
quarie-street that have helped to make history.
He is a small, quiet, slightly-deaf man, with a
pleasant unassuming manner that makes one teei
friendly at first sight. He sat on his flower-
shaded verandah and talked as follows :
“No, I’m not an Australian. I’m of Welsh
blood, but was born at Liverpool, Eng., about <56
years ago. Was brought up to school-teaching,
and followed it for years. Came out to Queens-
land in ’B3, and applied for a position under the
Education Department, but the Dept, took so
long to answer that I drifted out-back and became
a bushman. Drifted to Sydney about 85, I think,
and joined the School of Arts and the Balmain
Debating Club. John Norton was a member of
the School of Arts at that time ; so was Holman,
whom I met in a debate —he for the School and I
for Balmain. No; don’t worry about debating-
clubs now. I’ve been reading for the Bar, and
passed my preliminary exam, last November. I m
taking a course of fiction now, as an antidote.
“ I was an active Unionist long.before becoming
a politician. In ’9O, I was a member of Balmain
Labor League, but it was not till ’94, after
lecturing on Labor and Politics. (first as a
private lecturer and then as the emissary of the
Labor Federation) that I became a Parlia-
mentary candidate. I was selected for Cowra,
but had not enough cash to fight that con-
stituency properly, so I suddenly descended
on Sydney and assaulted Lang. The other
candidates were J. D. Fitzgerald, independent
Labor-Protectionist, and John Taylor Free-
trader. We had an exciting time, and when the
dust cleared away I found myself on top, 108
above Taylor and 250 over Fitz. The trouble
with Fitz. was that he would not take the Soli-
darity pledge. Since then I’ve increased my lead
every time ; till now I can proudly say I repre-
sent an absolute majority. .
“Yes, I’m going for the all-Australian Parlia-
ment. The Federal Labor League has selected
me for West Sydney. I have not the slightest
idea who my opponents will be. Our Federal
programme is short and to the point, and will, 1
think, command the support of many who are not
actively associated with Labor. I’m afraid the f
Federal Parliament will have very little to do.
with fixing the hours of labor and levelling-up
wages by means of minimum-wage Acts—that will
mostly beleft to the Stated. Still, allthebigUmons,
such as the maritime unions, wharf labourers and
others are inter-state, and once a labour dispute
gets outside one State the Federal Govt, can in-
tervene with arbitration. And of course an Arbi-
tration Court can fix a minimum wage and limit
the hours of labour in any industry. And any
dispute can be made inter-state if necessary. Still,
it is a roundabout way of doing it, and it seems a
pity we couldn’t settle it all with one comprehen-
sive Act. We hope to have from 20 to 25 mem-
bers in the House of Representatives, and we
shall do our best for our programme every way
we can.
“Our chief plank is, of course,.a White Aus-
tralia. There’s no compromise about that. The
industrious coloured brother has to go and re-
main away ! While on that question, no doubt,
we shall be able to do something to prevent the
influx of European ‘ pauper ’ labour by instituting
educational tests and making it necessary to have
enough money to last a month or two without
work. Also importation of labour under contract
A PUSH SYMPHONY.
WILLIAM MORRIS HUGHES, M.L.A. for
Lang (Sydney).
Photo, by Kerry.
must be prohibited. Then there’s the codification
and amendment of the Banking Laws and the
establishment of an Australian National Bank, to
be run on strictly business, as distinct from politi-
cal, lines. There are other things, plenty of them,
but the great questions are—White Australia,
Old-Age Pensions, a National Bank, and a Demo-
cratic Military System. We shall, at the jump,
tackle the question of making the Old-Age Pen-
sion system apply to the whole continent.
“The military question is one the party will
have to watch closely. It will want careful ad-
justment. This country can’t afford a big stand-
ing-army, and doesn’t want it if it could. The
standing-army means the military caste, alto-
gether antagonistic to democratic practices and
ideals, as seen in the insolence and cruelty of the
German officers to civilians. Citizen soldiers are
cheaper, and the Boer war has proved their
efficiency for defence. The whole population
(male) ought to be trained to arms, every male
between 18 and 21 undergoing three months train-
ing every year, of which six weeks should be con-
tinuous. By 21 he should be a fair soldier and a
respectable shot. After 21 the term might be
shortened so long as he kept his shooting up to
standard. I take it this country doesn’t want an
offensive army, but an armed people who can
shoot straight, and a regimental machine so that
every man can fall automatically into his place no
matter how suddenly the trouble comes. Straight
shooting should be encouraged by assistance to
rifle-clubs and national prizes for good marksman-
ship. Encourage shooting till it becomes the
national sport, as archei’y used to be in England.
“The Senate! Yes, that’s where we shall be
weak, I’m afraid. I don’t expect to see more than
six Laborites in the Senate. The big electorates
make us too dependent on newspapers, and news-
papers have a power of suppression that will
make it hard for us, unless we are included in a
bunch. But, anyhow, we shall be no worse off
than we are in the State Parliaments, and the
Senate will be a lot more democratic than any
State Council has hitherto been. So we shall be
no worse off.
“That’s the illuminated Address the Union
gave me. I’ve been connected with Unionism all
through the piece. Yes ! they seemed to be
pleased with me. We’ve done very well with that
Union. Eighteen months ago it didn’t exist—to-
day it practically includes every wharf-laborer of
Sydney port. We’ve achieved the eight hours,
which we never had before, even before the great
strike, when we are supposed to have had every-
thing. We’ve got -a good bank-balance. The
Union is run on the voluntary system ; all the
officers receiving Is. per year, just to make them
amenable to the law, you know. Oh, yes, our
Union is ‘ a flourishing institution.’
“There is a lot of fun in politics sometimes.
Curious mistakes are made. I remember at one
of my meetings my chairman was late, and excused
himself by saying he’d been ‘making arrange-
ments.’ When the speech was over, a long, gaunt
figure reared up in the third row and repeatedly
hiccuped out—-‘I —hie —wansh ask ye candidate a
queshion.’ Just then several strong men with set
faces commenced to approach the apparition, and
I suddenly grasped the meaning of the chairman’s
‘arrangements.’ These were patriots with whom
he had arranged to keep order by promptly chuck-
ing out anybody whose sudden departure might ap-
pear beneficial. They were getting uncomfortably
near the waving drunk, and I motioned them
back, and the wobbly person went on: “I wansh
ask Mr. Hughes hish opinion about Local Option.’
I jumped up and remarked : ‘ A very reasonable
question, which I have no hesitation about answer-
ing.’ But as I stepped to the edge of the plat-
form the chairman grabbed my coat-tails and
shouted : ‘ Pliwat ? None of yer infernal sec-
tarian business here ! Out wid him, lads ! ’ and I
struggled to my feet just in time to see the
wreck being bumped out with great enthusiasm.

London and Globe: The balance-sheet has
reached Australia. That which is good is clear as day,
that which is bad is dark and puzzling. Securities realised,
and mining properties purchased and re-sold, ±47.M00
about £lBO,OOO more than last year, but small compared
to’9B, when the amount stood at £1,015,962. The chief
and most mysterious item is that under the heading
“ Shares held in sundry companies, £2,332,632, a little
over half-a-million more than last year. No statement
made as to what the shares consisted of. Nothing to
indicate on what basis the valuation was made ; that they
were put down at boom prices there is no doubt. Coming*
events were casting their shadows at the time of the
meeting ; but probably not the biggest “ bear” or most
confirmed pessimist who attended imagined that the tall
a fortnight afterwards would be so awful. By this sheet
the co would seem to be in a solid, sound, and certainly
solvent condition ; the fact of a hurried re-construction
within three weeks, making 2,000,000 paid-up shares con-
tributing to the extent of 55., is proof enough that at the
time the sheet was issued it was rotten to the core, and
that over £2,300,000 valuation was given to shares, the
bulk of which consisted of paper rubbish. No wonder
the auditors slipped in this little phrase : “ Subject to
the sum written-off being adequate in respect of shares
held in various undertakings.”
* » * •
At the Broken Hill mines 7010 men are em-
ployed, as against 6195 a year ago, and 5777 two years
ago'. Add to this 2300 employed at the smelters at Pine,
Adelaide and Newcastle, and 250 at mines off the main
Broken Hill line. The B.H. Prop, now employs 983 men
on surface and 1453 underground, as against 1364 on sur-
face and 980 underground 12 months ago. The Central
(Sulphide Corporation) is the next biggest employer, with
a staff of 870 (these figures refer to the mines only) ; then
Block 14, 796 ; and Block 10, 697.
„ * * *
Many of the members of the Miners’ Association
are not at all well disposed towards the Miners’ Accident
Relief Fund, just established by Act in N.S.W.. The
Miners’ Association offers benefits to some extent similar
to those available under the fund ; and, as membership
of the fund is compulsory (the employer is bound to de-
duct the workman’s contributions from his wages), it is
feared some men will draw out of the association. Most
of the officers frankly admit that it isn’t within the true
province of the modern labor union to pay accident and
funeral allowances ; but they say that this cash connec-
tion is necessary—they can’t keep the membership regu-
lar so long as they have no accident or sick benefits to
offer. Yet the Bush Workers’ Union does it —and thrives.
* * * *
In Sydney recently a full-blown Judge, a
barrister (instructed by a firm of three solicitors) and
another legal man (instructed by two Sydney solicitors as
agents for a third solicitor, who lives in the country) ap-
peared about a cheque. A cheque for £ll had been lost
in the post while the A.J.S. Bank was sending it to the
B. of N.S.W. for collection, and the drawer was asked to
supply another, being offered the usual guarantee against
anv possible loss if the old cheque turned up. He refused
to' supply a new cheque unless he was paid ss. for weari-
ness, exertion, loss of time, ink, physical exhaustion, and
wear and tear of pen in writing it out. So the case went
to court; and he was ordered to supply a new cheque
gratis, and was struck with the costs of all the legal
gentlemen above enumerated.
Mt. Bischoff, half-yearly meeting: Earnings,
£62,123; total for year, £112,299 ; divs., £58,500. To
credit, £44,313 ; tin production, 948 tons : cost of mining,
crushing, and dressing, 6s. 3d. per ton of ore. The grade
continues to fall, and, as the working expenses must be
within a fraction of the minimum, a further fall in grade
and market value of the metal will make a big cut in
divs. Altogether this great mine has seen its best days,
and, while it will last many years, each year is likely to
be worse than the preceding one.
* * * *
The great Long Tunnel gold mine (Walhalla,
Vic.) has got on the rocks. It was one of the greatest
and most profitable gold mines Australia ever saw, but the
fool-directors paid away practically everything in divi-
dends (the dividends have been over £1,229,000), didn’t
develop the mine as they went along, and built up no
reserve-fund worth mentioning. And now, the visible
gold supply having given out, it is necessary to make
calls in order to sink deeper, and there is hardly any
available capital to call. So it seems quite possible that
the great Long Tunnel mine will close up. There are
known to be three unworked reefs in the co’s ground,
but the co. never opened them up in its good days, and it
would take patience and money to reach them. And
when a great mine, which was supposed to be as regular
and reliable as the Bank of England, dries up suddenly,
the shareholders are often struck by a sort of paralysis
and let things slide. It was recently proposed in Vic.
Assembly that it should be made compulsory for every
mining co. to carry a certain portion of its profits to a
reserve to be available for exploration and development
work. Such a law would do more for the Vic. gold in-
dustry than the floating of whole droves of “cats” in
England and elsewhere.
The alleged action of the Tas. Govt, in trying
to drive the N. Lyell people near the site where a M.P.
holds a number of' surveyed blocks is causing much local
•stir. Also, the meeting called to consider this matter
was invited “ to congratulate the Federal Ministry on
their judgment in excluding Tasmanian politicians from
their midst.”

The man who takes on the Treasurership of S.
Aus. and regards himself and his Estimates seriously
won’t keep on much flesh'. For consider the remarkable
and unforeseeable variations in the wheat province’s
wheat exports, with values, for the last seven years
1894 .. 6,303,950 bushels .. £741,912
1895 .. 3,527,033 „ ' 445,461
1896 .. 455,942 „ .. 89,515

1898 .. 97,073 „ .. 15,911
1899 .. 3,114,781 „ .. 422,439
1900 .. 3,625,051 „ .. 492,404
Still, though the “ wheat province,” S. Australia’s export
of wool is worth on the average twice as much as its ship-
ments of breadstuffs.
• * * *
A Watsonville (Q.) correspondent: “We have
had splendid rain here, and, in re grass and water, every-
thing is looking very well. Mines will now start crush-
ing and smelting, thus enabling the value of some of the
properties to be defined. This is an extensive field, and
has all kinds of minerals—principally tin, silver, copper
and gold. District only in its infancy, and will need
much money before regular large yields can be looked
for, but I haven’t the slightest doubt (after 19 years’ local
experience) that some of the shows will pay good interest
on any reasonable outlay. Country principally granite,
porphyry and sandstone, the sedimentary formations
lying on the granite.”
Someone sends along the balance-sheet of the
Darling Downs Permanent Building and Investment
Society (Toowoomba, Queensland), now in its fourth
year :
Profits. Dividends. Reserves.

The Darling Downs Permanent B. and I. Society appears
to be rising rapidly from a very small beginning. Its
progress shows that no institution should lose heart.
Even a very small concern that has barely £5 of a surplus
may rise with careful and enterprising management until
it becomes a financial landmark in the land, or till it
either gets or deserves five years’ hard labor. And this
particular institution is apparently building up a solid
position for itself.
* * * *
Loddon Deep Leads, Yic. : This is another
Bottomlev prospectus. The idea is to work a deep-lead
g.m. lease. Only £650,000 asked for it, and to prove that
it is worth it, and more too, the results are given of
mines working from two to eight miles away. The yields
and divs. of the famous Madame Berry mine are also re-
corded, as well as the fact that that celebrated mine can
be seen from this co.’s property. Though not stated, it
must be taken for granted that you are not short-sighted
when you make the attempt, and if you get up a high
tree so much the better.

Greta Coal: Announced that £750,000 has been
raised to build a dock at Newcastle and to put down two
ahaftfl, on'* 1830 ft. deep, to Up the Grata coal seam.
Apropos, the A.A. Co. at Newcastle last year put a bore
down over 2000 ft. without striking any seam eonsiderea
good enough to follow with a shaft, so the project to sink
one was abandoned.
* * » *
Tarcoola, S. Australia’s far-north goldfield,
starts very early on its labour troubles. Though in tpe
most desolate of the Gordelpus country, hundreds oi
miles by sand-track from anywhere, where water tor a
man costs Is. a day, they have been paying skilled miners
only 9s. 4d. a day. And the men, who know that under
even better circumstances in the West miners got their
12s. a day, ask for 11s. a day for skilled men, and 9s 4d.
for surface labourers. The real miner has always been
treated scurvily in S.A. ; only to the “miners oi tne
Exchange has the Holy City bowed the knee.
* * * *
Boulder Perseverance : Some frank admissions
by Zeb. Lane concerning the mine and Frank Gardner,
in an interview published in the Rialto, just before he
left London, six weeks ago: —“The machinery is not by
any means the success that I anticipated, but it will come
out all right. I admit that lam responsible for the new
plant, but it is quite as difficult for Zeb. Lane to design
machinery in Old Jewry for mines in Westralia as it would
be for Mr. Dalziel to sit in his city office and design
a cable from Pretoria. Dear old Frank—or, I should
say—Mr. Gardner, is a more sanguine spirit than 1. naa
it not been for him, I should have been a poorer man to-
day. As a financier, he is first-class—one of The best l
ever met—but I am afraid he is too optimistic m
mining matters. I have not supported the market
because I am not a speculator. Quite true that,
when the shares were £l6, I expressed the opinion
that they would go to £2O ; I did not buy any
shares myself, though my friends did on my advice. My
object in life is to live ; it worries me to see others starve,
and that is why you do not see me much in bad times. Mr.
Gardner was quite right in supporting the Perseverance
market. He has millions of loose capital, and does not
value money as I do. The more he buys the more he will
get, and when he has got the lot he cannot get any more
—can he? X am sorry for those who have lost their
money, but it is not the first time that they have lost it.
Referring to the recently floated Windsor Consolidated,
Lane admitted that he had bought the mine cheaply, and
sold at a good profit; that he had told his friends the
shares would go to £2 ; equally true he did not buy any
himself : denied that he had sold any, and was sorry they
had not reached a better price. Probably no one will
take so little pleasure in reading this interview as X rank
Gardner.

Ed. Bulletin. —Be your recent note about the
Matto Grosso (Brazil) concessions of the Trans-Pacific In-
vestment Syndicate. The facts, shortly' stated, are these.
Matto Grosqo State has big areas of auriferous country,
worked first in 1717 by Portuguese with slave labor. There
were large exports of gold (although heavy royalties and
export duties resulted in a great quantity being smuggled
out undeclared) up to the early 30’s, when slavery was
regulated and lessened, and in ’BB all slaves were emanci-
pated. Since then little work has been done, and on the
Coxipo and Rio D’Oro Rivers the auriferous sands were
treated by the primitive methods of making wing-dams,
pumping the enclosed area as low as possible (never with-
in 2ft. of bottom), lifting the wash out with scoops and
sluicing' it. Averag’e depth of wash for a distance of 32
miles is given by probing tests at 6ft. 6in. ; and the scoops
never got within 2ft. of bottom. Yet the scheme ha 3
been paying by this slow method of metal-winning. On
the total distance of 32 miles of river there is but one bar
and rapid too high for a dredge to pass over. The reli-
able Australian mining man who secured the property
says he saw no boulders a bucket could not handle, that the
average depth of river in dry 7 season is 20in., with occa-
sional holes of 10ft. in depth, and that. the river bed
is almost innocent of snags. Bed of river 90ft wide.
Elevation above the sea of the up-stream end of the con-
cession, 1098 ft. Following tests were made :—lyd. of
wash, from surface to depth of 2ft 6iii. gave 13. gr. gold;
lyd. from centre of wash gave 29gr. gold; lyd. from centre
to bottom of wash gave lOdwt. 2gr. gold, equal to £2 0s 4d.
5 dishes from skimniings of bedrock (a soft decomposed
slate) averaged over 4gr. to the dish, equal to loz.
2dwt. to the yard ; 2 dishes of wash from the crevices
in the bedrock gave 6|dwt. gold ; 2yds. of wash from
surface to bedrock yielded 12dwt. 20gr. gold, equal to

During 1900 the value of the frozen meat ex-
ported from Maoriland fell some £20,000, the figures
being :
1899. 1900.
Mutton .. .. £1,151,705 £948,158
Lamb .. .. 585,067 639,152
Beef 190,257 320,147
£1,927,029 £1,907,457
The shortage in mutton is likely to increase. During the
last five months of 1900 the sheep frozen were 454,985.
But dairy produce shows a marked advance, the figures
being :
1899. 1900.
Butter .. .. £561,414 £730,503
Cheese .. .. 140,228 227,711
£701,642 £958,214
With the present tendency to cut up large places into
dairy farms, these figures are certain to go on increasing.
According to returned Syd. D.T. war-cor-
respondent Frank Wilkinson, the points in which
Australian troops excel British are intelligence
and resourcefulness, horsemanship and veterinary
skill. Commenting on his luck in escaping injury,
Wilkinson mentions that his Cape-cart, alongside
of which he was frequently riding, had nine bullets
in it when he left S’Africa.

Rarely does a man abscond from bail,
keep clear for six years, then get caught, and
after all escape. Yet this happened to an alleged
larcenist in Melbourne t’other day. He was
charged with stealing a purse, and the victim —a
barmaid, had got lost or married or something,
anyhow she wasn’t to be found, and her employer,
who originally signed the charge-sheet, had died
years ago. So the prosecution lapsed.
Swaddies, returned from S’Africa, tell
some hair-raising stories about the tyranny of
some of the Australian officers. Yet the contm-
genting craze is still active. Here is a cut from
a letter just received from a Quirindi (N.S.W.)
warrior:—•
“ We are at present getting treated like a lot of pig-s
more than anything else. We get one night in bed and
one on duty. We are simply alive with vermin.
(the officer’s name) seems to neglect us, as we
never get any mail, and to tell you the truth he is going
off his head. The men hate the very sight of him. He
has already led them into a couple of death-traps, and
has been censured. We are half-clothed, and have a hard
job to even get a bit of soap to wash ourselves. It. will
be God help some of the officers if ever they get back to
civilian life again. The way the Boers are carrying on
their guerilla warfare is 20 times more dangerous than a
big battle, as you never know when you ride into a death-
trap. If you put this letter in print don’t sign my name
to it, or else leave out ’s name, as he gets the papers,
and he may make it hot for me. I don’t care what 1
write about him once I get back. They ought to give
him a job driving Kaffirs, instead of leading men to their
death—the swine. God knows when we will get
home. On the whole we have had a hard lot of fighting
and a very trying time, and for it we get half-clothed,
and get old cancered bullocks to eat, while the Maori-
landers and others get nice frozen meat. It’s no one s
fault hut the officer’s. . . . I am not the only one that is
complaining —there is not a man in the whole regiment
that is satisfied.”
And you should just bear the nice things that re-
turned Queenslanders are saying with a loud voice
about a prominent officer of theirs !
A Word to Women.
The success or failure of men, as well as
Governments, largely depends upon women
Women rule the world. Men may occupy high
offices and arrogate to themselves the directorship
of public and private affairs, but back of tins out-
ward manifestation is the silent but irresistible
force of women. It enters into every thought and
action ; it permeates every fibre, tissue and bone.
It inspires men with all that is good, pure and
noble. Show me the quality of the wife, and I
will tell you the standard of the husband. With
the history of successful men, should be written
“ Inspired, aided and strengthened by a thought-
ful and high minded wife.” A community of
good women will produce a community of good
men. , , .
It is hardly necessary to say that good health is
essential to the accomplishment of good work.
No matter how ambitious, or how pure and lofty
one’s ideas may be, if disease is sapping our
vitality and attracting the thoughts to bodily
ailments, we shall not be able to render to our
loved ones and society that care, sympathy and
assistance they have a right to expect of us. If
you are ailing, your first duty is to get well. You
have no right to expect to enjoy the fulness of a
man’s love or the favors of society if you are con-
tinually fretting and bemoaning your trials and
afflictions. It is hard, you say, to be cheerful
with a splitting headache, a sluggish liver, or a
rebellious stomach. True —very true. But why
be tormented with t' ose ailments ? Why not take
the remedy that will cure you, “Warner’s Safe
Cure ? ”
Mrs. Eliza Butler, of Broken Hill, N.S.W.,
writing on Oct. 11, 1900, says: “I have great
pleasure in testifying to the good results I have
obtained from the use of Warner’s Safe Cure.
Eor some time I was suffering great agony with
terrible pains in my side and around the body.
After trying several remedies, which failed, I
used Warner’s Safe Cure, and am happy to state
that lam now quite restored to health. You may
make this public and accept my thanks for the
good results I obtained.”*

POLITICAL

“ First Federation,” said stout old Petei
Lalor of Eureka Stockade.
11 As the British Empire expands, the
Chamberlain family contracts.”
Australia has the men, but she has not yet
the Man—the Australian Man.
The Sydney professional musicians, drop-
ping all artistic frill, have had the pluck to
affiliate their benefit-organisation to the Trades
and Labour association, thus recognising them-
selves as workers first and artists afterwards.
Good !
John Plumper Hoolan is to oppose Drake
for a seat in the Federal Parliament. Renegade
vs. Renegade; but Drake still has his uses, and
Hoolan is of no use. Once he had democratic
grit; now he is simply a cynical humbug out for
political loot.
London correspondent of Sydney D.T.
speaks of “the slim and mobile opposition by
which Barton will be confronted.” Mobile it may
be, but with George Reid it certainly won’t be
slim. Also, “mobile” sounds less offensive, as
applied to Reidian opposition, than “shifty.”
Maoriland Government has started to or-
ganise a Seventh Contingent. Contingents follow
each other so fast from Maoriland now that each
one has to step like an ostrich to avoid having its
feet trodden by the next. The Bulletin implores
Chamberlain to make Seddon Duke of Puketi-
whino at once—or sooner, if possible.
“In Victoria they simply turn the men
out of the factories and put little children in,”
said the veracious Reid in his latest Foreign-trade
Jeremiad. Which, considering that the increase
of men in Victoria’s factories during the last few
years (the Reid “ Freetrade” tariff years) has been
2000 more than the increase in N.S. W., is a pretty
good sample of G. H. Reid’s reliability.
The Victorian Govt, is still “taking
steps ” to cancel the pension of the old man who
spends it in drink. This is a dismal hypocrisy.
The people who spend money in drink are one of
the principal sources of revenue to every Aus-
tralian State, and the State can’t say that drink is
sinful unless it also admits that it is a mercenary
scoundrel who lives on sin, and that Premier Pea-
cock’s salary is largely the wages-of iniquity.

N.S. W. Premier Lyne (from the election-
eering platform) :
I intend, before I leave State politics, to have a monu-
ment erected in the Centennial Park in Sydney to the
memory of those who fell so far from home. (Loud
cheers.) I think something of this kind should certainly
be done. (Renewed cheers.)
Is the monument to be built with Treasury money?
Is there to be any direct individual self-sacrifice
connected with it, or is it to be another loan-fund
fake ?
“ Brains ” Ashton’s personal desire is to
run for the Senate in N.S. W. ; but the Dry Dog
party wants his vote in the other House and presses
him to run against Chanter for the Riverina—
where he will probably be beaten. One of Ashton’s
chief longings—and an excellent one, too—is to
break down the idea that the Senate is the dod-
derers’ chamber. Until somebody or something
can do that, he argues, the Commonwealth will
wear lead on its feet.

quence of the interference of Christian nations in

The heathen’s blind, and cannot see
’Tis for his good alone, this strife.

The Leg. Council seat for S.A. Central
District, lately vacated by Kingston, has been
captured by that dreadful Tory, J. L. Parsons,
who collected 4665 votes to the democratic candi-
date’s 2671. The result gives a fair proof of
Kingston’s weight in S.A. politics. The Tory
majority in the Central district is about 2000—
almost 2to 1; yet Kingston, the ultra-democrat,
carried it by storm. Apparently his personal
magnetism was worth a good 1000 votes.
One of Gr. H. Reid’s chief excuses for his
double-dealing at the first Federal referendum
was his solemn warning, chiefly to Sydney:
“ Remember ! you cannot expect to get Freetrade
under this Bill.” Yet he is now wasting a tre-
mendous amount of energy in trying to prove not
only is Freetrade possible, now the Bill is passed,
but that any other policy will be disastrous to the
country under it! These facts should be posted
on every hoarding and every fence throughout
Australia.
That wonderful man, hatter Storey,
M. of N.S.W., got himself interviewed by
the Freetrade paper at Launceston (Tas.) recently
and told some remarkable things. Among others,
he assured his interviewer cordially that there was
nothing at all in the theory that N.S.W. has
largely kept afloat by borrowed money :
P° n ’t you believe it. There is nothing in it. I haven’t
the figures by me, but I am sure the per capita debt of
N. is no greater than that of Victoria.
Yet every official and unofficial publication in
Australia that touches on finance tells Storey in
plain figures that the N.S.W. debt is much larger
per capita than the Victorian. And a man so
ignorant as not even to know a little thing like
that is allowed to make laws for this unfortunate
country !
So far only two Australian States
have paid Old Age Pensions, and they
are both Protectionist. Only one
State has fixed a legal minimum wage
for workers to the confusion of the
sweating brigade, and it is Protec-
tionist. Only one State has a thorough-
ly efficient compulsory Conciliation and
Arbitration law, and it is Protection-
ist. ' Only three. States have State Land
Banks in full working order, and they
are all Protectionist. Only one State
is resuming big estates for closer settle-
ment on a large scale, and it is Pro-
tectionist. Only two States smite
the big earth-monopolist heavily with
a graduated land-tax, and they are
both Protectionist. Only two States
have a reasonably efficient absentee-
tax, and they are likewise Protectionist.
These things may not be caused by Pro-
tection, but they seem to have a re-
markable habit of going along with
Protection.
George Turner formally quitted
the Victorian Premiership on sth Feb-
ruary, and Premier Peacock reigns in
his stead. No new man was appointed,
so there is one Minister less, with a
saving of £lO4O a year, and Victoria
is minus a salaried P.M.G. This is the
first attempt to reduce the cost of
any State Parliament under Federa-
tion. Several individuals demanded
that the portfolios should be spread out
rather thinner, so that despite the abo-
lition of the P.M.G. there would still
be nine paid Ministers, but Premier
Peacock laughed loudly in the ear of
the chief malcontent and broke him up.
In Queensland, desperately hard-up as
they are, they shovel the public money
about much more liberally, and the
vacancies created by Diekson’s death and Drake’s
promotion have both been filled. Philp is a tired
too tired to economise.

It was this way. The N.S.W. Laborite
bosses reckoned that there was only one man who
could be reasonably certain of getting even near a
Senate seat in N.S.W., and that was leader
M‘G-owen. But, then, M‘Growen is the only man
of the party who can win the Darlinghurst seat,
and that is judged to be a “ soft thing ” for him ;
and so, as M‘Gowen himself very much preferred
the safer and easier seat, he was not pressed to
go the other way. After that the party took very
little interest in the matter.
John Kidd, N.S.W. candidate for Federal
Senate, is almost funny because of his utter want
of fun. He is a secret-society man, top and
bottom. Gr. R. Dibbs made him a Minister. The
office given him had been previously filled by the
illustrious Dan O’Connor, and when the acidulous
Parkes heard of the arrangement he observed
(speaking politically): “ What would you expect
after a goat but a kid ? ” Kidd, who looks a sens-
ible man, is perhaps the only politician in N.S.W.
who can talk for an hour without pleasing an
audience or making it angry.
Perth Morning Herald says straight out
that John Forrest was» transferred at his o vn
request from the Federal Post Office to the
Federal Defence Department, because, when he
faced the duties of the former billet, he was
broken up with a sudden knowledge of his own
incompetence. In W.A., it alleges, the P.M.Gr.
leaves things to the permanent officials, and John
only realised his utter ignorance of everything,
and his utter inability to learn anything, when he
tried to run a postal department himself.
Freely stated that Chief-Justice Stout, of
Maoriland, is to be hauled over the coals for
his pro-Federation utterances, on the plea that
Judges shouldn’t meddle in politics.” Federa-

now it’s started you’ve (jot to approve of it,”
nobody talked about “ meddling in politics.” If
the Seddon Govt. doesn’t look out it will yet be
known as the champion muzzier of Australasia.
Federal Postmaster - General Drake at
Sydney Town Hall :
Mr. Reid, in his speech the other night, disposed of
Queensland by saying that it was in favor of Freetrade,
with the exception of one or two spots. (A voice •
“Quite correct.”) He did not say that the statement
was not correct, but he should have liked the hori. gen-
tleman to define what he meant by the word “spot,”
because the truth or untruth of his statement would de-
pend upon that word. He took it that the meaning of
the statement was this, that Queensland was in favor of
Freetrade except where the population resided.
Neat !
A truly beautiful item from the Freetrade
crank of Launceston (Tas.) Examiner :
Protectionists seem to revel in the idea that the greatest
happiness that can overtake a nation is to always export,
and receive nothing in return. I would ask then, Is it
intended to give the goods away ? By no means. This is
the farthest thought from the Protectionist’s mind. He
has some shady idea that if he could get gold for his
wares he would be happy. But we do not want gold in
Australia. We have gold to sell. If we studied our own
true interests; we should give these products of our
country we have in excess in exchange for things we
cannot produce. This is true economy. The country
that is always giving (exporting) and taking nothing in
exchange (importing) would soon become bankrupt.
A rational being might rather think that the
country which exports and doesn’t import would
soon get rid of its foreign debts, and be all the
richer for it instead of becoming bankrupt. And
think of the wonderful intelligence of the Free-
trader who says that Australia doesn’t want gold !
This country has between £14,000,000 and
£15,000,000 a year to pay in gold as interest on its
public and private foreign debts (set down by
Coghlan at £370,000,000). Even with the great gold-
boom in Westralia it doesn’t quite manage to get
enough gold to pay this interest, saving nothing
of reducing the principal of the debts’!! There are
very few countries which want gold worse than
gold-producing Australia.

who can be seen daily driving four-
in-hand turn-outs throughout the

up, and not too proud to accept a
ift from the man with the turban.

Wise, K.C., will have a tough
fight for Canoblas. His chief op-
ponent is one “Tom” Brown,
Labor M.P. for Condobolin, who is
utterly unknown to the general
public, but whose diligence as a
roads-and-bridges hustler has given
him a high place in the affections
of local settlers, in a region where
roads and culverts are the be-all and end-all of
the elector’s existence. Wise will find trouble in
convincing them that the Federal representative
will not be a roads-and-culverts man on a larger
scale.
No other Australian statesman can show-
such a consistent democratic record as Kingston,
who completes 20 years of political life next June’.
S.A. owes to him Payment of Members, Protec-
tion, Factories legislation, Adult Suffrage, Early-
Closing, Workmen’s Liens and Compensation,
and Closer Settlement, all of which are now law.
Kingston is one more proof that the great demo-
crats of Australia (the men who did things as
distinguished from the men who talked about
them), the great political intellects of Australia,
the great reformers of Australia are all on the
Protectionist side. A cause is known by the
brains whose company it keeps.
Federal Attorney-General Deakin met
with a flattering reception at Sydney Town Hall.
Especially did his repartee delight the crowd'.
Deakin was very sarcastic at the expense of G. H.
Reid. First he related several bombastic speeches
of the latter, who, on his return from Victoria,
told the Sydney press of the great stir his (Reid’s)
speeches had made down south, where, indeed, the
benighted Protectionists were on the brink of con-
version. Then he quoted “No-Yes” from his
Protestant Hall address wherein Reid predicted
that N.S.W. would find arrayed in the first
Federal parliament “An unbroken phalanx of
Protectionists ” from Victoria. See-saw Reid !
William Lyne told the Hume electors the
other day that Hindoos are “subjects of the King
and cannot be stopped coming in ” to the Com-
monwealth. On the other hand Premier Barton,
who is Lyne’s political boss, has promised that
Asiatics will be “stopped coming in.” And
Maoriland has proved, by the simple process of
doing it, that it is possible to stop the influx of
the King’s undesirable subjects. Premier Barton
had better chain up his clumsy Minister for
Internal Affairs if he is going to say foolish things
like this. Australia can’t afford to be governed
by large helpless individuals who have such a pro-
found respect for the King’s nigger that they will
let him take the bread out of the mouths of the
King’s white Australian subjects.
Westralia’s post and telegraph employes
rejoice with a great joy that Bigjohn has ceased
to be Federal P.M.G. They have had enough of
Groper management, and of Bigjohn, and
especially of his small deputy nigger-driver, Post-
master-General Richard Adolphus Shol’l. In
previous times Adolphus drove the department to
go on strike by his petty persecutions, and though
peace was patched up on condition that none of
the strikers should suffer in any way through their
action they have practically all been harried out
of the service. The newest trouble has arisen
through the refusal of the high-handed Adolphus .
to recognise the union of telegraph employes. In
addition to refusing he promptly transferred
the principal union-officials in the Perth office to
the most remote and desolate stations in all W.A.
Which is one reason why, when Federal P.M.G.
Drake takes over the postal department, the
union will probably serenade its Adolphus with a
bad concertina and a kerosene tin.
The Federal Premier is to get £2lOO a
year, and his colleagues £1650 each. But the
Federal Constitution does not prohibit a Minister
also drawing his salary of £4OO a year as a private
member, and with this added the Premier will
have £2500 and his colleagues £2050 each. The
only amendment The Bulletin can suggest is
that the amounts should be at least doubled. Aus-
tralia wants to always get the best men, and they
can seldom be had without paying the best price.
rhis«country has had lots of cheap men in charge
of its affairs —-Treasurers whom no board of
directors would dream of putting in charge of a
small bank, and Works Ministers that wouldn’t
be trusted to superintend a small contract and
Ministers of Education who hadn’t any. It has
saved thousands in salary by these means, and
lost millions by incapacity and bad financing. If
the railways were run on the same principle they
would be placed in charge of a porter at 6s. 6d. a
day to save the Commissioner’s salary—but the
country somehow had a casual gleam of sanity
when it fixed up its system of railway manage-
ment.
