BRISBANE.
NEWCASTLE.
A poverty-stricken looking old man
recently admitted to Rookwood (Sydney) Asylum
for the Destitute, died shortly afterwards. £4O
in gold was found sewn inside his waistcoat and
securities to the value of £4OO were contained in
his clothing. No heirs are forthcoming so the
money goes into the Intestate Estates office. The
Institutions’ officials though used to (smaller) dis-
coveries of this sort, were astonished at their find,
the deceased appearing to be the last man one
would suspect of hidden wealth.
A long epistle in Sydney E. News from
somebody called Smith seems to be calling for
the dismissal of the Sydney police force generally
because, having lost his two boys in the crowd
round Queen’s Statue on Funeral Day, said
Smith appealed to various custodians of the
public welfare to find them immediately, and
these sad-eyed men merely replied with some
irrelevance, “I have had no dinner yet.” It
might be fairly put to Smith that if he was a
dinnerless constable at about 40s. a week, he pro-
bably wouldn’t take much interest in hunting for
two small boys that he had never seen, and
wouldn’t know from Shem and Japhet if he saw
them.

Thirty thousand more troops wanted for
S’Africa —for the war that is “practically over !”
Looks as if Adelaide isn’t just yet going to get a
chance of banging-off that £2OOO-worth of fire-
works it so long ago laid in against the proclama-
tion of the cessation of hostilities.
Queen Wilhelmina of Holland had a
telephone fixed up between her palace and the
home of her impending consort, whom she
married last week. To rise to the very height of
really scientific love-making they should have
kissed by wireless telegraph, and hugged each
other per induction-coils.
From a M.L. trooper’s letter :
In your letter you seem to expect that the war will
soon be over, but I do not think it will be over yet for
months. We have been burning every house, taking
every hoof, and destroying all the grain. In most cases
the owners are out fighting, and now will never surrender.
You might as well reason with a Red Indian on the war-
path, and with about as much chance of inducing him to
lay down his arms. The sufferings of the women and
children are pitiful.
After all, how much do any of the war-
writers know? If there was one thing they
agreed on more absolutely than on all others it
was that Roberts was too tender in his treatment
of the enemy, while Kitchener would be stern to
the point of mercilessness. And yet Kitchener
begins by modifying Roberts’ proclamation in the
direction of offering more clemency to leaders
who surrender.
“ H.8.T.” : Re beaching ships on the
Westralian coast (8., 26/l/’01): —
At Carnarvon, Wyndham, Broome, and Port Darwin
there is from natural causes a large tidal range, amount-
ing to 18ft. at Carnarvon, and 27ft. at Port Darwin ; and
I have often heard sailor men say that it is thereabouts
usual to beach vessels and load them in the manner indi-
cated. In fact, the mud beach near the jetty at Port
Darwin is always pointed out to new arrivals as “ our dry
dock.”
Writes Harry Stockdale :
While I was, the other night, lecturing to Sydney Boys’
Brigade on “The Aborigines of Australia” a curious
thing occurred. During the description of a native com-
bat—in which the lads seemed particularly interested —a
shrill whistle was heard on the stairs, and in a jiffy the
hall was half empty ! the boys, as they rushed out, crying
—“ The ’Arbours—the ’Arbours !” No bugle-call could
have been more promptly responded to. This whistle, it
turned out, was the summons to battle of a rival crowd
called the “ Harbour Push.” But it was a false alarm, and
inside five minutes my juvenile audience was sitting in
its place again as though nothing unusual had happened.
The “Tyser”line steamer Star of Aus-
tralia, now due at Melbourne, carries the largest
shipment of American-manufactured goods that
has ever left an American port. Her cargo list
covers 120 pp., each 50 per cent, larger than an
average foolscap sheet. In this is included every
variety of manufactured goods produced in the
U.S. There are 400 tons of sewing-machines
alone. For Auckland the vessel brings an
entire gas-plant, weighing 500 tons. There are
rifles, shot-guns, revolvers, and ammunition suf-
ficient to supply a brigade, whilst lawn-mowers,
reapers, waggons, wheels, coffee-mills, patent
medicines, &c., are reckoned by tons. Kerosene
to the extent of 80,000 cases and 1500 barrels, 1000
tons of wire, 1200 tons of bar iron, 400 tons of roll-
paper, hundreds of pianos and organs, together
with an extensive array of miscellaneous articles,
comprise a cargo which, for size and value, has
probably not been exceeded in any other vessel
coming to Australia.

Government Notices.
Public Works Department,
Sydney, Jan. 17, 1901.
CITY RAILWAY EXTENSION AND DEVONSHIRE
STREET, CEMETERY.
TT having been determined to extend the City Railway
to Devonshire-street, notice is hereby given that in
all cases where application has been made to the Minister
of Justice, by the representatives of deceased persons
buried in the Devonshire-street Cemetery, and permission
obtained to exhume such bodies with the sanction of the
Cemetery Trustees within two months from this date, the
Department of Public Works will bear all reasonable
expenses incurred in the re-interment of such bodies in
such Cemetery as the said representatives may desire
The cost of removing and re-erecting all monuments,
stone-work, etc., now in a fair state of preservation, will
also be borne by the Government.
In the ease of those bodies for which no applications
will have been received at the expiration of this notice,
exhumation will be carried out by the Government and
re-interment effected at R.ookwood Cemetery.
I orms of application and other information may be
obtained on application to the Clerk in Charge of Bonds
and Contracts at this Department.
ROBERT HICKSON,
Under Secretary for Public Works and
Commissioner for Roads.

From a N.S.W. Lancer’s letter published
in Sydney Daily Telegraph
“ We had seen a bit of the seamy side of the war, and
we were not very sentimental; but I thought of my old
mother many a time, and I don t mind telll ”EJ° u ,
more than once actually cried to see some of the cruel
and senseless things our fellows did. Youd see perhaps
a poor old woman and a couple of young girls lying on
makeshift bed on the earthen floor. The soldiers would
rush in, ransack the house, and take away things they
could not possibly have any use for, or carry far. Thej d
even collar the bedding of the wretched creatures
crouching in terror there on the floor. I thought more
than once, “And such might be the lot, some day, of my
poor old mother.”
The following, from a recent copy of
Home Notes (Eng.) should interest Dr. Ross :
SMOKING SCHOOLBOYS.
In Australia there seems to he not only no strong objec-
tion to juvenile smokers, but they are actually encour-
aged. incredible though it may appear, we are assured
bv the Cigarette World that the advisability of attach-
ing smoking-rooms to schools is already being discussed.
A Melbourne suburban J.P., called upon
the other day to mete out punishment to a baker
convicted for the severalth time of giving very
short weight in his broad, inquired anxiously if
there was “no way of stopping this sort of
thing.” This is an instance of the Law s tender
regard for the respectable person, especially the
respectable employer. If that baker s delivery-
boy had stolen one loaf he would probably have
been gaoled right off; if he persisted m
thus unlawfully increasing his wages, he
would, for the third or fourth offence,
most certainly have got a, long sentence and
probably a stiff dose of “solitary.” It was stated
in court that one of. the offending bakers was
making £5OO a year out of short weights, yet
though he is an even more wholesale thief than
the pickpocket or the loaf-pilferer, the Law care-
fully stipulates that he shall be fined some trifling
sum “ not exceeding” a stated amount. Of
course he pays up smiling, knowing he’ll make it
up out of more short weights in less than no time.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Saturday s racing at Caulfield had two or three
terns of interest. Wakeful, a Sunrise mare rated as a 25
to 1 chance for the Newmarket, won Oakleigh Plate in
SOme an( h presuming that some of the animals
sne ran away from were seriously fancied, the perform-
ance must have been a good one. Anyway the time—6B
secs, for 6J furlongs—was satisfactory. In Woodcliff
Handicap (llfur.) ancient Dreamland and the 3-year-old
uosport had the finish all to themselves, the youngster
overhauling his venerable antagonist in the straight and
beating him by half a head. Contrast, Barbarossa and
t l6< u admiral (alj of them backed for more money) were
far belnnd the pair. Dreamland, as was lately suggested
by Thb Bulletin, is coming on again in his old age. If it
was merely want of condition that stopped him (and,
Judging by the market, Scobie didn’t think him nearly
ripe) Dreamland will cut an important figure at V.R.O.
Autumn meet. On the other hand it may be that the
resurrected 9-year-old has not gained any stamina, and is
the same old deceiver as before. Gosport’s form was cer-
tainly encouraging. A son of Portsea and Fenellaß.,he
is bred to improve with age. The colt ran like a stayer,
and should be watched by punters.
* * * *
Wakeful’s bolt with Oakleigh Plate in Melb. on
Saturday was an eye-opener, and caused her to be rushed
down to eights (offered and freely taken) for the New-
market. Subsequently 700 to 100 was accepted twice,
and, at time of writing, she is firm at eights. Wakeful is
by Trenton from Insomnia, and will hump 7. 6., including
101 b. penalty. O. L. McDonald (long manager of now-
dispersed St. Albans stud) owns her, and is said to have
gathered in coin over Saturday’s run-away. The nag
was backed from tens down to fours. Wakeful’s perform-
ance stamps her a, clinker. Time was § sec. faster than
Coil s—the previous best done in that race—and only
1 sec. slower than Wild Rose’s Australian record (1. 7)
for the distance—s£ fur.
• • *
M.L. reputed marvel, Advance (now almost
paralysing l Newmarket Handicap betting*), was shipped
st°tu uc^an( l on Saturday week, and goes straight to
Melbourne. “Banjo” Paterson says of him: “Many
judges consider Advance as good a horse as Carbine ever
was. He doesn’t look it. He is about 15.3 high, a very
plain wear-and-tear sort, with a pony’s head, a coarse
neck, splendid barrel and limbs, and common-looking
hind-quarters rather badly coupled on to his body. About
the last animal a stranger would pick out as a champion
by his looks. All the same he is undoubtedly a galloping
phenomenon. I saw him run a working gallop. He moves
like a piece of clock-work and gallops with his head high,
which gives him the appearance of looking about him at
the scenery, while the horse alongside is straining every
nerve to keep there. In all his races lately he has finished
m the same way, with his head up, looking round him.
There is nbthing in M.L. that can make him extend him-
self at weight for age. Advance will meet very much
better horses here than over there. A leading M.L.
racing man said ‘ They will make a mistake if they try
to win the Newmarket with Advance. He can stay tiil
further orders, and in the long races he will not meet
many as good as himself, but at 6 fur. he may meet a
dozen flyers who will jump away and cut out such a pace
that his big weight will tell.’ N.S.W. and Yic. racing
men will rub their eyes with astonishment when they see
the plain-looking black go out for his gallop, and they will
be more astonished than ever when they see him move
along.”
* * * *
The Sydney and Melb. “ books ” are laying
from 200 to 1 to 500 to 1 (outside combinations) the
doubles—Newmarket and Australian Cup, and Doncaster
and Sj'dney Cup, the longer odds being very smellful, as
a rule. Fancy asking Ikey Mo for 3363 to 1 about the
former, and 9476 to 1 about the latter 1 Yet those are
the figures against any single combine Newmarket and
Cup (84 x 40—1), and the other double (104 x 92—1). The
gullible punter often wonders where Ikey and Co. get
their jewellery ; he oughtn’t to.
* * * *
Some people who have seen Fulminate and
Watch Dog when near their best believe that, should the
two incur no penalty, whatever can beat them will win
the Newmarket.
» * * *
Vic. trainer Scobie has started out on another
scoop. He gathered in Alma Stakes (per medium of
Hautvilliers) and Oakleigh Purse by means of another
two-year-old, United States (late Commonwealth), at
Caulfield, on Saturday. Hautvilliers is owned by Mr. W.
Bailey, hut was bred by Mr. “ Sam ” Hordern at Wilton
Park (N.S.W.), and sold as a yearling for 430 gs. United
States is by Bill of Portland from Happy Land, and so is
half-brother to the whilom famous Hova. Ancient Dream-
land (also among Scobie’s lot) went near winning Wood-
cliff Handicap same day. Gosport only beat her by half
a head.
» * * «
Projectile—Hirondelje 3yr.-old colt Com-
mander t’other morning put up the season’s record
(1.4 J) for 5 fur. off the sand at Flemington. This nag seems
to be coming on again. When owned by Sydney trainer
“Sam’’Fielder he promised well, and “Oxy” offered
£l,OOO for him. Fielder wasn’t on then, but subsequently
accepted very much less.
* * * *
Trainer and owner Sam Fielder’s Bother (late
Mother) atop of her double at Moorefield (Sydney) week
before, won Rosehill Sprinters’ Stakes, 6 fur. (Saturday)
carrying 101 b. penalty. The Watchdog and Bange were
behind. Bother ’ll win again soon if wanted and handi-
cappers haven’t quite got her measure.
• • • *
Apparent best N.S.W. nag out this season—and
for a long time—is Stillwater, winner of Rosehill two-
year-old race, Saturday. He is by Far Niente from
Rivula, and was bred at Neotsfield. As a yearling, Still-
water brought 150 gs. At dispersal of Chipping Norton
stud “Tucka Tucka” Smith secured his sire cheap.
Rivula is one of the Hobartville lot and went to Mr"
H. C. Dangar for lOOgs.
* • * *
April Fool, starting almost friendless, won
Rosehill (Sydney) February Handicap on Saturday in a
manner that should make punters keep an eye on him.
April Fool’s Sydney Cup weight is 8.3—121 b. less than he
won with on Saturday, and there is no penalty.
» * m m
At moment of writing, Northern N.S.W. nag
The Prize is running Kulaui close in favor for
S.T.C. Anniversary Handicap at Randwick this Saturday.
If as well as said, she’ll take much licking. When last in
Sydney (with 7.12 up) the mare ran third to Cyanide (7.4)
and Horace (6.7) in Place Handicap at A.J.C. Autumn
Meet. Winner’s time (2.361) was fast, and The Prize
finished less than a length off, despite getting away badly
and being last out of the straight.
* * » ♦
Crack Q. mare, Ma Ma, will trouble punter and
Mo no more. She died of “ influenza enteric ” at Rose-
hill (Sydney) t’other day.
* * * »
Said that Merriwee (Melb. Derby and Cup
winner in ’99) has hopelessly broken down and gone to
where she was bred—Bathurst, N.S.W. Also stated that
the race-track has seen the last of Bobadil.
* * * *
Cabled that Australian Peer—Colors’ nag Aus-
tralian Star secured Sandown Grand Prize (2m. over
hurdles, and worth 450 sovs. to winner), in Eng., last
Saturday. Entries hadn’t closed when last mail left
’Ome. Australian Star was bred by Eales Bros., at
Duckenfield Park (N.S.W.), and is full-brother to Aus-
tralian Colors, who also performed well at Rosehill same
day. Kogarah “Andy” Gollan bought A.S. at yearling
sales in ’97 for 30gs., but he shaped badly as a two-year-
old. Last season, a list of big successes went down to
him, commencing with Farm Stakes, at Warwick Farm
(N.S.W.), and finishing up with Guineas and Eclipse
Stakes, at Caulfield (Vic.). Present owner, S. H. Gollan
(no relation to previous one), then gave 2500g5. for the
nag, and took him to England, where he started eight
times without being placed. Despairing of success on
the flat, owner tried him over sticks. LatMt is Australian
Star’s initial succass “ down under.”
• * * *
N.S.W.’s huge score of 918 against South Aus-
tralia places her batsmen well to the front in inter-State
averages. Trumper and Gregory are in the 70’s, and
others high in proportion. Poidevin, with an aggregate
of 154 for twice out, is nominally at the head. Yet the
batting failed on five occasions, and but for the mammoth
score in the return match against S.A. would show up
badly in the season’s figures. Stuckey, who was only got
out once by N.S. W. in his four visits to the wickets in the
two matches with the mother State, shows an average of
200 against N.S.W. bowlers. ,
* * J|| £
Clem. Hill, with 620 runs" for six completed
innings, heads the Sheffield averages, with an average of
103, Poidevin running second with 77 for two completed
innings. Next in order are veteran Sid. Gregory (71) V
Trumper (65), Stuckey (68), and Noble (50). Poidevin and
Armstrong’are perhaps the best juniors of the season, and
a notable incident was the insignificant performance of
Fred. Hack, the lanky S.A. junior, who went top last
year and is nowhere this season. Saunders, the Vic
bowler, has been a solid asset this year, and not only
secured best average but took most wickets (29). Travers
the S.A. slow-bowler, who took 29 wickets, was the
other success of a season which gave no indication of suc-
cessors to Lyon, Giffen, Jones and Darling.
“ TJnley ”: This season’s Sheffield Shield con-
tests produce some curious results. Although NSW
made 2368 runs, averaging 33 per wicket, Victoria' onlv
1898, averaging 28, and S.A. 1637, averaging 23, Victoria
goes through the season unbeaten; and N.S.W and SA
are level for bottom ! N.S.W. put up her runs against
S.A., and the only decent score against Victoria was the
second innings, 452, last week. Writer (who is not a
Sydney man and is unbiassed) still considers N S W bv
far the best of the three State elevens. ‘
*• * » *
On this season’s form, Australia would perhaps
be represented in international cricket by Hill, Trumble
Travers, Stuckey, Saunders, M’Leod, Noble Gregory’
Marsh, Iredale and Ross or Kelly. In variety of bowling
it would be better, but in all other respects weaker than
Darling’s combination of ’99.

Vic. left-hand bowler Saunders’ rise has been
meteoric. At inception of last season he was unknown
His initial appearance (over 12 months since) in a big
match was against S.A. in Melb. when he bagged 6 for 71
Though having only played in 7 first-class contests so far’
his list tots up 50 wickets. Now he fills the bill to the
letter, being just the thing as a left-hander.

The Australasian Swimming Carnival concluded
in Melb. on Saturday proved the immense superiority of
N.S.W. cracks over those of other States. The Vies, were
absolutely “ not in it,” and yet, years since, they had
among them as good, if not better, than N.S.W. owns
even now—notably the MTndoes, de Littles, Kenneys,
Webster, and co. Explanation lies in the fact that swim-
ming boomed in Victoria then, especially at Geelong
where the finest baths on the continent existed. Interest
fell away subsequently, until at last, from a dozen or
more strong clubs operating freely and frequently, the
number dwindled to one, which only gave faint flickers
of life occasionally. The cracks of the old days dropped
out of the game, and through lack of incentive none
came along to fill the breach, hence the present position
Meanwhile natation flourished and developed in the
mother State as it never did anywhere before (mainly
owing to well-directed work of out-and-out enthusiasts
and exceptional facilities for swimming. Q. never pos-
sessed a genuine top-rung performer, chiefly because of
the almost total absence of what is so amply'provided in
F-^Y-~ opportunities for P ra °tice. The Wheat-State
hadn t a man nearly good enough to represent it. M.L.
came next to N.S.W. through Champion swimming third
to Cavill and Craig in the 100 at Ballarat, but he was dis-
appointing in face of what had been trumpeted by M.L.
papers, and the fact that less than a month ago he
gathered in the whole of that country’s premierships
Though Champion is said to have bullocked over the
mile at home and finished up strong, what he showed in
Melbourne pointed to sprinting and not staying as beine-
his game. 6
* * * *
Scribes have been digging at N.S.W. A.S.A.
for sending Dick Cavill and Bob Craig, and ignoring Geo.
Read, as reps, in the big swimming events, last week, yet
the right thing was done. Craig beat Read over the short
distances—looyds. and 200yds.—while Cavill got away
with the longer struggles—quarter, half, and one mile.
What more was required as endorsement of the excellent
judgment evidenced ?
* * * •
Nevertheless, Read, who went over to
Melb. “ on his own,” proved a veritable surprise party •
but for letting Dick Cavill get too far away from him in
the last couple of laps he might have won the Half Mile
At the end there wasn’t 2yds of water between them, and
Dick had it all out. Read swam his first race this season
—a championship—and, bar winning a 50yds “ flutter ”in
Melbourne, hasn’t scored a first yet ; still he is a
wonderfully fine swimmer—about the most promising
comer in the State and liable to turn the tables on boy
Dick Cavill ere present season closes, as the N.S.W. Half
Mile and Mile premierships are among future big things.
Sydney Stas telegram, published in two places
on Saturday, that Cavill’s 1.2 for the 100yds swimming
supremacy at Ballarat the day previous wasn’t accepted
“ because timekeepers had taken it from the word ‘go ’
instead of from the moment the competitors entered the
water ” was amusing. This scribe thinks it should be the
other way about. Timekeepers probably clocked the
Bmm from entering the water instead of from the starter’s
signal which should shorten distance by yards. Proof is
that Cavill subsequently couldn’t do better than 1 3 1-5
-1-5 faster than his Goulburn (N.S.W.) time over 102yds.
and accomplished just before going to Melbourne. He has
never travelled faster in public.

Thedisplays of M.L. swimming-crack Champion
in Australian premierships last week quite upset M L
scribes’ contention that their country’s fresli water at a
low temperature isn’t nearly so favorable to speed as the
warm sea water of Australia. They reckoned Champion’s
1.6 for the 100 at home was as good as 1.4 here. Cham-
pion failed to endorse this view, and swam no faster than
his home form indicated. N.S.W. reps, consider him
more a sprinter than a stayer.

The greatest worry that the manager of the
N.S.W. team of swimmers in Victoria had was keeping
boy Dick Cavill from surrounding all the tarts and such
like within reach at the various festivities.
* * * *
Second of P. A. Y. C.’s events for all yachts,
30 rating class, took place in Sydney harbour on Satur-
day. Course the usual one, extending over 16 2-5 miles
A fluky breeze (changeable from E. to S.E.) barely sup-
plied a turn to windward and made the thing drag. The
light “leading” blow favored Mr. J. O. Fairfax’s Magic
so much that she won quite easily, thereby showing un-
mistakably that others will always have their work cut
out to beat her under similar conditions. Magic looks
sure to appropriate the “ rub,” as (assuming she is given
the first race, which looks certain) her points will total 6,
the next being Meteor 3. Meteor is the only boat having
a chance to get level, and to do this she must win with
Magic out of a place.
* * * •
Eighteen craft lined up for Sydney Flying
Squadron’s General Handicap (Saturday) and provided a
fine spectacle. The light breeze favored big sails, which
had things their own way. 22-footer Plover (generously
treated by the handicappers) got home first in the final
with 18ft. Ira second and 25ft. Young Harry third.
* * ♦ *
“ Png ” : The Imperial troops did not inspire
wholesouled universal admiration in Melbourne, for
although the factory-girl who parades the South side of
Bourke-street “collared on ” to the swaggering rooineks,
and the church-going middle-class type of man beheld
them with mute adoration, a great number of adult male
gumsuckers were filled with wrath by the king-of-the-
kids air of the imported swaddies, and resented it with
bad language and pressing invitations to fight. Colonel
Wyndham complained on leaving that his men were con-
tinually provoked to pugilistic encounters, and not a few
were handed on board so badly damaged as to be unfit
for publication for several days. Of course the Colonel
heard only his men’s stories. Sometimes the provocation
came from the military gents. The writer saw one bulky
Scotchman who had “poked borak” at Australia and
Australians, taken on by a S. Melb. light-weight and most
artistically licked out of his kilts. Another fight was pro-
voked by the declaration of an Australian that the local
Permanent Artillery could give points to the visitors in
training, physique, and all else. This resulted in several
engagements, and in every instance the red and plethoric
strangers were taken on and done for by dry, wiry, brown
blackguards of local growth. Although the visitors were
drilled daily to keep them in nick, they seemed beefy and
stertorous, and the lack of science amongst them was
most surprising to those who have been led to believe
that, when in barracks, Tommy spends half his spare
time flourishing the gloves.

wanted to enlist, but ’twas no go The pancake-chested,
spider-underpinned devotees of the spiked-shoe are not
in demand as fighting material.

Now seems settled that 40 Australian bowlers
(16 from Vic., 14 from N.S.W., and 10 from M.L.) will go
’Ome next April. On the whole, they’re not a crack lot,
and anything but representative,' though sprinkled
among them there are a few good ones. Idea is that all
shall meet at some particular place in London, and make
that their headquarters. The team will not be picked till
then.

The second-grade cycle-tyre that is now being
manufactured by the Dunlop Tyre 00. is to be known as
the “ Oceanic tyre.” This name has been selected out of
15,000 submitted in connection with the Dunlop competi-
tion for a suitable name. The winning name was sent in
twice, and, strange to say, the two entries were received and
entered-up on the same date, so that the £2O-prize will be
divided. The prize-winners are : O. E. Brown, Grammar
School,Oampbelltown, N.S.W., and J. R. Warren, Mt. Ros-
kill, Auckland, M.L. A cheque for £lO has accordingly been
forwarded to each of the fortunate competitors As re-
gards the “Oceanic ” tyre : This tyre is being manufac-
tured by the Dunlop Tyre Co., to meet the existing
demand for a good-wearing tyre at a cheap figure Of
course, the tyre will not equal the high-grade Dunlop
but will be far superior to all other cheap-grade tyres :
m fact, the “ Oceanic tyre ” will rank as the next best
tyre to the Dunlop.*
* * * *
The decay of British bruising prestige is amply
evidenced by late news that Geo. Chrisp and Jack
Scales will contend for the English heavyweight
supremacy. Lightweight Dick Burge (never any good
except when the struggle was a rough one) Once
easily beat Scales in half-a-round, and ’Murkan “ Coffee
Cooler” (middleweight) settled Chrisp not long since
Last of England’s real champions dropped out nearly
three. decades back. None worthy the name have
materialised since. Huge, unwieldy Brummagem tough
Jem Smith, wore the mantle, but he belonged to the’
cart-horse variety, and couldn’t get out of his own road
U. Mitchell—though he virtually beat that big Yankee
make-believe, John L. Sullivan, at Chantilly once—
couldn t live four rounds in front of Jim Corbett. Better
have no champion at all and leave things as they were 30
years ago, unless something approaching the quality of
Fitzsimmons, Jeffries and co. can be trotted out.

S’African mails often bring news of fist-fights
won bv one-time Australian crack wrestler and axe and
club-swinger Tom Burrows. Burrows went to England
with Tom Williams after the latter won Australian light-
weight pug-supremacy from “ Gentleman ” George Daw-
son in Melb. Both were under the wing of Piesse of
Piesse and Lubin, the big scent-firm. Williams got awav
with a couple of good fights, but did little afterwards.
The two worked together at Aldershot for years as boxing
and wrestling instructors, and prospered. By-and-byl
Burrows went to Cairo to manage a big sporting venture
while Williams drifted to U.S., ind proved a7la Sy mark
in half-a-dozen battles. Burrows’ spec, failed, hence his
presence with the British troops. He wasn’t in the box-
ing business here; that—together with remarkable
endurance club-swinging feats- has been developed since
Big Melb. sport of 12 years ago “Jack” Dougharty
(since dead) was a thick-and-thin supporter of Williams
and Burrows.

* » * *
Though always credited with being a thorough
“ sport,” Albert Edward was never enthusiastic about
any form of athletics. He is said to have gone into the
cricket-field on only one occasion, when he was one of a
party of London smart youths visiting a Welsh backblock
township. A cricket-match was arranged, and the Prince
was put in the team, the captain of the local men warning
all his men to let the royal visitor pile up a few runs.
But, when the coming K. took his place at the crease, the
captain himself was bowling, and rocked-in a hot ’un
which sent the Prince’s bails flying. A London paper in
reporting the incident, said : The Prince advanced to
the wickets. Mr. bowled. The Prince retired to
the pavilion.” That was the one and only appearance of
the King in England’s national game.

(N.S.W.) racecourse is between the cemetery and the

Apropos Sydney’s old cemetery being busi-
nessised. The Melb. Saturday night Cheap Meat
Market is appropriately over part of the local old
cemetery, which was chopped off without a “By
your leave ”to the corpses. In turning upt the
skeletons, one was found in a digger’s red shirt
and corduroy trousers, with a rope round its neck.
The Jews objected to their graves being torn up.
They were punished by the Hebrew compartment
being turned into the curator’s fowl-yard. Years
ago his ducks were seen quacking and his fowls
clucking as they ran over flat tombstones in-
scribed with Hebraic characters. However, it’s
all nice and quiet now. ’ ■
Many curious and sombre-minded indivi-
duals have been tangling themselves in the wilder-
nesses of Devonshire-street cemetery, Sydney, for
days past in the hopes of seeing an exhumation,
but so far they have met with little success!
Apparently it is only the very old sepulchres whose
contents are mere dust that are disturbed by day.
All the same, the cemetery wears the outward
show of a partial resurrection going on. Every-
where old tombstones lie sideways and end-
ways. The cemetery seems to have extended
further than is now apparent, for the small
boys in a cottage garden not far off play at
funerals with an assortment of real bones dug up
in their ground and re-interred to the Marche
Funebre played on a tin-whistle, a toastmg-fork,
and a horn handled knife.
In Victoria and Tasmania the recent com-
bination of fierce gales with bush-fires has wrought
devastation only second to that of the historic
Black Wednesday. In the Western District
(Vic.) fully 10,000 sheep have been destroyed, one
station losing 3000 and another 2000. The des-
truction of crops has also been enormous, and up
to date five human lives have been sacrificed—
three of the victims being boys of about 12, who
in the confusion got surrounded by the flames and
perished together. Numerous fire-fighters are
also in hospital, severely—and in some cases, it-
is feared, fatally—burnt. The flames in this dis-
trict were at one stage continuous over 30 miles of
country, and another fire in the N.E. district of
Vic. covered a space of 25 miles by 12. In Tas-
mania the town of Queenstown lost about 60
houses, and the neighbouring district has been re-
duced to a blackened desert, and in Albury dis-
trict (N.S.W.) there has also been great destruc-
tion of crops and produce—with apprehended loss
of human life.
Thousands Have Piles.
THEY ARE EASY TO CUEE.
Perhaps there is no disease more irritating
and annoying than Piles. It is the result of
imperfect circulation of the hlood. The venous
blood settles in the veins of the rectum, and
expanding these veins produces the nodules
known as Piles. This is sometimes produced hy
an inactive liver, which does not permit the
blood to return freely to the heart; sometimes
it is the result of a weak action of the heart
itself, and often it is due to large masses of faeces
lodging in the bowels and pressing upon the veins,
so that the blood cannot return to heart and lungs.
This disease is not only most painful, but also very
damgerous, as the inflamed nodules are very apt to
take on malignant action, and cancer of the rectum
is produced. Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills, in
their action on the liver, heart, and bowels, strike
at all these causes of Piles and remove them.

At Poverty Point.
An experienced pro. on manufactured “successes”:—
“ You can take little notice of long London runs as a cri-
terion of the quality, the popularity, or attracting power
of light opera, musical burlesque, or any entertainment
of the sort. Success, from the manager’s point of view,
is not infrequently guaranteed by the rich ‘ protector ’
of the surprisingly versatile and wonderfully beautiful
young actress who has just been discovered, and who was
evidently designed by Providence to create the role of the
heroine in the charming new opera by So-and-so. In one
instance the doting admirer of a rather clever but very
imperious songstress, being exceedingly blessed with
riches, and lamentably lacking in brains, did not let up
until he had spent over £70,000 on a London ‘ success ’
for the particular pair of tights that had infatuated
him ”
T. P. O’Connor, in M.A.P., about the late Sir Arthur
Sullivan :—“ In recent years everything about the man
seemed to have shrunk until he seemed but the shrivelled
remnant of himself. But the brilliancy of those wonder-
ful black eyes—inherited from his Italian mother—always
remained, and their effect and beauty were heightened
by the deadly pallor of the small shrunken face.”
The late Arthur Sullivan got his first training in music
as a choir-boy at the Chapel Royal, St. James’ Palace.
Alfred Cellier was a chorister at St. Paul’s Cathedral,
London, several of the brightest performers in the
op eras of Sullivan and Cellier sang praises to the Lord in
surplices before their young voices cracked, °o, one way
and another, English comic opera owes a .tt to the
Anglican Church. Let no joyous singer speak disrespect-
fully of a bishop’s gaiters, for the religion of a Saumarez
Smith has done more for the stage than all the creeds of
the dour elders and the “ brethering” who chant through
their noses.
Clement Scott, in his Free Lance, reminisces about the
premiCre of “ Ecarte,” a comedy by Lord Newry, who
accompanied Edinburgh, York, and Co. to Australia.
Clem, says the manageress irrigated during a picnic on
the stage, with the real Maginnis, or Clicquot, instead of
the familiar gingerbeer. Consequently she came on the
stage wearing a red boot and a green one, and looking
as if her things would drop off generally, while she
made a squiffy oration, with tears —crying-drunk, in short.
Tay Pay O’Connor’s paper, “ M.A.P.,” states that Cart-
wright is an English Catholic, and the only member of
his family who was sufficiently worldly to get married.
His brothers are all priests, and his three sisters are nuns,
says “ M.A.P.” Some English Catholic families seem
addicted to that sort of thing : only one of the big family
of old Colonel Vaughan (father of Sydney’s R.O. Arch-
bishop) got married.

slobbery as might be expected, and he even tries to say a
kind word for Ellen Tree and Fanny Kemble, adding that
Lady Martin had a favorable opinion of Rachel ! The big
advt. for the book was to be the placing of Helen Faucit’s
bust alongside that of Shakespeare in Stratford Church,
but even the influence of Her Maj. could not pull that off.
Helen Faucit was the only individual in the profession
who had a poor opinion of 6. V. Brooke, with whom she
frequently acted ; he had a knack of annexing the lion’s
share of the applause by his rough methods. However
she was the finer artist. Brooke had the genius—what-
ever it was worth. Ooppin says—“ You never knew how
he would act.” Off the boards he'was almost a clod.
A London paper—apparently unaware that the late Sir
Arthur Sullivan’s original name was Solomon—refers to
“ his true Celtic liberality.” But Celt or Semite, the
deceased musician had munificent notions of coin-using.
He entertained royalty once, for instance, at Leeds in a
style which his conductor’s fee of £2OO (for the local
musical festival) didn’t go far to support ; and, .during
rehearsals, kept dashing to and fro between Leeds and
London in special trains, as the humor took him. He
would even take a special train to enable him to attend a
private dinner.
Related by Sarah Bernhardt that, in New York once,
there having been some talk of a small operation upon a
bad knee caused to her by kneeling on a rusty nail—a
Chicago museum-manager wired to her that, if her leg
had to be amputated, he would like to have it for his
show; and added that, if required, the severed limb
would be becomingly draped.
It was a treat for old-timers to see George Darrell take
the floor at Her Majesty’s (Melb.) last Friday afternoon.
George’s curious circular way of introducing his legs to
notice looked more original than ever. Many mimics
have imitated George’s voice, but nobody has had the
audacity to attempt to walk like the Sunny Southerner.
“ Haresfoot ” : Darrell says he has been twenty years
on the stage. Thirty-five. Youngest pro. I ever knew.
What a bonanza in the cinematograph-way the views of
Queen Victoria’s funeral ought to be !
The organist of St. Paul’s, London, states that the late
Sir Arthur Sullivan has left in his hands a fully-scored Te
Deum, to be sung on the conclusion of the S’African war,
whenever that may be !
Kenningham’s recent occasional disappearances from
the “Florodora” cast are due to the demon sciatica,
which originated in acute dyspepsia, brought about by the
iced drinks of America.
Clement Scott speaks reminiscently of the “ lovely
curse scene in “Leah," which gave Miss Bateman,
from America, the chance to take London by storm m
the ’6o’s. The great success of Kate Bateman had
great results. It happened at the Lyceum, and,
some years later, when her father resumed the
management of the theatre, he went for an
other coup of the same gloomy kind by pro-
ducing “ The Bells,” with Irving in the central
character. But for “Leah,” the peculiar
genius of Irving would probably have had to
wait much longer for such an opening as
“ The Bells ” gave him. Bateman was the
only London manager who could see a possible
fortune in the one-part play. The production
was a daring gamble ; Bateman had an un-
quenchable thirst for gambling. Much of the
money he made by Irving’s first triumphs in
“Bells,” “Charles the First,” and “Hamlet_
was said to have been lost at the card-table. He
died during the run of “ Hamlet,” leaving the lease
of the theatre and the more valuable lease of
Irving to his widow. The star actor’s salary had by
that time been raised from the original £2O, or so, to
£l5O a week.
Frank Fillis —at present in Glasgow, Scotland, with a
Savage S’African shovy—advertises that he is the Origin
nator and Inventor of S’Africa,” and that he has lived
in S’Africa for 22 years, and has been in personal contact
with Kruger, Steyn, Joubert, Botha, Cronje, and all the
other celebrities engaged in the present war, and is
thereby enabled to place before the British public a true
and faithful representation of life as it really is in
S’Africa.” For unassuming truthfulness, you can t
the circus-man! f

with a concert co., which, let us hope, will include

“ E.’’: Correspondent “Sfogato” doesn’t class me, I
hope, with the “ possibly venal scribes ” who represent
Marchesi as the greatest of all singing mistresses.. I
merely say she is a successful teacher with a connection
that is not limited to Australians and Americans, as
stated by “ Sfogato.” To give him the names of her most
promising French and Russian ( and German) pupils
would make him none the wiser, unless, perchance, one
of them has already scored a notable success in public.
I suggested that Marchesi’s critics were probably actuated
by personal prejudice. “ Sfogato,” for instance, appears
to have been at some pains to get evidence against her.
He quotes a French newspaper critique of her daughter
and pupil, Blanche Marchesi, as though it were neces-
sarily unprejudiced and honest, whereas it con-
tains evidence of malice in the query:—“ In what
school has Madame Marchesi learnt to produce
sound in such an exasperating fashion.” The writer
must have known that she had studied at her mother’s
school, so the affectation of ignorance can be taken to in-
dicate professional jealousy. Anyhow there is more than
one opinion about Blanche Marchesi. The Morning Post
of January 2nd noticing the New Year’s Day concert at
the Queen’s Hall (London) said : —“Madame Blanche Mar-
chesi was the vocalist. She sang Handel’s ‘ Ombra mai
fu ’ in a deeply expressive manner, and was obliged to re-
peat Parker’s song—‘The Lark now leaves his wat’ry
nest ’ —a clever and bright imitation of the old English
style.” Sfogato ignored the fact of Madame Blanche hav-
ing been a favorite London artist for the past year, or
more. Perhaps he didn’t know that she lately appeared
as Brunehild in “ Die Walknre,” at Prague, and scored a
big success.
The beauteous Forbes Robertson was quietly married
on Xmas Eve to Gertrude Elliott, who played small parts
with her sister (Mrs. Nat. Goodwin) in Australia, but who
has since become a leading lady in London. The real
family name proves to be Dermot.
The initiated get fresh entertainment from “ Florodora’
since Wallace Brownlow took to topical gags, which are,
presumably, encroachments on the low-comedian’s
preserves. Brownlow at the outset used to announce
himself merely as a Spirit of the Past, but now the bari-
tone Spirit adds the statement that he is looking for an
Old Age Pension. And he makes one or two other serious
attempts to be funny, as though he were competing
against Lauri for the jester’s cap and bells. Lauri accord-
ingly “ throws off ” a little at presumptuous Brownlow.
When he comes to feel the warbler’s head he professes to
discover the bumps of Humor and Beauty sticking out
like skittle-balls, and the victim in the chair doesn’t seem
very comfortable under the imputation.
Advertised that George Musgrove’s pantomime impor-
tations were only engaged for 16 weeks performance in
Australia. Probably the very shortest engagement ever
booked by an English artist was Clarence Leumane’s
original contract. He came out to fill the tenor part in
“Princess Ida” for six weeks at a big salary, first-class
return fare being guaranteed in the event of his not suit-
ing. But at the end of the term Leumane signed a fresh
contract, and was with the Firm for year afterwards with-
out a break.
C. M. Rodney, a “ variety ” entertainer who threatens
to give a new and original form of one-man show, will
open at Melb. Masonic Hall on March 2nd. Rodney was
well known in England of yore as a successful comic-song
writer and warbler, but for the past seven years he has
been a S’African identity, and he comes from that gore-
stained country with the reputation of being the first
professional humorist to appear in Mafeking after show-
man Baden Powell had concluded his long season. The
fact that Rodney didn’t come here to lecture on S’Africa
is evidence of his good faith in his own particular enter-
tainment, concerning the details whereof he preserves an
appetising mystery.
Not generally known that Titheradge once played lago
in Melbourne, at the Royal. It was George Ireland’s
benefit—Sept. 10, ’B6—the beneficiary appearing as the
Moorish soldier who smothers his wife.
The late Helen Vivian played Miami in “The Green
Bushes,” at Melb. Opera House, at the time when Essie
Jenyns was enjoying her Shakespearian boom at the
Bijou. Clever little Dora Mostyn made her first appear-
ance in Melbourne with the Vivians in the “ Bushes.”
Chas. Hoyt, who died lately of brain disease at a
palatial residence in ’Murka, represented the very
cheapest kind of talent for making money by stage-work.
He figured as author, composer, and manager of such
musical comedies as “ Chinatown ” and “ The Milk White
Flag,” wherein the human interest consisted largely of
the principal comedian’s thirst for “ cocktails.” Looking
back upon “ A Trip to Chinatown," the piece that
yielded £20,000 a year to Chas. Hoyt for five consecutive
years, one remembers nothing but its worthlessness. The
characters were contemptible, the dialogue trivial, and
the music third-rate stuff, whether original or otherwise.
Hoyt only knows how much of it was not absolutely
stolen from other third-rate musicians. The flat tenor’s
ballad (in “ Chinatown ”) concerning “ Nancy—his fancy,
sweeter than chicken ye gods '.—belonged to the lower
order of coon songs. As for “ The Bowery ” song—“ O, the
Bowery, the Bowery, did you ever hear tell of the
Bowery ? ” —it was set to the tune of “O, the Har-e m, the
Har-e m, did you ever hear tell of the Har-e m ? ” com-
posed by one Fred. Gilbert, a music-hall agent, in the
70’s. Men of the Hoyt stamp are calamities. With the
minimum of ability and the maximum of push and impu-
dence, they manage to bluff the public into accepting
hogwash for humor.
Artist Harry Furniss (who visited Australia a few
years ago) is touring the English provinces with an en-
tertainment entitled, “ Peace with Humour."
After the magnificent coronation of George IV., de-
cidedly the most expensive in English history, manager
Elliston bought all the props., and had the Coronation at
Drury Lane. He even got drunk in emulation of the
real King, and hiccuped to the pit, “Bless you, my
people 1 ” Be believed he was George IV. , just as George
believed the Charge at Waterloo.

Once upon a time the tenor and the baritone in a
musical co. operating at a metropolitan theatre had bitter
differences over a little damsel in the ballet, and hated
each other sorely. For some time each vented his feelings
in scornful disparagement of the other’s personal appear-
ance and artistic pretensions, but at length the insults
the tenor directed towards the baritone, and the baritone
returned to the tenor, went beyond human endurance,
and it was understood that Blood alone could atone —hot,
human blood 1 Neither quailed before the shocking
possibilities of the situation, and the quarrel reached its
climax in a long and particularly gory battle in the ring
at a local boxing-hall, the combatants being the baritone
and the tenor as represented by their dressers. When
the dressers had beaten each other into a jelly, there was
an affecting reconciliation between the principals, who
mingled their tears while the battered underlings were
being carried away to bed.
The “ Owen Hall ” who wrote the book of “ Florodora ”
is “ Teddy” Davis, formerly identified with the audacious
Bat newspaper. A criminal libel on Lord Lonsdale, or
Lady Lonsdale, or somebody, burst up the Bat and sent
the proprietor into exile at Boulogne. It was there that
a friend advised him to turn his pen to theatrical
account. Davis pointed out the absurdity of his hoping
to make any money by any work that bore his name.
His creditors would garnishee his author’s fees. “How
much do you owe and who are your creditors ? ” asked
the friend. Davis replied that he owed a lot of money to
everybody. His pen-name, “ Owen Hall,” was the out-
come of the discussion.
Mrs. Palmer was one of the sweetest Bohemian Girls
that Melbourne has heard. Under her maiden name,
Rosina Oarandini, she played the heroine in Balfe’s opera
22 years ago at the old Academy of Music.
Writes Springfield (U.S.) Republican “ Rachel was a
Jewess, Bernhardt is a Jewess, Edwin Booth was a Jew
by blood and inherited genius, Olga Nethersole, Emma
Oalv6, and Mrs. James Brown-Potter, if not Jews, were of
Jewish extraction. The greatest of all impresarios, Maur-
ice Grau, is a Jew. The most enterprising and powerful
of modern managers, Charles Frohman, is a Jew. The
most artistic and refined of theatrical directors, Daniel
Frohman, is a Jew. There are almost as many Jews con-
trolling the stage as there are employed in its perform-
ances. It is not impossible that the art of our theatre
shall become a separate province of the Jews. It is
not only possible, but probable, that the govern-
ment of its ventures may be engrossed by the
Jews. Our Hebrew friends have developed remarkable
aptitude for its affairs. They are shrewd students of
public taste, they are courageous in expenditure, they
are good losers, they are philosophic and abundant in
hope, they have not personal fads or fancies to force upon
the audience, they regard the entertainment of the people
as a legitimate business and conduct it on business prin-
ciples. Semitism seems to be the spirit of modern
advancement in the drama and music. Gentile manage-
ment has many faults of which Hebraic management is
free. Within the past dozen years we have observed
that once famous and prosperous Christians have
been forced by their own errors to relinquish control oi
play-houses that are now successfully conducted by Jews.
Managers who insist on giving entertainment which they
want, rather than providing amusement which the public
wants, have fallen by the wayside. The Jews have no non-
sense or selfish sentiment in the running of a theatre.
Certain Christian managers have compelled their
patrons to accept leading women with whom their
relations were meretricious; others have forced
their audience to endure actors and authors for
whom they had no approval. Such errors are not
Hebraic. ‘Hath not a Jew eyes?’cried Shylock. ‘Hath
not a Jew organs, senses, passions, affections ? ’ Verily,
but he does not allow them to be obtruded upon the
public. Business is business. A Jew’s romances and
friendships are as strong as a Christian’s, but they never
interfere with his purpose in carrying out any under-
taking to which his efforts are applied. The Jews are not
only hard-working, indefatigable, brainy managers ; they
are'self-respecting. Scandal, too frequent in tbe theatre,
seldom assails them. Certain of the chief managers of
England have been involved in disgraceful incidents.
‘ These,’ to quote Shylock again, ‘ these be your
Christian husbands.’ There has never been scandal in
public prints about our Jewish managers.”
Snazelle, talking of his youthful days, says he was a
dead failure at commercial-travelling. He got an engage-
ment with a French firm in London to push the sale of
“ side-spring ” boots, which were then a novelty and a
particularly good line for a novice to start upon. “ But,”
says Snazzy, “ the commercial-traveller who would suc-
ceed must be the possessor of an angelic temper, untiring
patience, and much perseverance in the face of rebuff and
insult. I could boast none of these things. My side-
springs brought me not a single order, but involved me
in 13 fights with blustering bootmakers.”

Miss Irene Vanbrugh who came to Australia in ’9l with
poor old Johnny Toole’s co.

Patrick Joyce, a resident of Gulgong
(N.S.W.) from its opening, died the other\lay.
For 30 years he had been blind. He was a news-
agent, and delivered his papers, without aid, never
making a blunder.
A significant news clip :
On Christmas Eve the Guild (of “Loyal Women”) will
"W wreathes on flowers of the graves of British and
Butch who have died of wounds or disease in Cape
Colony during the year, and special services will be held
in the churches. You would have thought that such a
kindly act would soften the hearts of even those women
whose hatred of the British is most virulent. Yet, so
bitter appears to be the feeling against us at the Gape
that ‘ A Dutch Woman ” in the South African News
protests violently, and resents the very idea of the
graves of her countrymen being touched by British hands,
and her letter actually appeared with the horrible head-
mg, “ Hands Off, you Ghouls ! ”

Interesting specimen of the British Curse,
as read out in a breach of promise case, in London,
just before Xmas. The lady had “carried on”
with the rather opulent defendant during his
wife’s life, and, some time after the latter’s death,
he refused to marry his other darling, as promised:
. May you be left lonely, friendless, wretched, and alone
m your old age, and on your deathbed be haunted by the
lives you have spoiled. Judas, how many times you have
betrayed me with a kiss ! May God’s deepest, 'bitterest
curse rest on you. It is a great consolation to me to know
that you will never get a woman to care for you ; you are
too old, ugly, and repulsive-looking. May you never have
another moment’s happiness in your contemptible life,
lou are no man. but a thing, a monkey. May the rest of
your life be a hell on earth !

Some More Preetrade Attitudes.
These three items were extracted last week from
•j ent i^ ore '^ n '* ra(^e Papers. When put
lection Sl^6 f° rm a rather interesting col-
W the Victorian tariff is adopted at the start it will
r> . * ea< l to an increase of factories dependent
on Protection, and it is an iniquitous proceeding on the
part ot any Government to induce people by a high tariff
t °/o\ t xr b ‘ Sh factories that are not self-supporting.
(z) Now, what was asked by Protectionists when first
they requested a trial for their policy ? Was it not “ Give
us Protection to enable our infant industries to get a
8 e “’ s< ?tf laC they will not be crushed out by the older-
established factories of other lands, more particularly
those of Great Britain”? Recognising what seemed a
reasonable request, all classes agreed to give what
1 rotectionists asked for, and Protection became the fiscal'
policy of the colony (Victoria). That policy has had a
eu-years trial. ... If Protection were required to start
industries in the first instance, should not some, at least,
iill* 'ndustries g 0 established, after being protected for
all these years, be in a position to say, “ We have been
protected long enough, and can now hold our own?”
Have we ever heard that said ? Are they not now crying
out for Protection under the Commonwealth as well, and
predicting ruin unless they are protected against the
W0 1 i after the trial Protection has had in Victoria,
protected manufacturers are still crying out for its con-
tinuance, does not the system stand self-condemned, and
show itself a proved fallacy ?
(3) Mr. Reid returned to Sydney yesterday after his
tour through Tasmania and Victoria. The Federal Free-
trade leader states that the revenue-tariffists will gain
nve seats in Victoria to the Protectionists’ one. In Vic-
toria Mr. Reid found a great revulsion of feeling against
Protection, especially among the farming class.
The latter item is rather a pathetic revival. That
P reetrade resurrection in Victoria has been pro-
phesied confidently at every election for goodness
knows how many years—like the end of the
world, and the millennium, and the triumph of
teetotalisin, and the total extinction of the human
race through drink, and the Second Coming, and
the collision of this globe with a comet, and the
destruction of human liberty owing to the machin-
ations of the Pope, and many other crazy tales,
yarns and prophecies. But the logic of the
r oreign-trade party, as shown by the above ex-
tracts, is even more wonderful than its prophetic
powers. It may be condensed thus :
(1) If the Common wealth adopts a scientific Protective
tariff that tariff will stay permanently. The people, who
are the ruling power of the Commonwealth, will be so
well satisfied with Protection when they give it a fair trial
that they won’t allow it to be removed. This proves that
it is bad.
(2) Victoria has had Protection for 30 years, and the
people are so well satisfied with it that they stick to it
persistently. The Assembly--the people’s House—is Pro-
tectionist almost to a man. The fact that the people have
found Protection to be too good to part with proves it to
be bad.
(3) At the same time the alleged fact that they are
going to part with it also proves it to be bad.
(4) But while the fact of a Protectionist community
being so wholly satisfied with Protection as to stick to it
persistently proves it to be bad, and though the fact of
a Protectionist community abandoning Protection (as
Britain did) proves Protection to be worse, and though
the fact of a Freetrade community taking to Protection
(as did Germany, the United States, and very many other
places) likewise proves Protection to be worst of all, the
rule doesn’t work both ways. If a Freetrade community
sticks to Freetrade that proves that Freetrade is good.
If a Freetrade community abandons Freetrade, that also
proves that Freetrade is good.
(5) Some too-enthusiastic Protectionists in the early
Victorian days thought that Protection alone would
abolish poverty. It has certainly enabled Victoria to pay
the highest wages in the Eastern States, and supply three
times as much employment as does any other piece of
ground of its size in Australia. But‘it didn’t wholly
abolish poverty, as one or two wildly - irresponsible
persons thought it would—therefore it is utterly rotten.
It was promised in N.S.W. that Freetrade would abolish
the unemployed in 24 hours, that it would make everybody
Jolly and Fat, and that it would make N.S.W. the highest
wages State in Australia. It did none of these things,
but this all-round failure only further proves the immor-
tal truth of Freetrade.
(6) The Freetraders’ theory is, that the only way you
? ( a ” that Protection is right is by abandoning it.
If the industries which were built up under Protection
are sound,” they say, “ prove it by removing the Protec-
tion, and thus demonstrate that they can stand alone.”
Britain abandoned Protection, and showed that its Pro-
tection-built industries were sound by keeping them
afloat, even if they didn’t grow quite so fast as in the old
Protective days. But this, strangely enough, is the Free-
trader’s strongest evidence that Protection is wholly
wrong. J
Many a man has come to grief through mixing his
drinks, and the party which so woefully mixes its
lies is aptto come to grief also in the long run.
The other day in N.S.W. Leg. Council Dr. Creed
drawing attention to “The Commercial Value
of Needlework to N.S.W.”—a small pamphlet re-
cently issued by the Singer Manufacturing Co.—•
pointed out, with regard to the present system of
teaching needlework in the State schools, that it
is entirely a system of hand-work, inaugurated
many years ago and very little changed since.
When it was instituted skill in hand-work
was essential, machines being then very expensive
and of less general utility than the experts in
hand-sewing. But the conditions, it is pointed
out, have since entirely altered, machine-work
having now completely superseded hand-sewing,
lhe hand system having thus lost its value, the
teaching of it, it is contended, is a sheer waste of
public money, so that by non-training in machine-
work N.S.W. swells the number of her unem-
ployed and lets workers trained in other lands
sew for her. Thus in 1898 N.S. Wales paid for
ready-made clothing, £900,218; for millinery
£60,206 ; for boots and shoes, £216,650 ; for
boot uppers, £5868; and for gloves, £84,654. The
99 figures were much larger, and the pamphlet
goes on to advise that our young folk should be
taught to cut out, fix and make their garments by
machinery, and places the need of teaching the
art of clothing the body upon a footing of equal
importance with that of feeding it, as already
taught in the public-school cookery-classes. The
Department would thus—it is summed-up—reap
substantial advantages, and as an evidence of the
superior value of machine-work, are instanced the
bales of needlework sent from the public-schools
for the S’African war, and rendered -possible only
through the allowed temporary use of machines.
The recognition by England of the necessity of so
training the young as to increase the commercial
power of the country has recently been dwelt
upon by .Rosebery and Chamberlain, and the said
necessity is surely even greater in a new country
in the first stages of its development.
The Sydney working carters and drivers
have started a union, with an initial membership
of 500. “Such an association was urgently needed,
for these men—with their practically unlimited
labor-hours—have been amongst the most shame-
fully sweated in Sydney.

tralia to wake up re accommodation at the National
Parliament. There will not be room for more
than a dozen in the first row. I suggest, right off
that Melb must only have two seats, with two
ipiece each for Sydney, Brisbane, Adelaide
Hobart, and Perth. A fight will ensue over this-
A gallery committee should be formed at once
representative of all the cities, unless a new Press
Association is to furnish reports. I don’t see how
that will be endured. Of course Melb. is not
essentially more interested than any other city.

dailies had disingenuously refused to print. The
letter had, however, appeared in the Daily
Telegraph of the 7th February, and it is
unfortunate for S. M. Herald that it
should have shut-down as regards, per-
haps, the most cogent and telling contr bution
to the discussion that has yet appeared. All the
same—boiled down—the position, as stated by
father Masterson, amounts simply to this. Both
Catholic and Protestant theologians admit that
statement may sometimes rightfully disagree with
fact; but, whereas the Protestant says you may
m certain circumstances tell a downright lie, the
Catholic says—“No! you musn’t do that: but
you may tc!! it by implication.” Tweedledum
and Tweedledee!

According to Dr. Mailler Kendall’s report
upon Sydney’s health during last year, there has
been a slight decrease in the phthisis deaths upon
those of 99 but an increase in mortality from
diphtheria, diarrhoea and typhoid. Of the last-
named disease there were 1031 oases, as against
795 in 99, 883 in ’9B, and 471 in ’97. Typhoid-
more to be feared than the plague,” the report
during last year “ occurred in supposed
Healthy zones, showing clearly that in order to
accurately locate each case it will be necessary to
know where each individual worked or passed his
time during the day.”
Out of 59 carts employed in the work of
street-maintenance in Sydney, more than half are
widows carts,” kept on in a charitable sort of
way because the owners’ late husbands were in the
city s employ. The trouble is that when the hired
driver who acts as the widow’s representative,
often at a miserable wage, gets drunk or neglects
his work, nobody cares to fine the owner because
she is a widow, so the widows’ carts do very little
street maintenance, and are chiefly useful to give
the horse fresh air and gentle exercise. The new
Council says, in a despairing sort of way, that it
doesn t want to be hard on any widow, but the
work must be attended to. Why shouldn’t the
city own its own carts and do its own main-
tenance ?


Three Years
Intense Suffering.
The Case of Mr. H. M. KEYEY.
(By a Special Reporter.)
All government, indeed every human
benefit and enjoyment, every virtue and
every prudent act, is founded on com-
promise and barter. To promote our
personal welfare we balance inconveni-
ences, preferring to give and take in
order to attain our ends. We remit
some® rights so that we may enjoy
others, and we choose rather to be happj
independent citizens than servile ten-
ants. Great sacrifices'are made by wor
kers who aspire to become their owi
landlords for the advantages to be de
rived from the attainment of their as
pirations. “My illness,” said Mr. Henn
Moran Kevey, of G arfield-street, Par
neil, Auckland," was brought on througl
working night and day at my sedentarj
trade of bootmaking. The object I had
in view was to purchase a home for my
wife and family; but I stuck to my task
so closely that it caused me three years
intense suffering. It is eight years age
since my constitution was completely
broken down.”
“You look strong and vigorous now,”
aid the reporter.
“And I feel as well as I look. But
Here was a time when I thought I was
roing into consumption. My face was a
deathly colour, and my eyes were sunk
deeply into my head. You would not
have given twopence for my life if you
had seen me then. Before I was struck
down I thought of nothing but work,
and I took my meals at all hours, some-
times going without them altogether.
I felt myself getting weaker and
weaker every day, till I could
hardly crawl down to my shop
A few minutes after starting work
I felt giddy and faint, and I was obliged
to take frequent rests during the day
Then my digestive organs went wrong
as shown by the tightness in my chest
after eating, and also great pain and
discomfort in my stomach, which felt as
though I had swallowed some foreign
matter which was turning sour and pu-
trid. Indeed, my breath bore out this
theory, as it was most objectionable,
and my mouth had a very bad taste
continually, which made me feel sick.
Sharp pains used to dart under my
shoulders and down to the end of my
ribs, and in a short while I was unable
to retain a meal on my stomach. As
soon as I ate anything I was seized with
violent fits of vomiting, and the pain
and distress I suffered was beyond de-
scription. I felt tired and miserable,
being- without enough energy to sit
down and read a book. As a matter of
fact, my eyesight was too bad, and my
eyes seemed to have little spots danc-
ing before them, besides being hot and
swollen.”
-ow^ aid you manage to keep on
working in that condition ?”
! I could not do a stroke of work at fast
I had to knock off altogether. 1
tel t heavy and drowsy, and suffered
greatly from splitting headaches. M 3
bowels were irregular. For days thej
were constipated, then followed a weak-
attack of d arrhcea. I found great
difficulty m swallowing anything, even
suen a simple thing as a cup of tea
making me conscious of the fact bha<
my throat was very sore. My appetite
went right off eventually, and my ner-
vous system then collapsed. An addi-
tional complaint in the form of a vile
attack of neuralgia now commenced to
worry the life out of me. Shooting pains
darted from the ear to my eyes, and
thence to my forehead. The agony was
30 acute that I could get no rest day or
flight I twisted and turned from side
side all night long, and in the morn-
-1 s . to ° exhausted to get out of
bed. This new torture continued for
months, till at last I thought I was
going mad.® A peculiar feature of my
dreadful llness was the way my tongue
used to swell. It felt too thick for my
mouth, and almost choked me. My
brain wis full of evil-forebodings as I
lay throughout the sleepless nights in ■
a state of nervous terror. Many a time
I have awakened my wife from her slum-*
ber and told her that the tortures of
my complaint were driving me crazy,
and in the day time I felt so greatly run
down that I used to say to my wife, ‘I
suppose I will live through this summer,
but if I do I am sure I will die during
the winter.” One night my condition
was so alarming that my wife went
straight away for the doctor, who said
I had a diseased liver, and that my
blood was in a terribly bad state.”
“Was there anything to show that
your blood was impure ?”
“Rather. I will tell you, and then
you will know that my blood was poi-
soned. It happened through wearing
a soft woollen hat, the rims of whim
used to fall down on my ears. I used to
wear a blue serge suit, and my better
half told me I ought to get a hat to
match the colour. As I always study
comfort before appearance I bought the
soft one alluded to. _ The result was
that my ears were poisoned by the dye
out of the hat. My ears swelled and
brof e out, and my agonies increased
ten fold. I used to pray for death to
release me from my sufferings; but no,
I had to endure it. The tops of my ears
were eaten away, as you can see for
yourself, and even when they healed up
I believe I had enough poison in my
blood to kill twenty men. I was really
on the verge of death by now, without
animation, without hope.”
“Was there any benefit derived from
your doctor’s medicines ?”
“Not the slightest. Neither did I re-
ceive any benefit from the many adver-
tised remedies I tried; but I was saved
by the advice of a friend, who urgm me
to give Clements Tonic a trial. I did
so, but I can honestly say that had it
not been for Clements Tonic I would
be a dead man now. I started on that
matchless medicine with a feeling ot
great anxiety, as I knew my fate de-
pended on it. My anxious fears were
soon set at rest, as I felt greatly soothed
and stimulated in a few days. My case
was a bad one, and I could not expect
to be rid of all my ills for some time;
but Clements Tonic proved equal to the
great task before it. A gradual slacken-
ing off of the sharp pains in my back
and loins, besides the absence of discom-
fort in my stomach, showed that
Clements Tonic was doing the work in
first-rate style. My appetite, which had
so long lain dormant put in a feeble ap-
pearance at first, and then increased
every day till I could eat a hearty meal.
My head was again free from aches, and
every morning I awoke feeling decidedly
better for the sleep which resulted
through my continuance of Clements
Tonic. My nervous system was streng-
thened and invigorated by the same
medicine, which eradicated all skin erup-
tions and purified my blood, besides
driving away the raking neuralgia, which
has never since troubled me. Each
day I gathered weight and strength,
meanwhile shaking off the fit of melan-
cholia which has depressed me for three
and my work was undertaken
vith the same pleasure as of old. Cle-
nents Tonic gave me perfect health,
.'hen all else failed.”
“Was the cure a permanent one?”
“Yes, and I am anxious that every-
body should know that such a grand
nedicine exists, so you may publish
hese facts in any way you like.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, Henry Mohan Kevet, of Garfield Street,
Parnell, Auckland, in the colony of Ne» Zealand,
lo solemnly and sincerely declare that I have care-
ully read the annexed document, consisting oi
hree folios and consecutively numbered from one to
three, and that it contains and is a true and faithful
recount of my illness and cure by Clements Tonic,
and also contains my full permission to publish in
any way my statements—which I give voluntarily,
without receiving any payment; and I make this
solemn declaration conscientiously believing the
same to be true, and by virtue of the provisions of
an Act of the General Assembly of New Zealand,
intituled “The Justices of Peace Act, 1882."

A recent row at Cardiff (Eng.) Infirmary
arose through the bitter lamentations of a North
of Ireland man whose arm had been amputated.
His ground of complaint lay in the fact that the
limb in question had been buried with the body
of a Catholic.
The late Mountmorganaire, James Wesley
Hall, left to his widow a legacy of £lO,OOO, also
an annuity of £2OOO and an allowance not exceed-
ing £lOOO per annum for the education and main-
tenance of each of his children —all this free from
the control of any husband, should the beneficiary
re-marry. He left to his three sisters-in-law—
Georgina Dempster, Maria Dempster, and Jenny
Madeline Elma Hooper—looo shares each in Mt.
Morgan mine. After providing for other legacies,
the residue of the estate is bequeathed to his
children, in equal shares.
“ Puyyaka ” concerning a medical catas-
trophe :
The recent cabled mishap in Milan (Italy) whereby a
number of people died of lock-jaw after injection of anti-
diphtheria serum, looks like culpable carelessness in the
laboratory, judging from the cabled accounts. With all
manner of cultures and of serums in different stages of
preparation lying about, the details of labelling, &c., are
often left to an attendant, usually of the ordinary rule-
of-thumb variety. In the present instance it looks as if a
cultivation of tetanus had been substituted for tl e
diphtheria anti-toxin, or at any rate mixed with it after
the final attenuation. Had it been otherwise the horse
used for attenuation would have shown symptoms suffi-
cient to warn the experimenters. Anti-tetanic serum is
extensively made in manufacturing laboratories, and early
cultures, while virulently active, closely resemble prepared
anti-diphtheritic serum in outward appearance. The
mistake must have led to some horrible tortures, since
tetanus carries perhaps the most dreadful death in the
whole gamut of disease, arid even the minority who re-
cover long remain shattered in mind and body. The
possibilities of mischief by careless or ignorant people in
bacteriological laboratories are unlimited, and although
the effects generally fall upon the bungler himself, the
Milan case shows that they may extend further. In
Vienna a few years ago the drunken habits of an attend-
dant were the direct cause of the deaths of three people
(one a noted bacteriologist) from plague, and might have
been the means of infecting the whole city.

Three Little Australians.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“He’s nearly dead,” Nan said, mournfully, but
her eyes sparkled again as she saw the massive tail
almost sweeping the ground.
“His ribs are getting bigger and bigger, and
his head’s hanging lower and lower. Do horses
go to heaven when they die ?■” Em asked, touched
by the condition of the horse.
“Yes,” Ted volunteered, “Mr. Trundle said
there was a separate compartment for animals,
and the man at the gate pointed out the way.
’Spec it’s something like drafting. The man at
the gate’d be like the man at the raoe. He drafts
’em off into different yards. If it’s a man he
opens the gate one way, an’ if it’s a beast he
opens it another.
“ An’ wot if it’s a woman ?'
“Oh, you fool, isn’t a woman the same as a
man ? She’d go into the man’s yard.”
“ No, she wouldn’t, she’d go in a nicer one, cause
she’s better.”
“ Swim-out! ” was Ted’s answer.
“Me an’ Nan’s better ’an you, ’cause you
swear.”
“Men always swear. They’re grander than
women. ’Tisn’t wicked for them to swear, ’cause
it comes out easily. ‘ Damn ’’d stick in a woman s
throat. That shows it’s wicked for her to swear.
An’ if you don’t go in with us you’ll have to go m
with the beasts, ’cause there’s only two yards.”
“He won’t die to-day,” Nan said, indicating
the horse. “Come on home.”
* * * *
“ There he is, lying down.”
The three made a rush towards the horse.
“ Is he dead, Nan ? ” Ted said.
“ I don’t know ; wait till I ffeel his heart.”
But Ted made a rush for his tail and began to
pull.
“Let go,” Nan said savagely. “ You’re not to
touch him till we’re sure he’s dead. Besides, the
tail’s mine.”
“’Tisnot. I saw him down first, ihe tans
mine.”
“ It’s mine ! I’ve been watching him for a
week. I found out he was sick first. You
wouldn’t have known only I told you.
“ I’ll have his mane then! ” put in Ted.
“ An’ what’ll I have ? ” whined Em.
“You can have the tail of the next,” was Nan’s
answer. But Ted suggested she should have the
hoofs.
“I shan’t! ” indignantly returned Em. Ihe
Chinaman won’t buy them.”
“Yes, he will. Don’t you remember in the
first book where it says ‘ the. hoofs are good to
make combs to comb our hair with ’ ” repeated
Ted.
“ ’Tisn’t ; it’s ‘ the horns are used to make
combs to comb our hair with.’ ”
“Well he’s got no horns, so you better take his
hoofs. You can tell him they’re used to make
combs with, he won’t know the difference.”
“ Won’t he ! ” put in Nan. “ He’s always wink-
ing his eye as if he knows a lot.”
“Well, you ask him, Em. They’re good to
make glue, anyhow. I’m sure he’d give you a
ha’penny for the lot.”
“ A ha’penny ! An’ you an Nan’ll get sixpence
between you. I want some hair.” Em began to
howl.
“ Is he dead ? ” Ted asked again.
“ He’s lying on his left side, so I can’t feel his
heart. We’d better wait a bit and see if he
moves.”
“When I was sick the doctor felt my pulse,
and Ted made a rush for the front foot. “No,
it’s not beating, Nan, he must be dead.”
Nan began to pull out the tail while Ted tugged
at the mane.
“The hoofs won’t come off,” Em said, grunt-
ing. “ How do you get ’em off, Ted?” Then she
caught sight of two bright shoes on the hind feet.
“Oh!” she yelled, “look—the shoes are good
ones. I’ll get tuppence each. That’ll be more
than you or Nan.” Excitedly— “ They’re mine !
they’re mine ! The shoes are mine.”
Nan and Ted came round to look. Yes, there
was no doubt of it. There they were, nearly new,
the catches still on them.
“But you can’t get ’em off,” Ted said, jealously.
“I can; I’ll bring a chisel and a hammer. I
saw Tom take some off his horse one day.”
Away went Em, returning in about half an
hour with the required tools.
By this time Nan and Ted had extracted every
hair from the mane and tail, leaving the horse a
pitiable sight.
Em took no notice of the look of the carcase.
It held no horror for her. All she saw were two
shining shoes, or was it four black pennies in the
Chinaman’s pocket ? .
After much grunting, and straining and pulling
she succeeded in getting them off.
The three set off with their separate burdens.
• « * *
That was a very long time ago, and we are all
K own-up now. Only the other day Ted came
ck from shooting Boers in Africa, and Em and
I went to see “ the procession.” Em said it was
grand.
