The Bulletin hears that a Sydney
suburban boarding-house, which received from
Govt a Commonwealth-guest retainer for £6O,
had to take in but two State guests. “Our
bleeding country,” indeed!
The board of directors of N.S.W. Aus-
tralian Natives’ Association has carried a resolu-
tion expressing the opinion that the position of
Svdney Town Clerk should be filled by an Aus-
tralian citizen, and requesting the Mayor to
receive a deputation on the subject. All very
well but if the A.N.A. can’t find an Australian
citizen who can write better grammar than that
in the address the A.N.A. sent to Lord Hopetoun
on the Queen’s death, the A.N.A. aforesaid can-
not reasonably object to some intelligent and well-
educated Chinaman filling the billet.
“Pastoral and Commercial Report” in
Bourke (N.S.W.) Herald of 15th Jan.
Cool weather, certainly, for this time of the year, but
we would sooner have a heat wave averaging 120 deg. m
the shade than the continual wind and dust-storms that
we have been treated to for the past three months or
more Whirlwinds are the rule, dust-fine, insinuating
dust amalgamates with pur daily food; the river water is
like nea-soup, and oh ! for a hath in clean water. When
will this dog-goned, dreadful drought end. There
will not he a live hoof, excepting those that are
hand-fed in the district directly. What is the result to
be ’ Simply this. We will get rain sometime between
this and the next decade, and all those who have not died
from starvation will succumb from fright.
Our Mine.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Sam and I were sent down by a Sydney syndicate
to try a reef near Andon. They were an Unre-
liability Co. and we knew it, but we were tired of
working in the sewers, and bosses had a habit ol
asking us to leave, anyhow.
Sam infused some energy into the secretary of
the syndicate (a barber) by styling him the Legal
Manager, and we left Sydney with £lO and got
our fares paid to the nearest railway-station to the
The reef was three miles from a pub ; but we
partly overcame this inconvenience by camping
half-way between. Then we started to make
inquiries over the bar about the original pros-
pectors and the reef. The landlord, as he changed
a fiver for Sam, predicted a great future for the
mine. Sam said it was likely-looking country
for gold, and we did some preliminary prospecting
between the camp and the pub, and camped-out
several nights without blankets.
Then we started to select a site for a new shaft.
There was a shaft already sunk on the reef, about
20ft. deep; but Sam decided that it was not sunk
in the proper place, so we began to sink another
near a shady tree. The old shaft was in the full
glare of the sun. I did the sinking, and Sam
read up some mining literature in the shade, un-
til the sfyaft required a windless. We had sunk
12ft. when the handle came out of the windlass
(our own make), and it and the bucket fell on me.
Sam was not hurt, but stuck to the handle man-
fully, and, after he hauled me up, went on crack-
ing quartz and spitting on it, as usual. He, how-
ever, decided that the shaft needed timbering.
He said there were several ways to timber a shaft,
but that I had better do it my own way, and I
did it that way. The reef had a big underlay, or
else it was a knock-kneed reef, and the bucket
used to drag out some of the timber, but I had
the shaft constructed on safe principles, and used
to suspend all operations whilst the bucket was
going up or coming down, which, however, was
not often. ,
Sam was boss, and it was through a relation ol
his that we got the appointment. The aforesaid
relation had to pretty well support Sam, so he dis-
tributed Sam’s keep amongst a mining syndicate.
I lent Sam a pound, and, as I seemed the only one
likely to lend him another, he selected me as his
mate. Neither of us had ever done any mining,
but Sam was a liar of a high order.
His first report read: “Sinking on a body ol
ore, payable quality ; reef, 3ft. wide; in well-
defined walls.” Brief reports, Sam said, were the
best; but still our wages were in arrear, and the
publican was no longer effusive in his manner
towards us. .
People of various occupations used to call.
They all said they could see no gold in the stone,
but Sam said it was mineralised stone, and that
gold was not visible in that kind of stone.
An unforeseen occurrence, however, disturbed
things. We saw gold in the stone; it was only
one color ; still, even Sam could tell it was gold,
so he decided to go down the shaft and inspect
the new development. He tied himself securely
in the rope and got me to lower him. It was his
first time down, and he was pale with excitement
—so he said—at the discovery. Presently he
called on me to heave-up. I got him to the sur-
face with such vigour that he “ concussed badly
against the windlass. There was not much skm
left on his hand, or much bad language left that
he didn’t use—but he never mentioned how the
accident happened in his next report, which was_:
“Had bad accident; hand severely crushed.
Discovered rich shoot of gold ; will have to put
on man. Will personally superintend opera-
tions, however.”
This report brought down our money and the
man’s wages, and the publican seemed sorry for
past coolness.
We had been using dynamite—in our reports—
for some time, but now there was a probability of
having to use it in earnest. Sam ordered rhe to
bore a hole in the reef. That hole took some
time, as I tapped the drill very lightly so as not
to hurt my hand too much when I missed the
drill. Sam told me to put plenty of stuff in the
hole, but I “struck” when it came to lighting
the fuse, as a coil of fuse would not reach to the
surface. Sam overcame that difficulty by-getting
me to put some dry bushes down the shaft, and
then dropping a firestick on them. This idea
worked well, but we forgot to take off the wind-
lass. The explosion was more thoughtful, and
after it was over we required some new rope. I
thought we also required a new shaft, a*nd went
on strike again.
About this time the company wanted us to for-
ward some specimens. There was also some talk
of the legal manager bringing down a speculator
with a view of placing the' mine on the London
market. Sam was very indignant and threatened
to resign if they interfered with his management.
I thought it very probable they would interfere,
but I was strong on having a new shaft and buy-
ing some specimens to pacify the directors.
Sam placed his view of the matter before the
company. The mine was too good to sell. It
was only mines that were worked-out or else no
good that were put on the London market. He
would not take £5OOO for his interest in the mine
—a twelfth. This report put matters on a more
solid basis for us ; and, after an elaborate survey,
Sam fixed a site for a straight-shaft to cut the
underlay at 100 feet. The site selected for the
new shaft was nearer to the public-house than the
old one. . , , ,
Then Sam, in a burst of excitement, raked-up
some specimens and forwarded them to Sydney.
The co. was so pleased that it came up bodily and
inspected the property. And now Ham afraid
there is nothing else for it but the sewersagam.
YOKEL.
Trooper Fred Smith, of Wallabodah, near
Quirindi, N.S.W., has in his possession a silver
trowel, presented to the late Gen. Joubert on the
occasion of the laying of a Transvaal church
foundation-stone. The returned trooper found it
one day, while at the front, in following up a
Boer retreat.
As—according to R.C. Bishop Doyle—
three-fourths of the Irish priests barrack for
Healy, it is at least doubtful whether Redmond
and his United Irish League will be able to down
“the hell-cat” Timothy. The Tory papers
attribute the recrudescence of boycotting to this
same League. And—allowing for prejudice—the
notion receives color from the fact that, at latest,
there were about 300 farms lying idle in Ireland,
their previous owners having been evicted and
nobody venturing to take them up. And, any-
.how, if Parnell himself had to go down before the
priests, what show has Redmond ?

The Maori’s Wool.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Now, this is just a simple tale to tell the reader
how
They civilised the Maori tribe at Rooti-iti-au.
The Maoris are a mighty race—the finest ever
known ;
Before the missionaries came they worshipped
wood and stone ;
They went to war and fought like fiends, and
when the war was done
They pacified their conquered foes by eating every
one.
But now-a-days about the pahs in idleness they
lurk,
Prepared to smoke or drink or talk—or anything
but work.
Around the hills their flocks of sheep are fed on
tribal lands —
A communistic ownership that no one under-
stands.
The richest tribe in all the North in sheep and
horse and cow
Were those who led their simple lives at Rooti-
iti-au.
’Twas down to town at Wellington a noble Maori
came,
A Rangatira of the best, Rerenga was his name—
(The word Rerenga means a “snag”—but until
he was gone
This didn’t strike the folk he met—it struck them
later on).
He stalked into the Bank they call the “ Great
Financial Hell,”
And told the Chief Financial Fiend the tribe had
wool to sell.
The Bold Bank Manager looked grave—the price
of wool was high.
He said, “ We’ll lend you what you need—we’re
not disposed to buy.
You ship the wool to England, Chief! You’ll
find it’s good advice,
And meanwhile you can draw from us the local
market price.”
The Chief he thanked him courteously and said
he wished to state
In all the Rooti-iti tribe his mana would be great,
But still their tribe were simple folk who might
not understand
This strange financial jugglery which gave them
cash in hand
Before the wool was sold at all ; and even after
sale
Perhaps they mightn’t get the price—the notion
turned him pale !
So off he started home again, with trouble on his
brow,
To lay the case before the tribe at Rooti-iti-au
They held a great korero in the Rooti-iti clan,
With speeches lasting half a day from every lead-
ing man.
They called themselves poetic names—“lost
children in a wood ” ;
They said the Great Bank Manager was “ Kapai ”
—extra good !
And so they sent Rerenga down, full-powered and
well-equipped,
To draw as much as he could get, and let the wool
be shipped,
And wedged into a “Cargo Tank,” full up from
stern to bow—
A mighty clip of wool went Home from Rooti-
iti-au.
It was the Bold Bank Manager who drew a heavy
cheque ;
Rerenga cashed it thoughtfully, then clasped him
round the neck.
A hug from him was not at all a thing you’d call
a lark.
You see he lived on mutton-birds and dried re-
mains of shark.
But still it showed his gratitude, and, as he
pouched the pelf,
“ I’ll ‘ haka ’ for you, sir,” he said. “In honor of
yourself ! ”
The “haka” is a striking dance —the sort they
don’t allow
In any place more civilised than Rooti-iti-au.
He “haka’d” most effectively—then, with an
airy grace
Rubbed noses with the Manager, and vanished
into space.
But when the Wool-return came back, ah me,
what sighs and groans !
For every bale of Maori wool was loaded up with
stones !
Yes —thumping great New Zealand rocks among
the wool they found ;
On every rock the Bank had lent just seven pence
a pound.
And now the Bold Bank Manager, with trouble
on his brow,
Is searching vainly for the chief from Rooti-iti-au.

THE BANJO.
Victoria’s Viticultural College at Ruther-
glen—with nearly 1000 acres of the best vine-
growing land, class-rooms and accommodation for
40 students, casks, implements and machinery,
horses and buggies, large brick wine cellars,
presses, distillery, laboratory, sterilising appara-
tus, &c. —boasts, at present, Of just one student.
It has seldom had more in the 10 years of
existence, though once, it actually did have no less
than sin 3 years. The fortunate youth aforesaid
is about the most extravagantly-educated student
on the face of the earth. For his sole benefit the
State provides a viticultural expert at £3OO a
year, an overseer at £l6O, a gardener, a carpenter,
a blacksmith, a groom, a housekeeper, two lads,
nine laborers and a type-writer ! The Bulletin
hopes she is a nice girl. Also 24 acres of vines 9
years old, 9 at 3, and 15 at 1 year, besides 3 acres
of nursery and a farm. There has never been
anything like this since the time when, after
scores of thousands had been spent on Sydney
University, all it could show for the money was
kts first graduate—the late Judge Windeyer.
"Laborare Est Orare.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I took rather a fancy to that small house —while
it was building.
When it was allegedly finished I saw it was two
houses —a builder’s beguilement of the unwary
into the delusion that they were not practically
11 pigging” with their next-door neighbor. There
were two squat little balconies jammed together,
two mean little front doors, with two skimpy por-
ticoes, two slips of sand before and behind, and so
on. It was naturally advertised as “ Two Genteel
Villa Residences.” Sheol and Sydney are just
full of such gentility in badly-baked bricks and
rotten mortar. I did not take it, but rented a
more commodious cottage in the vicinity. But
because I might have become the tenant of the
genteel rabbit-hutch, I thereafter took a languid
interest in it.
* * * *
The thing was let at last to two families.
The occupants of the right half I never saw.
I have a theory that the snores of the other people
kept them awake all night, and that they
accordingly did their sleep in the day-time. One
morning early, however, an unctuous, smooth-
faced man of uncertain age, wearing a large mouth
which wobbled, and a walking-costume, even to the
gloves thereof, appeared on the slip of sand in
front of the left villa. He was accompanied by a
small bucket full of water, and a white jug, pro-
bably used to take in the family milk, water, and
tuberculosis.
He was watering his “ front garden ” with the
jug. The garden might possibly have accommo-
dated twelve soup-plates placed edge to edge in
four rows of three. My real impression is that
nine soup-plates would have been crowded. When
the horticulturist detected that I curiously re-
garded him, he blushed turbidly, his ample lips
waggled, and he precipitated himself within
doors, carrying the jug to safety but leaving the
bucket to its fate.
I made a point of looking at him attentively
ever after—to encourage him.
When, on the same day I first discovered him,
I passed homewards to lunch, the toiler was still
at it, and there was quite an amount of water
slopped around. As there was no sign of digging
it seemed evident that he contemplated a “lawn”
only an oasis per favor of the Water and Sewer-
age Board. He caught my eye again, reddened,
tried to yawn indifferently, failed and retreated,
holding on to his jug. But he was there in the
afternoon, perspiring profusely. He had taken
off his gloves.
* * * *
It very day, and at all hours, that devoted man
might have been seen plugging away at his
miniature desert with bucket and jug. In very
hot weather, and then only, he relinquished his
coat, always hastily resuming it and coloring pain-
fully and retiring if he caught my eye. On several
occasions five small, severe girls, armed with
assorted jugs, superintended operations.
Then the man would endeavor to assume the
aspect of an indulgent parent who amuses his
children, but as the children stood in a row on the
verandah—there was not room for them and their
father on the “lawn”—it was apparent to the
most cursory observer that the girls were not only
rigidly directing their father’s efforts, but were
displeased with them.
* • * *
No single blade of green responded to the man’s
arduous efforts, yet he continued to water-away,
week after week, with an unflagging heroism
which I began to regard as almost pathetic.
* * * •
This went on for nearly a year. Summer,
autumn and winter flitted. Spring dawned. He
increased, if possible, his exertions.
Nothing came !
One memorable day a new, small watering-can
reposed on the lawn. With its advent the worker
seemed to gain some confidence, possibly feeling
that the can was an outward and visible sign to
the observing world that he was no longer a mere
dilettante gardener, but had passed into the profes-
sional ranks. He retired, when gazed-at, less
frequently, though he still faintly blushed, and his
loose, pendulous lips worked uncannily. In the
autumn I went away oversea. The very last day
I was in Sydney a glance over the iron railings of
the villa assured me there was no sign of vegeta-
tion—not even a light mildew.
* » * *
Away many months, it was hot summer before
I returned to the old spot. The villa, man, and
watering-can were still there. The man looked
discouraged. In a corner of his patch was stuck
something which looked like a plant. I concluded
he had relinquished his great design of an entire
lawn and would compound—part grass, part
flowers. He saw me, started, but did not vanish.
Indeed, the shade of a wan and deprecatory smile
hovered around his voluminous lips. It was as if
he had said, “Do not judge me too harshly; I
have done my best.” Once or twice, as the days
wore on, I remarked a pipe in his mouth. No
smoke ever issued from it. It was never even
loaded. Once, the pipe in evidence, a voice—a
most terrible female voice—called from the villa’s
interior: “Horatio!” In a flash he pocketed
the empty pipe and hurried indoors, with that
hopeless expression of countenance which some of
us, the saints allege, will carry about through all
eternity.
* * * *
One torrid autumn morning there was, for a
wonder, no one in the villa garden. A strange
oppressive silence brooded. I looked up at the
windows. All the blinds were close drawn. That
had never happened before. I stopped, and looked
over the iron railings, which somehow reminded
me of those surrounding a genteel grave. A
young lady, of acid and angular visage, was also
observing the villa-front, while she arranged a
rusty black stocking that might have concealed
a bony knee, had it been darned.
“ Bloke’s dead ! ” she remarked, with a jerk of
her chin upwards.
“ Dead ? ” I ejaculated.
“ Killed ’isself by ’ard graft, they sy.”
My eye fell upon the lawn. It was terribly dry
and sandy—but—yes—as I live—in the centre,
about the size of one soup-plate, was a little patch
of green grass. And, in the corner, a marigold
bloomed !
And the toiler was dead !
But his life-work was accomplished—“ Laborare
est orare! ”
